POIROT EXPLAINS\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER I. I GO TO STYLES\r\n\r\nThe intense interest aroused in the public by what was known at the\r\ntime as \xe2\x80\x9cThe Styles Case\xe2\x80\x9d has now somewhat subsided. Nevertheless, in\r\nview of the world-wide notoriety which attended it, I have been asked,\r\nboth by my friend Poirot and the family themselves, to write an account\r\nof the whole story. This, we trust, will effectually silence the\r\nsensational rumours which still persist.\r\n\r\nI will therefore briefly set down the circumstances which led to my\r\nbeing connected with the affair.\r\n\r\nI had been invalided home from the Front; and, after spending some\r\nmonths in a rather depressing Convalescent Home, was given a month\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsick leave. Having no near relations or friends, I was trying to make\r\nup my mind what to do, when I ran across John Cavendish. I had seen\r\nvery little of him for some years. Indeed, I had never known him\r\nparticularly well. He was a good fifteen years my senior, for one\r\nthing, though he hardly looked his forty-five years. As a boy, though,\r\nI had often stayed at Styles, his mother\xe2\x80\x99s place in Essex.\r\n\r\nWe had a good yarn about old times, and it ended in his inviting me\r\ndown to Styles to spend my leave there.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe mater will be delighted to see you again\xe2\x80\x94after all those years,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother keeps well?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. I suppose you know that she has married again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI am afraid I showed my surprise rather plainly. Mrs. Cavendish, who\r\nhad married John\xe2\x80\x99s father when he was a widower with two sons, had been\r\na handsome woman of middle-age as I remembered her. She certainly could\r\nnot be a day less than seventy now. I recalled her as an energetic,\r\nautocratic personality, somewhat inclined to charitable and social\r\nnotoriety, with a fondness for opening bazaars and playing the Lady\r\nBountiful. She was a most generous woman, and possessed a considerable\r\nfortune of her own.\r\n\r\nTheir country-place, Styles Court, had been purchased by Mr. Cavendish\r\nearly in their married life. He had been completely under his wife\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nascendancy, so much so that, on dying, he left the place to her for her\r\nlifetime, as well as the larger part of his income; an arrangement that\r\nwas distinctly unfair to his two sons. Their step-mother, however, had\r\nalways been most generous to them; indeed, they were so young at the\r\ntime of their father\xe2\x80\x99s remarriage that they always thought of her as\r\ntheir own mother.\r\n\r\nLawrence, the younger, had been a delicate youth. He had qualified as a\r\ndoctor but early relinquished the profession of medicine, and lived at\r\nhome while pursuing literary ambitions; though his verses never had any\r\nmarked success.\r\n\r\nJohn practised for some time as a barrister, but had finally settled\r\ndown to the more congenial life of a country squire. He had married two\r\nyears ago, and had taken his wife to live at Styles, though I\r\nentertained a shrewd suspicion that he would have preferred his mother\r\nto increase his allowance, which would have enabled him to have a home\r\nof his own. Mrs. Cavendish, however, was a lady who liked to make her\r\nown plans, and expected other people to fall in with them, and in this\r\ncase she certainly had the whip hand, namely: the purse strings.\r\n\r\nJohn noticed my surprise at the news of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s remarriage and\r\nsmiled rather ruefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRotten little bounder too!\xe2\x80\x9d he said savagely. \xe2\x80\x9cI can tell you,\r\nHastings, it\xe2\x80\x99s making life jolly difficult for us. As for Evie\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nremember Evie?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I suppose she was after your time. She\xe2\x80\x99s the mater\xe2\x80\x99s factotum,\r\ncompanion, Jack of all trades! A great sport\xe2\x80\x94old Evie! Not precisely\r\nyoung and beautiful, but as game as they make them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were going to say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, this fellow! He turned up from nowhere, on the pretext of being a\r\nsecond cousin or something of Evie\xe2\x80\x99s, though she didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem\r\nparticularly keen to acknowledge the relationship. The fellow is an\r\nabsolute outsider, anyone can see that. He\xe2\x80\x99s got a great black beard,\r\nand wears patent leather boots in all weathers! But the mater cottoned\r\nto him at once, took him on as secretary\xe2\x80\x94you know how she\xe2\x80\x99s always\r\nrunning a hundred societies?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of course the war has turned the hundreds into thousands. No\r\ndoubt the fellow was very useful to her. But you could have knocked us\r\nall down with a feather when, three months ago, she suddenly announced\r\nthat she and Alfred were engaged! The fellow must be at least twenty\r\nyears younger than she is! It\xe2\x80\x99s simply bare-faced fortune hunting; but\r\nthere you are\xe2\x80\x94she is her own mistress, and she\xe2\x80\x99s married him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt must be a difficult situation for you all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDifficult! It\xe2\x80\x99s damnable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThus it came about that, three days later, I descended from the train\r\nat Styles St. Mary, an absurd little station, with no apparent reason\r\nfor existence, perched up in the midst of green fields and country\r\nlanes. John Cavendish was waiting on the platform, and piloted me out\r\nto the car.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGot a drop or two of petrol still, you see,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cMainly\r\nowing to the mater\xe2\x80\x99s activities.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe village of Styles St. Mary was situated about two miles from the\r\nlittle station, and Styles Court lay a mile the other side of it. It\r\nwas a still, warm day in early July. As one looked out over the flat\r\nEssex country, lying so green and peaceful under the afternoon sun, it\r\nseemed almost impossible to believe that, not so very far away, a great\r\nwar was running its appointed course. I felt I had suddenly strayed\r\ninto another world. As we turned in at the lodge gates, John said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you\xe2\x80\x99ll find it very quiet down here, Hastings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, that\xe2\x80\x99s just what I want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s pleasant enough if you want to lead the idle life. I drill\r\nwith the volunteers twice a week, and lend a hand at the farms. My wife\r\nworks regularly \xe2\x80\x98on the land\xe2\x80\x99. She is up at five every morning to milk,\r\nand keeps at it steadily until lunchtime. It\xe2\x80\x99s a jolly good life taking\r\nit all round\xe2\x80\x94if it weren\xe2\x80\x99t for that fellow Alfred Inglethorp!\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nchecked the car suddenly, and glanced at his watch. \xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ntime to pick up Cynthia. No, she\xe2\x80\x99ll have started from the hospital by\r\nnow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCynthia! That\xe2\x80\x99s not your wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Cynthia is a prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9e of my mother\xe2\x80\x99s, the daughter of an old\r\nschoolfellow of hers, who married a rascally solicitor. He came a\r\ncropper, and the girl was left an orphan and penniless. My mother came\r\nto the rescue, and Cynthia has been with us nearly two years now. She\r\nworks in the Red Cross Hospital at Tadminster, seven miles away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs he spoke the last words, we drew up in front of the fine old house.\r\nA lady in a stout tweed skirt, who was bending over a flower bed,\r\nstraightened herself at our approach.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo, Evie, here\xe2\x80\x99s our wounded hero! Mr. Hastings\xe2\x80\x94Miss Howard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard shook hands with a hearty, almost painful, grip. I had an\r\nimpression of very blue eyes in a sunburnt face. She was a\r\npleasant-looking woman of about forty, with a deep voice, almost manly\r\nin its stentorian tones, and had a large sensible square body, with\r\nfeet to match\xe2\x80\x94these last encased in good thick boots. Her conversation,\r\nI soon found, was couched in the telegraphic style.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWeeds grow like house afire. Can\xe2\x80\x99t keep even with \xe2\x80\x99em. Shall press you\r\nin. Better be careful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I shall be only too delighted to make myself useful,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nresponded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say it. Never does. Wish you hadn\xe2\x80\x99t later.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a cynic, Evie,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\xe2\x80\x99s tea to-day\xe2\x80\x94inside\r\nor out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut. Too fine a day to be cooped up in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome on then, you\xe2\x80\x99ve done enough gardening for to-day. \xe2\x80\x98The labourer\r\nis worthy of his hire\xe2\x80\x99, you know. Come and be refreshed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Howard, drawing off her gardening gloves, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m\r\ninclined to agree with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe led the way round the house to where tea was spread under the shade\r\nof a large sycamore.\r\n\r\nA figure rose from one of the basket chairs, and came a few steps to\r\nmeet us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy wife, Hastings,\xe2\x80\x9d said John.\r\n\r\nI shall never forget my first sight of Mary Cavendish. Her tall,\r\nslender form, outlined against the bright light; the vivid sense of\r\nslumbering fire that seemed to find expression only in those wonderful\r\ntawny eyes of hers, remarkable eyes, different from any other woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthat I have ever known; the intense power of stillness she possessed,\r\nwhich nevertheless conveyed the impression of a wild untamed spirit in\r\nan exquisitely civilised body\xe2\x80\x94all these things are burnt into my\r\nmemory. I shall never forget them.\r\n\r\nShe greeted me with a few words of pleasant welcome in a low clear\r\nvoice, and I sank into a basket chair feeling distinctly glad that I\r\nhad accepted John\xe2\x80\x99s invitation. Mrs. Cavendish gave me some tea, and\r\nher few quiet remarks heightened my first impression of her as a\r\nthoroughly fascinating woman. An appreciative listener is always\r\nstimulating, and I described, in a humorous manner, certain incidents\r\nof my Convalescent Home, in a way which, I flatter myself, greatly\r\namused my hostess. John, of course, good fellow though he is, could\r\nhardly be called a brilliant conversationalist.\r\n\r\nAt that moment a well remembered voice floated through the open French\r\nwindow near at hand:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you\xe2\x80\x99ll write to the Princess after tea, Alfred? I\xe2\x80\x99ll write to\r\nLady Tadminster for the second day, myself. Or shall we wait until we\r\nhear from the Princess? In case of a refusal, Lady Tadminster might\r\nopen it the first day, and Mrs. Crosbie the second. Then there\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\nDuchess\xe2\x80\x94about the school f\xc3\xaate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was the murmur of a man\xe2\x80\x99s voice, and then Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s rose\r\nin reply:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, certainly. After tea will do quite well. You are so thoughtful,\r\nAlfred dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe French window swung open a little wider, and a handsome\r\nwhite-haired old lady, with a somewhat masterful cast of features,\r\nstepped out of it on to the lawn. A man followed her, a suggestion of\r\ndeference in his manner.\r\n\r\nMrs. Inglethorp greeted me with effusion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t too delightful to see you again, Mr. Hastings, after\r\nall these years. Alfred, darling, Mr. Hastings\xe2\x80\x94my husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked with some curiosity at \xe2\x80\x9cAlfred darling\xe2\x80\x9d. He certainly struck a\r\nrather alien note. I did not wonder at John objecting to his beard. It\r\nwas one of the longest and blackest I have ever seen. He wore\r\ngold-rimmed pince-nez, and had a curious impassivity of feature. It\r\nstruck me that he might look natural on a stage, but was strangely out\r\nof place in real life. His voice was rather deep and unctuous. He\r\nplaced a wooden hand in mine and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a pleasure, Mr. Hastings.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, turning to his wife: \xe2\x80\x9cEmily\r\ndearest, I think that cushion is a little damp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe beamed fondly on him, as he substituted another with every\r\ndemonstration of the tenderest care. Strange infatuation of an\r\notherwise sensible woman!\r\n\r\nWith the presence of Mr. Inglethorp, a sense of constraint and veiled\r\nhostility seemed to settle down upon the company. Miss Howard, in\r\nparticular, took no pains to conceal her feelings. Mrs. Inglethorp,\r\nhowever, seemed to notice nothing unusual. Her volubility, which I\r\nremembered of old, had lost nothing in the intervening years, and she\r\npoured out a steady flood of conversation, mainly on the subject of the\r\nforthcoming bazaar which she was organizing and which was to take place\r\nshortly. Occasionally she referred to her husband over a question of\r\ndays or dates. His watchful and attentive manner never varied. From the\r\nvery first I took a firm and rooted dislike to him, and I flatter\r\nmyself that my first judgments are usually fairly shrewd.\r\n\r\nPresently Mrs. Inglethorp turned to give some instructions about\r\nletters to Evelyn Howard, and her husband addressed me in his\r\npainstaking voice:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs soldiering your regular profession, Mr. Hastings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, before the war I was in Lloyd\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you will return there after it is over?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps. Either that or a fresh start altogether.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary Cavendish leant forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat would you really choose as a profession, if you could just\r\nconsult your inclination?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that depends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo secret hobby?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cTell me\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re drawn to something?\r\nEveryone is\xe2\x80\x94usually something absurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll laugh at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ve always had a secret hankering to be a detective!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe real thing\xe2\x80\x94Scotland Yard? Or Sherlock Holmes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Sherlock Holmes by all means. But really, seriously, I am awfully\r\ndrawn to it. I came across a man in Belgium once, a very famous\r\ndetective, and he quite inflamed me. He was a marvellous little fellow.\r\nHe used to say that all good detective work was a mere matter of\r\nmethod. My system is based on his\xe2\x80\x94though of course I have progressed\r\nrather further. He was a funny little man, a great dandy, but\r\nwonderfully clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike a good detective story myself,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Miss Howard. \xe2\x80\x9cLots of\r\nnonsense written, though. Criminal discovered in last chapter. Everyone\r\ndumbfounded. Real crime\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99d know at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere have been a great number of undiscovered crimes,\xe2\x80\x9d I argued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t mean the police, but the people that are right in it. The\r\nfamily. You couldn\xe2\x80\x99t really hoodwink them. They\xe2\x80\x99d know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, much amused, \xe2\x80\x9cyou think that if you were mixed up in a\r\ncrime, say a murder, you\xe2\x80\x99d be able to spot the murderer right off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course I should. Mightn\xe2\x80\x99t be able to prove it to a pack of lawyers.\r\nBut I\xe2\x80\x99m certain I\xe2\x80\x99d know. I\xe2\x80\x99d feel it in my fingertips if he came near\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might be a \xe2\x80\x98she\xe2\x80\x99,\xe2\x80\x9d I suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight. But murder\xe2\x80\x99s a violent crime. Associate it more with a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in a case of poisoning.\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s clear voice startled me.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDr. Bauerstein was saying yesterday that, owing to the general\r\nignorance of the more uncommon poisons among the medical profession,\r\nthere were probably countless cases of poisoning quite unsuspected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Mary, what a gruesome conversation!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Inglethorp. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nmakes me feel as if a goose were walking over my grave. Oh, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nCynthia!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA young girl in V.A.D. uniform ran lightly across the lawn.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, Cynthia, you are late to-day. This is Mr. Hastings\xe2\x80\x94Miss Murdoch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCynthia Murdoch was a fresh-looking young creature, full of life and\r\nvigour. She tossed off her little V.A.D. cap, and I admired the great\r\nloose waves of her auburn hair, and the smallness and whiteness of the\r\nhand she held out to claim her tea. With dark eyes and eyelashes she\r\nwould have been a beauty.\r\n\r\nShe flung herself down on the ground beside John, and as I handed her a\r\nplate of sandwiches she smiled up at me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSit down here on the grass, do. It\xe2\x80\x99s ever so much nicer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI dropped down obediently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou work at Tadminster, don\xe2\x80\x99t you, Miss Murdoch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor my sins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo they bully you, then?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see them!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Cynthia with dignity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have got a cousin who is nursing,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd she is terrified\r\nof \xe2\x80\x98Sisters\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder. Sisters _are_, you know, Mr. Hastings. They simp-ly\r\n_are_! You\xe2\x80\x99ve no idea! But I\xe2\x80\x99m not a nurse, thank heaven, I work in the\r\ndispensary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow many people do you poison?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, smiling.\r\n\r\nCynthia smiled too.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hundreds!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCynthia,\xe2\x80\x9d called Mrs. Inglethorp, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think you could write a few\r\nnotes for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, Aunt Emily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe jumped up promptly, and something in her manner reminded me that\r\nher position was a dependent one, and that Mrs. Inglethorp, kind as she\r\nmight be in the main, did not allow her to forget it.\r\n\r\nMy hostess turned to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn will show you your room. Supper is at half-past seven. We have\r\ngiven up late dinner for some time now. Lady Tadminster, our Member\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife\xe2\x80\x94she was the late Lord Abbotsbury\xe2\x80\x99s daughter\xe2\x80\x94does the same. She\r\nagrees with me that one must set an example of economy. We are quite a\r\nwar household; nothing is wasted here\xe2\x80\x94every scrap of waste paper, even,\r\nis saved and sent away in sacks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI expressed my appreciation, and John took me into the house and up the\r\nbroad staircase, which forked right and left half-way to different\r\nwings of the building. My room was in the left wing, and looked out\r\nover the park.\r\n\r\nJohn left me, and a few minutes later I saw him from my window walking\r\nslowly across the grass arm in arm with Cynthia Murdoch. I heard Mrs.\r\nInglethorp call \xe2\x80\x9cCynthia\xe2\x80\x9d impatiently, and the girl started and ran\r\nback to the house. At the same moment, a man stepped out from the\r\nshadow of a tree and walked slowly in the same direction. He looked\r\nabout forty, very dark with a melancholy clean-shaven face. Some\r\nviolent emotion seemed to be mastering him. He looked up at my window\r\nas he passed, and I recognized him, though he had changed much in the\r\nfifteen years that had elapsed since we last met. It was John\xe2\x80\x99s younger\r\nbrother, Lawrence Cavendish. I wondered what it was that had brought\r\nthat singular expression to his face.\r\n\r\nThen I dismissed him from my mind, and returned to the contemplation of\r\nmy own affairs.\r\n\r\nThe evening passed pleasantly enough; and I dreamed that night of that\r\nenigmatical woman, Mary Cavendish.\r\n\r\nThe next morning dawned bright and sunny, and I was full of the\r\nanticipation of a delightful visit.\r\n\r\nI did not see Mrs. Cavendish until lunch-time, when she volunteered to\r\ntake me for a walk, and we spent a charming afternoon roaming in the\r\nwoods, returning to the house about five.\r\n\r\nAs we entered the large hall, John beckoned us both into the\r\nsmoking-room. I saw at once by his face that something disturbing had\r\noccurred. We followed him in, and he shut the door after us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, Mary, there\xe2\x80\x99s the deuce of a mess. Evie\xe2\x80\x99s had a row with\r\nAlfred Inglethorp, and she\xe2\x80\x99s off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvie? Off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn nodded gloomily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; you see she went to the mater, and\xe2\x80\x94Oh,\xe2\x80\x94here\xe2\x80\x99s Evie herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard entered. Her lips were set grimly together, and she carried\r\na small suit-case. She looked excited and determined, and slightly on\r\nthe defensive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d she burst out, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve spoken my mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Evelyn,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Cavendish, \xe2\x80\x9cthis can\xe2\x80\x99t be true!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard nodded grimly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue enough! Afraid I said some things to Emily she won\xe2\x80\x99t forget or\r\nforgive in a hurry. Don\xe2\x80\x99t mind if they\xe2\x80\x99ve only sunk in a bit. Probably\r\nwater off a duck\xe2\x80\x99s back, though. I said right out: \xe2\x80\x98You\xe2\x80\x99re an old\r\nwoman, Emily, and there\xe2\x80\x99s no fool like an old fool. The man\xe2\x80\x99s twenty\r\nyears younger than you, and don\xe2\x80\x99t you fool yourself as to what he\r\nmarried you for. Money! Well, don\xe2\x80\x99t let him have too much of it. Farmer\r\nRaikes has got a very pretty young wife. Just ask your Alfred how much\r\ntime he spends over there.\xe2\x80\x99 She was very angry. Natural! I went on,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99m going to warn you, whether you like it or not. That man would as\r\nsoon murder you in your bed as look at you. He\xe2\x80\x99s a bad lot. You can say\r\nwhat you like to me, but remember what I\xe2\x80\x99ve told you. He\xe2\x80\x99s a bad lot!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did she say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard made an extremely expressive grimace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Darling Alfred\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98dearest Alfred\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98wicked calumnies\xe2\x80\x99 \xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98wicked\r\nlies\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98wicked woman\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94to accuse her \xe2\x80\x98dear husband!\xe2\x80\x99 The sooner I left\r\nher house the better. So I\xe2\x80\x99m off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut not now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis minute!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment we sat and stared at her. Finally John Cavendish, finding\r\nhis persuasions of no avail, went off to look up the trains. His wife\r\nfollowed him, murmuring something about persuading Mrs. Inglethorp to\r\nthink better of it.\r\n\r\nAs she left the room, Miss Howard\xe2\x80\x99s face changed. She leant towards me\r\neagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Hastings, you\xe2\x80\x99re honest. I can trust you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was a little startled. She laid her hand on my arm, and sank her\r\nvoice to a whisper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook after her, Mr. Hastings. My poor Emily. They\xe2\x80\x99re a lot of\r\nsharks\xe2\x80\x94all of them. Oh, I know what I\xe2\x80\x99m talking about. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t one\r\nof them that\xe2\x80\x99s not hard up and trying to get money out of her. I\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\nprotected her as much as I could. Now I\xe2\x80\x99m out of the way, they\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\nimpose upon her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, Miss Howard,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll do everything I can, but I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nsure you\xe2\x80\x99re excited and overwrought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe interrupted me by slowly shaking her forefinger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYoung man, trust me. I\xe2\x80\x99ve lived in the world rather longer than you\r\nhave. All I ask you is to keep your eyes open. You\xe2\x80\x99ll see what I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe throb of the motor came through the open window, and Miss Howard\r\nrose and moved to the door. John\xe2\x80\x99s voice sounded outside. With her hand\r\non the handle, she turned her head over her shoulder, and beckoned to\r\nme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbove all, Mr. Hastings, watch that devil\xe2\x80\x94her husband!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no time for more. Miss Howard was swallowed up in an eager\r\nchorus of protests and good-byes. The Inglethorps did not appear.\r\n\r\nAs the motor drove away, Mrs. Cavendish suddenly detached herself from\r\nthe group, and moved across the drive to the lawn to meet a tall\r\nbearded man who had been evidently making for the house. The colour\r\nrose in her cheeks as she held out her hand to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is that?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked sharply, for instinctively I distrusted the man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s Dr. Bauerstein,\xe2\x80\x9d said John shortly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is Dr. Bauerstein?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s staying in the village doing a rest cure, after a bad nervous\r\nbreakdown. He\xe2\x80\x99s a London specialist; a very clever man\xe2\x80\x94one of the\r\ngreatest living experts on poisons, I believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he\xe2\x80\x99s a great friend of Mary\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d put in Cynthia, the irrepressible.\r\n\r\nJohn Cavendish frowned and changed the subject.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome for a stroll, Hastings. This has been a most rotten business. She\r\nalways had a rough tongue, but there is no stauncher friend in England\r\nthan Evelyn Howard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took the path through the plantation, and we walked down to the\r\nvillage through the woods which bordered one side of the estate.\r\n\r\nAs we passed through one of the gates on our way home again, a pretty\r\nyoung woman of gipsy type coming in the opposite direction bowed and\r\nsmiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a pretty girl,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked appreciatively.\r\n\r\nJohn\xe2\x80\x99s face hardened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Mrs. Raikes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one that Miss Howard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, with rather unnecessary abruptness.\r\n\r\nI thought of the white-haired old lady in the big house, and that vivid\r\nwicked little face that had just smiled into ours, and a vague chill of\r\nforeboding crept over me. I brushed it aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStyles is really a glorious old place,\xe2\x80\x9d I said to John.\r\n\r\nHe nodded rather gloomily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s a fine property. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be mine some day\xe2\x80\x94should be mine now\r\nby rights, if my father had only made a decent will. And then I\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be so damned hard up as I am now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHard up, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Hastings, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mind telling you that I\xe2\x80\x99m at my wits\xe2\x80\x99 end\r\nfor money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t your brother help you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLawrence? He\xe2\x80\x99s gone through every penny he ever had, publishing rotten\r\nverses in fancy bindings. No, we\xe2\x80\x99re an impecunious lot. My mother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nalways been awfully good to us, I must say. That is, up to now. Since\r\nher marriage, of course\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off, frowning.\r\n\r\nFor the first time I felt that, with Evelyn Howard, something\r\nindefinable had gone from the atmosphere. Her presence had spelt\r\nsecurity. Now that security was removed\xe2\x80\x94and the air seemed rife with\r\nsuspicion. The sinister face of Dr. Bauerstein recurred to me\r\nunpleasantly. A vague suspicion of everyone and everything filled my\r\nmind. Just for a moment I had a premonition of approaching evil.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II. THE 16TH AND 17TH OF JULY\r\n\r\nI had arrived at Styles on the 5th of July. I come now to the events of\r\nthe 16th and 17th of that month. For the convenience of the reader I\r\nwill recapitulate the incidents of those days in as exact a manner as\r\npossible. They were elicited subsequently at the trial by a process of\r\nlong and tedious cross-examinations.\r\n\r\nI received a letter from Evelyn Howard a couple of days after her\r\ndeparture, telling me she was working as a nurse at the big hospital in\r\nMiddlingham, a manufacturing town some fifteen miles away, and begging\r\nme to let her know if Mrs. Inglethorp should show any wish to be\r\nreconciled.\r\n\r\nThe only fly in the ointment of my peaceful days was Mrs. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nextraordinary, and, for my part, unaccountable preference for the\r\nsociety of Dr. Bauerstein. What she saw in the man I cannot imagine,\r\nbut she was always asking him up to the house, and often went off for\r\nlong expeditions with him. I must confess that I was quite unable to\r\nsee his attraction.\r\n\r\nThe 16th of July fell on a Monday. It was a day of turmoil. The famous\r\nbazaar had taken place on Saturday, and an entertainment, in connection\r\nwith the same charity, at which Mrs. Inglethorp was to recite a War\r\npoem, was to be held that night. We were all busy during the morning\r\narranging and decorating the Hall in the village where it was to take\r\nplace. We had a late luncheon and spent the afternoon resting in the\r\ngarden. I noticed that John\xe2\x80\x99s manner was somewhat unusual. He seemed\r\nvery excited and restless.\r\n\r\nAfter tea, Mrs. Inglethorp went to lie down to rest before her efforts\r\nin the evening and I challenged Mary Cavendish to a single at tennis.\r\n\r\nAbout a quarter to seven, Mrs. Inglethorp called us that we should be\r\nlate as supper was early that night. We had rather a scramble to get\r\nready in time; and before the meal was over the motor was waiting at\r\nthe door.\r\n\r\nThe entertainment was a great success, Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s recitation\r\nreceiving tremendous applause. There were also some tableaux in which\r\nCynthia took part. She did not return with us, having been asked to a\r\nsupper party, and to remain the night with some friends who had been\r\nacting with her in the tableaux.\r\n\r\nThe following morning, Mrs. Inglethorp stayed in bed to breakfast, as\r\nshe was rather overtired; but she appeared in her briskest mood about\r\n12.30, and swept Lawrence and myself off to a luncheon party.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch a charming invitation from Mrs. Rolleston. Lady Tadminster\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsister, you know. The Rollestons came over with the Conqueror\xe2\x80\x94one of\r\nour oldest families.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary had excused herself on the plea of an engagement with Dr.\r\nBauerstein.\r\n\r\nWe had a pleasant luncheon, and as we drove away Lawrence suggested\r\nthat we should return by Tadminster, which was barely a mile out of our\r\nway, and pay a visit to Cynthia in her dispensary. Mrs. Inglethorp\r\nreplied that this was an excellent idea, but as she had several letters\r\nto write she would drop us there, and we could come back with Cynthia\r\nin the pony-trap.\r\n\r\nWe were detained under suspicion by the hospital porter, until Cynthia\r\nappeared to vouch for us, looking very cool and sweet in her long white\r\noverall. She took us up to her sanctum, and introduced us to her fellow\r\ndispenser, a rather awe-inspiring individual, whom Cynthia cheerily\r\naddressed as \xe2\x80\x9cNibs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a lot of bottles!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed, as my eye travelled round the\r\nsmall room. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you really know what\xe2\x80\x99s in them all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay something original,\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Cynthia. \xe2\x80\x9cEvery single person who\r\ncomes up here says that. We are really thinking of bestowing a prize on\r\nthe first individual who does _not_ say: \xe2\x80\x98What a lot of bottles!\xe2\x80\x99 And I\r\nknow the next thing you\xe2\x80\x99re going to say is: \xe2\x80\x98How many people have you\r\npoisoned?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI pleaded guilty with a laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you people only knew how fatally easy it is to poison someone by\r\nmistake, you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t joke about it. Come on, let\xe2\x80\x99s have tea. We\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\nall sorts of secret stories in that cupboard. No, Lawrence\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\npoison cupboard. The big cupboard\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had a very cheery tea, and assisted Cynthia to wash up afterwards.\r\nWe had just put away the last tea-spoon when a knock came at the door.\r\nThe countenances of Cynthia and Nibs were suddenly petrified into a\r\nstern and forbidding expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cynthia, in a sharp professional tone.\r\n\r\nA young and rather scared looking nurse appeared with a bottle which\r\nshe proffered to Nibs, who waved her towards Cynthia with the somewhat\r\nenigmatical remark:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_I_\xe2\x80\x99m not really here to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCynthia took the bottle and examined it with the severity of a judge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis should have been sent up this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSister is very sorry. She forgot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSister should read the rules outside the door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI gathered from the little nurse\xe2\x80\x99s expression that there was not the\r\nleast likelihood of her having the hardihood to retail this message to\r\nthe dreaded \xe2\x80\x9cSister\xe2\x80\x9d.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo now it can\xe2\x80\x99t be done until to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d finished Cynthia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think you could possibly let us have it to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cynthia graciously, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are very busy, but if we have time\r\nit shall be done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little nurse withdrew, and Cynthia promptly took a jar from the\r\nshelf, refilled the bottle, and placed it on the table outside the\r\ndoor.\r\n\r\nI laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiscipline must be maintained?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. Come out on our little balcony. You can see all the outside\r\nwards there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI followed Cynthia and her friend and they pointed out the different\r\nwards to me. Lawrence remained behind, but after a few moments Cynthia\r\ncalled to him over her shoulder to come and join us. Then she looked at\r\nher watch.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing more to do, Nibs?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right. Then we can lock up and go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had seen Lawrence in quite a different light that afternoon. Compared\r\nto John, he was an astoundingly difficult person to get to know. He was\r\nthe opposite of his brother in almost every respect, being unusually\r\nshy and reserved. Yet he had a certain charm of manner, and I fancied\r\nthat, if one really knew him well, one could have a deep affection for\r\nhim. I had always fancied that his manner to Cynthia was rather\r\nconstrained, and that she on her side was inclined to be shy of him.\r\nBut they were both gay enough this afternoon, and chatted together like\r\na couple of children.\r\n\r\nAs we drove through the village, I remembered that I wanted some\r\nstamps, so accordingly we pulled up at the post office.\r\n\r\nAs I came out again, I cannoned into a little man who was just\r\nentering. I drew aside and apologised, when suddenly, with a loud\r\nexclamation, he clasped me in his arms and kissed me warmly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon ami_ Hastings!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is indeed _mon ami_ Hastings!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed.\r\n\r\nI turned to the pony-trap.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a very pleasant meeting for me, Miss Cynthia. This is my old\r\nfriend, Monsieur Poirot, whom I have not seen for years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, we know Monsieur Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cynthia gaily. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I had no idea\r\nhe was a friend of yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cI know Mademoiselle Cynthia. It\r\nis by the charity of that good Mrs. Inglethorp that I am here.\xe2\x80\x9d Then,\r\nas I looked at him inquiringly: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, my friend, she had kindly\r\nextended hospitality to seven of my countrypeople who, alas, are\r\nrefugees from their native land. We Belgians will always remember her\r\nwith gratitude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot was an extraordinary looking little man. He was hardly more than\r\nfive feet, four inches, but carried himself with great dignity. His\r\nhead was exactly the shape of an egg, and he always perched it a little\r\non one side. His moustache was very stiff and military. The neatness of\r\nhis attire was almost incredible. I believe a speck of dust would have\r\ncaused him more pain than a bullet wound. Yet this quaint dandified\r\nlittle man who, I was sorry to see, now limped badly, had been in his\r\ntime one of the most celebrated members of the Belgian police. As a\r\ndetective, his _flair_ had been extraordinary, and he had achieved\r\ntriumphs by unravelling some of the most baffling cases of the day.\r\n\r\nHe pointed out to me the little house inhabited by him and his fellow\r\nBelgians, and I promised to go and see him at an early date. Then he\r\nraised his hat with a flourish to Cynthia, and we drove away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a dear little man,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cynthia. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d no idea you knew him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been entertaining a celebrity unawares,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied.\r\n\r\nAnd, for the rest of the way home, I recited to them the various\r\nexploits and triumphs of Hercule Poirot.\r\n\r\nWe arrived back in a very cheerful mood. As we entered the hall, Mrs.\r\nInglethorp came out of her boudoir. She looked flushed and upset.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it\xe2\x80\x99s you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there anything the matter, Aunt Emily?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Cynthia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Inglethorp sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat should there be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThen catching sight of Dorcas, the parlourmaid, going into the\r\ndining-room, she called to her to bring some stamps into the boudoir.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, m\xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d The old servant hesitated, then added diffidently: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nyou think, m\xe2\x80\x99m, you\xe2\x80\x99d better get to bed? You\xe2\x80\x99re looking very tired.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you\xe2\x80\x99re right, Dorcas\xe2\x80\x94yes\xe2\x80\x94no\xe2\x80\x94not now. I\xe2\x80\x99ve some letters I must\r\nfinish by post-time. Have you lighted the fire in my room as I told\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, m\xe2\x80\x99m.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99ll go to bed directly after supper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe went into the boudoir again, and Cynthia stared after her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGoodness gracious! I wonder what\xe2\x80\x99s up?\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Lawrence.\r\n\r\nHe did not seem to have heard her, for without a word he turned on his\r\nheel and went out of the house.\r\n\r\nI suggested a quick game of tennis before supper and, Cynthia agreeing,\r\nI ran upstairs to fetch my racquet.\r\n\r\nMrs. Cavendish was coming down the stairs. It may have been my fancy,\r\nbut she, too, was looking odd and disturbed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHad a good walk with Dr. Bauerstein?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, trying to appear as\r\nindifferent as I could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t go,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied abruptly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Mrs. Inglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the boudoir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer hand clenched itself on the banisters, then she seemed to nerve\r\nherself for some encounter, and went rapidly past me down the stairs\r\nacross the hall to the boudoir, the door of which she shut behind her.\r\n\r\nAs I ran out to the tennis court a few moments later, I had to pass the\r\nopen boudoir window, and was unable to help overhearing the following\r\nscrap of dialogue. Mary Cavendish was saying in the voice of a woman\r\ndesperately controlling herself:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you won\xe2\x80\x99t show it to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo which Mrs. Inglethorp replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Mary, it has nothing to do with that matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen show it to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell you it is not what you imagine. It does not concern you in the\r\nleast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo which Mary Cavendish replied, with a rising bitterness:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course, I might have known you would shield him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCynthia was waiting for me, and greeted me eagerly with:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say! There\xe2\x80\x99s been the most awful row! I\xe2\x80\x99ve got it all out of\r\nDorcas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat kind of a row?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetween Aunt Emily and _him_. I do hope she\xe2\x80\x99s found him out at last!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas Dorcas there, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course not. She \xe2\x80\x98happened to be near the door\xe2\x80\x99. It was a real old\r\nbust-up. I do wish I knew what it was all about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI thought of Mrs. Raikes\xe2\x80\x99s gipsy face, and Evelyn Howard\xe2\x80\x99s warnings,\r\nbut wisely decided to hold my peace, whilst Cynthia exhausted every\r\npossible hypothesis, and cheerfully hoped, \xe2\x80\x9cAunt Emily will send him\r\naway, and will never speak to him again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was anxious to get hold of John, but he was nowhere to be seen.\r\nEvidently something very momentous had occurred that afternoon. I tried\r\nto forget the few words I had overheard; but, do what I would, I could\r\nnot dismiss them altogether from my mind. What was Mary Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconcern in the matter?\r\n\r\nMr. Inglethorp was in the drawing-room when I came down to supper. His\r\nface was impassive as ever, and the strange unreality of the man struck\r\nme afresh.\r\n\r\nMrs. Inglethorp came down last. She still looked agitated, and during\r\nthe meal there was a somewhat constrained silence. Inglethorp was\r\nunusually quiet. As a rule, he surrounded his wife with little\r\nattentions, placing a cushion at her back, and altogether playing the\r\npart of the devoted husband. Immediately after supper, Mrs. Inglethorp\r\nretired to her boudoir again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSend my coffee in here, Mary,\xe2\x80\x9d she called. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve just five minutes to\r\ncatch the post.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCynthia and I went and sat by the open window in the drawing-room. Mary\r\nCavendish brought our coffee to us. She seemed excited.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you young people want lights, or do you enjoy the twilight?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you take Mrs. Inglethorp her coffee, Cynthia? I will pour\r\nit out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not trouble, Mary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Inglethorp. \xe2\x80\x9cI will take it to Emily.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\npoured it out, and went out of the room carrying it carefully.\r\n\r\nLawrence followed him, and Mrs. Cavendish sat down by us.\r\n\r\nWe three sat for some time in silence. It was a glorious night, hot and\r\nstill. Mrs. Cavendish fanned herself gently with a palm leaf.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s almost too hot,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall have a thunderstorm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlas, that these harmonious moments can never endure! My paradise was\r\nrudely shattered by the sound of a well known, and heartily disliked,\r\nvoice in the hall.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDr. Bauerstein!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Cynthia. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a funny time to come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI glanced jealously at Mary Cavendish, but she seemed quite\r\nundisturbed, the delicate pallor of her cheeks did not vary.\r\n\r\nIn a few moments, Alfred Inglethorp had ushered the doctor in, the\r\nlatter laughing, and protesting that he was in no fit state for a\r\ndrawing-room. In truth, he presented a sorry spectacle, being literally\r\nplastered with mud.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you been doing, doctor?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Mrs. Cavendish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must make my apologies,\xe2\x80\x9d said the doctor. \xe2\x80\x9cI did not really mean to\r\ncome in, but Mr. Inglethorp insisted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Bauerstein, you are in a plight,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, strolling in from\r\nthe hall. \xe2\x80\x9cHave some coffee, and tell us what you have been up to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, I will.\xe2\x80\x9d He laughed rather ruefully, as he described how he\r\nhad discovered a very rare species of fern in an inaccessible place,\r\nand in his efforts to obtain it had lost his footing, and slipped\r\nignominiously into a neighbouring pond.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sun soon dried me off,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m afraid my appearance is\r\nvery disreputable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this juncture, Mrs. Inglethorp called to Cynthia from the hall, and\r\nthe girl ran out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust carry up my despatch-case, will you, dear? I\xe2\x80\x99m going to bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door into the hall was a wide one. I had risen when Cynthia did,\r\nJohn was close by me. There were therefore three witnesses who could\r\nswear that Mrs. Inglethorp was carrying her coffee, as yet untasted, in\r\nher hand.\r\n\r\nMy evening was utterly and entirely spoilt by the presence of Dr.\r\nBauerstein. It seemed to me the man would never go. He rose at last,\r\nhowever, and I breathed a sigh of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll walk down to the village with you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Inglethorp. \xe2\x80\x9cI must\r\nsee our agent over those estate accounts.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to John. \xe2\x80\x9cNo one\r\nneed sit up. I will take the latch-key.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III. THE NIGHT OF THE TRAGEDY\r\n\r\nTo make this part of my story clear, I append the following plan of the\r\nfirst floor of Styles. The servants\xe2\x80\x99 rooms are reached through the door\r\nB. They have no communication with the right wing, where the\r\nInglethorps\xe2\x80\x99 rooms were situated.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n       01\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nIt seemed to be the middle of the night when I was awakened by Lawrence\r\nCavendish. He had a candle in his hand, and the agitation of his face\r\ntold me at once that something was seriously wrong.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, sitting up in bed, and trying to collect\r\nmy scattered thoughts.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are afraid my mother is very ill. She seems to be having some kind\r\nof fit. Unfortunately she has locked herself in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll come at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI sprang out of bed; and, pulling on a dressing-gown, followed Lawrence\r\nalong the passage and the gallery to the right wing of the house.\r\n\r\nJohn Cavendish joined us, and one or two of the servants were standing\r\nround in a state of awe-stricken excitement. Lawrence turned to his\r\nbrother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think we had better do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNever, I thought, had his indecision of character been more apparent.\r\n\r\nJohn rattled the handle of Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s door violently, but with\r\nno effect. It was obviously locked or bolted on the inside. The whole\r\nhousehold was aroused by now. The most alarming sounds were audible\r\nfrom the interior of the room. Clearly something must be done.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry going through Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Dorcas. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, the\r\npoor mistress!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly I realized that Alfred Inglethorp was not with us\xe2\x80\x94that he\r\nalone had given no sign of his presence. John opened the door of his\r\nroom. It was pitch dark, but Lawrence was following with the candle,\r\nand by its feeble light we saw that the bed had not been slept in, and\r\nthat there was no sign of the room having been occupied.\r\n\r\nWe went straight to the connecting door. That, too, was locked or\r\nbolted on the inside. What was to be done?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, dear, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Dorcas, wringing her hands, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat ever shall we\r\ndo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe must try and break the door in, I suppose. It\xe2\x80\x99ll be a tough job,\r\nthough. Here, let one of the maids go down and wake Baily and tell him\r\nto go for Dr. Wilkins at once. Now then, we\xe2\x80\x99ll have a try at the door.\r\nHalf a moment, though, isn\xe2\x80\x99t there a door into Miss Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s rooms?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, but that\xe2\x80\x99s always bolted. It\xe2\x80\x99s never been undone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we might just see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe ran rapidly down the corridor to Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room. Mary Cavendish was\r\nthere, shaking the girl\xe2\x80\x94who must have been an unusually sound\r\nsleeper\xe2\x80\x94and trying to wake her.\r\n\r\nIn a moment or two he was back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo good. That\xe2\x80\x99s bolted too. We must break in the door. I think this\r\none is a shade less solid than the one in the passage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe strained and heaved together. The framework of the door was solid,\r\nand for a long time it resisted our efforts, but at last we felt it\r\ngive beneath our weight, and finally, with a resounding crash, it was\r\nburst open.\r\n\r\nWe stumbled in together, Lawrence still holding his candle. Mrs.\r\nInglethorp was lying on the bed, her whole form agitated by violent\r\nconvulsions, in one of which she must have overturned the table beside\r\nher. As we entered, however, her limbs relaxed, and she fell back upon\r\nthe pillows.\r\n\r\nJohn strode across the room, and lit the gas. Turning to Annie, one of\r\nthe housemaids, he sent her downstairs to the dining-room for brandy.\r\nThen he went across to his mother whilst I unbolted the door that gave\r\non the corridor.\r\n\r\nI turned to Lawrence, to suggest that I had better leave them now that\r\nthere was no further need of my services, but the words were frozen on\r\nmy lips. Never have I seen such a ghastly look on any man\xe2\x80\x99s face. He\r\nwas white as chalk, the candle he held in his shaking hand was\r\nsputtering onto the carpet, and his eyes, petrified with terror, or\r\nsome such kindred emotion, stared fixedly over my head at a point on\r\nthe further wall. It was as though he had seen something that turned\r\nhim to stone. I instinctively followed the direction of his eyes, but I\r\ncould see nothing unusual. The still feebly flickering ashes in the\r\ngrate, and the row of prim ornaments on the mantelpiece, were surely\r\nharmless enough.\r\n\r\nThe violence of Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s attack seemed to be passing. She was\r\nable to speak in short gasps.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter now\xe2\x80\x94very sudden\xe2\x80\x94stupid of me\xe2\x80\x94to lock myself in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA shadow fell on the bed and, looking up, I saw Mary Cavendish standing\r\nnear the door with her arm around Cynthia. She seemed to be supporting\r\nthe girl, who looked utterly dazed and unlike herself. Her face was\r\nheavily flushed, and she yawned repeatedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor Cynthia is quite frightened,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Cavendish in a low clear\r\nvoice. She herself, I noticed, was dressed in her white land smock.\r\nThen it must be later than I thought. I saw that a faint streak of\r\ndaylight was showing through the curtains of the windows, and that the\r\nclock on the mantelpiece pointed to close upon five o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\nA strangled cry from the bed startled me. A fresh access of pain seized\r\nthe unfortunate old lady. The convulsions were of a violence terrible\r\nto behold. Everything was confusion. We thronged round her, powerless\r\nto help or alleviate. A final convulsion lifted her from the bed, until\r\nshe appeared to rest upon her head and her heels, with her body arched\r\nin an extraordinary manner. In vain Mary and John tried to administer\r\nmore brandy. The moments flew. Again the body arched itself in that\r\npeculiar fashion.\r\n\r\nAt that moment, Dr. Bauerstein pushed his way authoritatively into the\r\nroom. For one instant he stopped dead, staring at the figure on the\r\nbed, and, at the same instant, Mrs. Inglethorp cried out in a strangled\r\nvoice, her eyes fixed on the doctor:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlfred\xe2\x80\x94Alfred\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Then she fell back motionless on the pillows.\r\n\r\nWith a stride, the doctor reached the bed, and seizing her arms worked\r\nthem energetically, applying what I knew to be artificial respiration.\r\nHe issued a few short sharp orders to the servants. An imperious wave\r\nof his hand drove us all to the door. We watched him, fascinated,\r\nthough I think we all knew in our hearts that it was too late, and that\r\nnothing could be done now. I could see by the expression on his face\r\nthat he himself had little hope.\r\n\r\nFinally he abandoned his task, shaking his head gravely. At that\r\nmoment, we heard footsteps outside, and Dr. Wilkins, Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nown doctor, a portly, fussy little man, came bustling in.\r\n\r\nIn a few words Dr. Bauerstein explained how he had happened to be\r\npassing the lodge gates as the car came out, and had run up to the\r\nhouse as fast as he could, whilst the car went on to fetch Dr. Wilkins.\r\nWith a faint gesture of the hand, he indicated the figure on the bed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVe\xe2\x80\x94ry sad. Ve\xe2\x80\x94ry sad,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Dr. Wilkins. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor dear lady. Always\r\ndid far too much\xe2\x80\x94far too much\xe2\x80\x94against my advice. I warned her. Her\r\nheart was far from strong. \xe2\x80\x98Take it easy,\xe2\x80\x99 I said to her,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Take\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x94easy\xe2\x80\x99. But no\xe2\x80\x94her zeal for good works was too great. Nature\r\nrebelled. Na\xe2\x80\x94ture\xe2\x80\x94re\xe2\x80\x94belled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Bauerstein, I noticed, was watching the local doctor narrowly. He\r\nstill kept his eyes fixed on him as he spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe convulsions were of a peculiar violence, Dr. Wilkins. I am sorry\r\nyou were not here in time to witness them. They were quite\xe2\x80\x94tetanic in\r\ncharacter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Wilkins wisely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to speak to you in private,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Bauerstein. He\r\nturned to John. \xe2\x80\x9cYou do not object?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe all trooped out into the corridor, leaving the two doctors alone,\r\nand I heard the key turned in the lock behind us.\r\n\r\nWe went slowly down the stairs. I was violently excited. I have a\r\ncertain talent for deduction, and Dr. Bauerstein\xe2\x80\x99s manner had started a\r\nflock of wild surmises in my mind. Mary Cavendish laid her hand upon my\r\narm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? Why did Dr. Bauerstein seem so\xe2\x80\x94peculiar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what I think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen!\xe2\x80\x9d I looked round, the others were out of earshot. I lowered my\r\nvoice to a whisper. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe she has been poisoned! I\xe2\x80\x99m certain Dr.\r\nBauerstein suspects it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What?_\xe2\x80\x9d She shrank against the wall, the pupils of her eyes dilating\r\nwildly. Then, with a sudden cry that startled me, she cried out: \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\r\nno\xe2\x80\x94not that\xe2\x80\x94not that!\xe2\x80\x9d And breaking from me, fled up the stairs. I\r\nfollowed her, afraid that she was going to faint. I found her leaning\r\nagainst the bannisters, deadly pale. She waved me away impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no\xe2\x80\x94leave me. I\xe2\x80\x99d rather be alone. Let me just be quiet for a\r\nminute or two. Go down to the others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI obeyed her reluctantly. John and Lawrence were in the dining-room. I\r\njoined them. We were all silent, but I suppose I voiced the thoughts of\r\nus all when I at last broke it by saying:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is Mr. Inglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s not in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOur eyes met. Where _was_ Alfred Inglethorp? His absence was strange\r\nand inexplicable. I remembered Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s dying words. What lay\r\nbeneath them? What more could she have told us, if she had had time?\r\n\r\nAt last we heard the doctors descending the stairs. Dr. Wilkins was\r\nlooking important and excited, and trying to conceal an inward\r\nexultation under a manner of decorous calm. Dr. Bauerstein remained in\r\nthe background, his grave bearded face unchanged. Dr. Wilkins was the\r\nspokesman for the two. He addressed himself to John:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Cavendish, I should like your consent to a post-mortem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that necessary?\xe2\x80\x9d asked John gravely. A spasm of pain crossed his\r\nface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Bauerstein.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean by that\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat neither Dr. Wilkins nor myself could give a death certificate\r\nunder the circumstances.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn bent his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case, I have no alternative but to agree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Dr. Wilkins briskly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe propose that it should take\r\nplace to-morrow night\xe2\x80\x94or rather to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d And he glanced at the\r\ndaylight. \xe2\x80\x9cUnder the circumstances, I am afraid an inquest can hardly\r\nbe avoided\xe2\x80\x94these formalities are necessary, but I beg that you won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\ndistress yourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a pause, and then Dr. Bauerstein drew two keys from his\r\npocket, and handed them to John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are the keys of the two rooms. I have locked them and, in my\r\nopinion, they would be better kept locked for the present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe doctors then departed.\r\n\r\nI had been turning over an idea in my head, and I felt that the moment\r\nhad now come to broach it. Yet I was a little chary of doing so. John,\r\nI knew, had a horror of any kind of publicity, and was an easygoing\r\noptimist, who preferred never to meet trouble half-way. It might be\r\ndifficult to convince him of the soundness of my plan. Lawrence, on the\r\nother hand, being less conventional, and having more imagination, I\r\nfelt I might count upon as an ally. There was no doubt that the moment\r\nhad come for me to take the lead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am going to ask you something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remember my speaking of my friend Poirot? The Belgian who is here?\r\nHe has been a most famous detective.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want you to let me call him in\xe2\x80\x94to investigate this matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94now? Before the post-mortem?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, time is an advantage if\xe2\x80\x94if\xe2\x80\x94there has been foul play.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lawrence angrily. \xe2\x80\x9cIn my opinion the whole thing is a\r\nmare\xe2\x80\x99s nest of Bauerstein\xe2\x80\x99s! Wilkins hadn\xe2\x80\x99t an idea of such a thing,\r\nuntil Bauerstein put it into his head. But, like all specialists,\r\nBauerstein\xe2\x80\x99s got a bee in his bonnet. Poisons are his hobby, so of\r\ncourse he sees them everywhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI confess that I was surprised by Lawrence\xe2\x80\x99s attitude. He was so seldom\r\nvehement about anything.\r\n\r\nJohn hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t feel as you do, Lawrence,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m inclined to\r\ngive Hastings a free hand, though I should prefer to wait a bit. We\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want any unnecessary scandal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d I cried eagerly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou need have no fear of that. Poirot is\r\ndiscretion itself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then, have it your own way. I leave it in your hands.\r\nThough, if it is as we suspect, it seems a clear enough case. God\r\nforgive me if I am wronging him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at my watch. It was six o\xe2\x80\x99clock. I determined to lose no time.\r\n\r\nFive minutes\xe2\x80\x99 delay, however, I allowed myself. I spent it in\r\nransacking the library until I discovered a medical book which gave a\r\ndescription of strychnine poisoning.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV. POIROT INVESTIGATES\r\n\r\nThe house which the Belgians occupied in the village was quite close to\r\nthe park gates. One could save time by taking a narrow path through the\r\nlong grass, which cut off the detours of the winding drive. So I,\r\naccordingly, went that way. I had nearly reached the lodge, when my\r\nattention was arrested by the running figure of a man approaching me.\r\nIt was Mr. Inglethorp. Where had he been? How did he intend to explain\r\nhis absence?\r\n\r\nHe accosted me eagerly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy God! This is terrible! My poor wife! I have only just heard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere have you been?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDenby kept me late last night. It was one o\xe2\x80\x99clock before we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nfinished. Then I found that I\xe2\x80\x99d forgotten the latch-key after all. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t want to arouse the household, so Denby gave me a bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you hear the news?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilkins knocked Denby up to tell him. My poor Emily! She was so\r\nself-sacrificing\xe2\x80\x94such a noble character. She over-taxed her strength.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA wave of revulsion swept over me. What a consummate hypocrite the man\r\nwas!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must hurry on,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, thankful that he did not ask me whither I\r\nwas bound.\r\n\r\nIn a few minutes I was knocking at the door of Leastways Cottage.\r\n\r\nGetting no answer, I repeated my summons impatiently. A window above me\r\nwas cautiously opened, and Poirot himself looked out.\r\n\r\nHe gave an exclamation of surprise at seeing me. In a few brief words,\r\nI explained the tragedy that had occurred, and that I wanted his help.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait, my friend, I will let you in, and you shall recount to me the\r\naffair whilst I dress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a few moments he had unbarred the door, and I followed him up to his\r\nroom. There he installed me in a chair, and I related the whole story,\r\nkeeping back nothing, and omitting no circumstance, however\r\ninsignificant, whilst he himself made a careful and deliberate toilet.\r\n\r\nI told him of my awakening, of Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s dying words, of her\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x99s absence, of the quarrel the day before, of the scrap of\r\nconversation between Mary and her mother-in-law that I had overheard,\r\nof the former quarrel between Mrs. Inglethorp and Evelyn Howard, and of\r\nthe latter\xe2\x80\x99s innuendoes.\r\n\r\nI was hardly as clear as I could wish. I repeated myself several times,\r\nand occasionally had to go back to some detail that I had forgotten.\r\nPoirot smiled kindly on me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe mind is confused? Is it not so? Take time, _mon ami_. You are\r\nagitated; you are excited\xe2\x80\x94it is but natural. Presently, when we are\r\ncalmer, we will arrange the facts, neatly, each in his proper place. We\r\nwill examine\xe2\x80\x94and reject. Those of importance we will put on one side;\r\nthose of no importance, pouf!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he screwed up his cherub-like face, and\r\npuffed comically enough\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cblow them away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all very well,\xe2\x80\x9d I objected, \xe2\x80\x9cbut how are you going to decide\r\nwhat is important, and what isn\xe2\x80\x99t? That always seems the difficulty to\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot shook his head energetically. He was now arranging his moustache\r\nwith exquisite care.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so. _Voyons!_ One fact leads to another\xe2\x80\x94so we continue. Does the\r\nnext fit in with that? _A merveille!_ Good! We can proceed. This next\r\nlittle fact\xe2\x80\x94no! Ah, that is curious! There is something missing\xe2\x80\x94a link\r\nin the chain that is not there. We examine. We search. And that little\r\ncurious fact, that possibly paltry little detail that will not tally,\r\nwe put it here!\xe2\x80\x9d He made an extravagant gesture with his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\r\nsignificant! It is tremendous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cY\xe2\x80\x94es\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot shook his forefinger so fiercely at me that I quailed\r\nbefore it. \xe2\x80\x9cBeware! Peril to the detective who says: \xe2\x80\x98It is so small\xe2\x80\x94it\r\ndoes not matter. It will not agree. I will forget it.\xe2\x80\x99 That way lies\r\nconfusion! Everything matters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know. You always told me that. That\xe2\x80\x99s why I have gone into all the\r\ndetails of this thing whether they seemed to me relevant or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am pleased with you. You have a good memory, and you have given\r\nme the facts faithfully. Of the order in which you present them, I say\r\nnothing\xe2\x80\x94truly, it is deplorable! But I make allowances\xe2\x80\x94you are upset.\r\nTo that I attribute the circumstance that you have omitted one fact of\r\nparamount importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is that?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not told me if Mrs. Inglethorp ate well last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI stared at him. Surely the war had affected the little man\xe2\x80\x99s brain. He\r\nwas carefully engaged in brushing his coat before putting it on, and\r\nseemed wholly engrossed in the task.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember,\xe2\x80\x9d I said. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, anyway, I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not see? But it is of the first importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t see why,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, rather nettled. \xe2\x80\x9cAs far as I can remember,\r\nshe didn\xe2\x80\x99t eat much. She was obviously upset, and it had taken her\r\nappetite away. That was only natural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot thoughtfully, \xe2\x80\x9cit was only natural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe opened a drawer, and took out a small despatch-case, then turned to\r\nme.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I am ready. We will proceed to the ch\xc3\xa2teau, and study matters on\r\nthe spot. Excuse me, _mon ami_, you dressed in haste, and your tie is\r\non one side. Permit me.\xe2\x80\x9d With a deft gesture, he rearranged it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_\xc3\x87a y est!_ Now, shall we start?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe hurried up the village, and turned in at the lodge gates. Poirot\r\nstopped for a moment, and gazed sorrowfully over the beautiful expanse\r\nof park, still glittering with morning dew.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo beautiful, so beautiful, and yet, the poor family, plunged in\r\nsorrow, prostrated with grief.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at me keenly as he spoke, and I was aware that I reddened\r\nunder his prolonged gaze.\r\n\r\nWas the family prostrated by grief? Was the sorrow at Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath so great? I realized that there was an emotional lack in the\r\natmosphere. The dead woman had not the gift of commanding love. Her\r\ndeath was a shock and a distress, but she would not be passionately\r\nregretted.\r\n\r\nPoirot seemed to follow my thoughts. He nodded his head gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, you are right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not as though there was a blood\r\ntie. She has been kind and generous to these Cavendishes, but she was\r\nnot their own mother. Blood tells\xe2\x80\x94always remember that\xe2\x80\x94blood tells.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would tell me why you wanted to know if\r\nMrs. Inglethorp ate well last night? I have been turning it over in my\r\nmind, but I can\xe2\x80\x99t see how it has anything to do with the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was silent for a minute or two as we walked along, but finally he\r\nsaid:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not mind telling you\xe2\x80\x94though, as you know, it is not my habit to\r\nexplain until the end is reached. The present contention is that Mrs.\r\nInglethorp died of strychnine poisoning, presumably administered in her\r\ncoffee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what time was the coffee served?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTherefore she drank it between then and half-past eight\xe2\x80\x94certainly not\r\nmuch later. Well, strychnine is a fairly rapid poison. Its effects\r\nwould be felt very soon, probably in about an hour. Yet, in Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s case, the symptoms do not manifest themselves until five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock the next morning: nine hours! But a heavy meal, taken at about\r\nthe same time as the poison, might retard its effects, though hardly to\r\nthat extent. Still, it is a possibility to be taken into account. But,\r\naccording to you, she ate very little for supper, and yet the symptoms\r\ndo not develop until early the next morning! Now that is a curious\r\ncircumstance, my friend. Something may arise at the autopsy to explain\r\nit. In the meantime, remember it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs we neared the house, John came out and met us. His face looked weary\r\nand haggard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is a very dreadful business, Monsieur Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHastings\r\nhas explained to you that we are anxious for no publicity?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI comprehend perfectly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see, it is only suspicion so far. We have nothing to go upon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely. It is a matter of precaution only.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn turned to me, taking out his cigarette-case, and lighting a\r\ncigarette as he did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know that fellow Inglethorp is back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I met him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn flung the match into an adjacent flower bed, a proceeding which\r\nwas too much for Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s feelings. He retrieved it, and buried it\r\nneatly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s jolly difficult to know how to treat him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat difficulty will not exist long,\xe2\x80\x9d pronounced Poirot quietly.\r\n\r\nJohn looked puzzled, not quite understanding the portent of this\r\ncryptic saying. He handed the two keys which Dr. Bauerstein had given\r\nhim to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShow Monsieur Poirot everything he wants to see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe rooms are locked?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDr. Bauerstein considered it advisable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen he is very sure. Well, that simplifies matters for us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe went up together to the room of the tragedy. For convenience I\r\nappend a plan of the room and the principal articles of furniture in\r\nit.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n       02\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nPoirot locked the door on the inside, and proceeded to a minute\r\ninspection of the room. He darted from one object to the other with the\r\nagility of a grasshopper. I remained by the door, fearing to obliterate\r\nany clues. Poirot, however, did not seem grateful to me for my\r\nforbearance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have you, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you remain there like\xe2\x80\x94how\r\ndo you say it?\xe2\x80\x94ah, yes, the stuck pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI explained that I was afraid of obliterating any foot-marks.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoot-marks? But what an idea! There has already been practically an\r\narmy in the room! What foot-marks are we likely to find? No, come here\r\nand aid me in my search. I will put down my little case until I need\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe did so, on the round table by the window, but it was an ill-advised\r\nproceeding; for, the top of it being loose, it tilted up, and\r\nprecipitated the despatch-case on the floor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Eh voil\xc3\xa0 une table!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, my friend, one may live in a\r\nbig house and yet have no comfort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter which piece of moralizing, he resumed his search.\r\n\r\nA small purple despatch-case, with a key in the lock, on the\r\nwriting-table, engaged his attention for some time. He took out the key\r\nfrom the lock, and passed it to me to inspect. I saw nothing peculiar,\r\nhowever. It was an ordinary key of the Yale type, with a bit of twisted\r\nwire through the handle.\r\n\r\nNext, he examined the framework of the door we had broken in, assuring\r\nhimself that the bolt had really been shot. Then he went to the door\r\nopposite leading into Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room. That door was also bolted, as I\r\nhad stated. However, he went to the length of unbolting it, and opening\r\nand shutting it several times; this he did with the utmost precaution\r\nagainst making any noise. Suddenly something in the bolt itself seemed\r\nto rivet his attention. He examined it carefully, and then, nimbly\r\nwhipping out a pair of small forceps from his case, he drew out some\r\nminute particle which he carefully sealed up in a tiny envelope.\r\n\r\nOn the chest of drawers there was a tray with a spirit lamp and a small\r\nsaucepan on it. A small quantity of a dark fluid remained in the\r\nsaucepan, and an empty cup and saucer that had been drunk out of stood\r\nnear it.\r\n\r\nI wondered how I could have been so unobservant as to overlook this.\r\nHere was a clue worth having. Poirot delicately dipped his finger into\r\nliquid, and tasted it gingerly. He made a grimace.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCocoa\xe2\x80\x94with\xe2\x80\x94I think\xe2\x80\x94rum in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe passed on to the debris on the floor, where the table by the bed had\r\nbeen overturned. A reading-lamp, some books, matches, a bunch of keys,\r\nand the crushed fragments of a coffee-cup lay scattered about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, this is curious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must confess that I see nothing particularly curious about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not? Observe the lamp\xe2\x80\x94the chimney is broken in two places; they\r\nlie there as they fell. But see, the coffee-cup is absolutely smashed\r\nto powder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said wearily, \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose someone must have stepped on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, in an odd voice. \xe2\x80\x9cSomeone stepped on it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rose from his knees, and walked slowly across to the mantelpiece,\r\nwhere he stood abstractedly fingering the ornaments, and straightening\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94a trick of his when he was agitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon ami_,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, turning to me, \xe2\x80\x9csomebody stepped on that cup,\r\ngrinding it to powder, and the reason they did so was either because it\r\ncontained strychnine or\xe2\x80\x94which is far more serious\xe2\x80\x94because it did not\r\ncontain strychnine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI made no reply. I was bewildered, but I knew that it was no good\r\nasking him to explain. In a moment or two he roused himself, and went\r\non with his investigations. He picked up the bunch of keys from the\r\nfloor, and twirling them round in his fingers finally selected one,\r\nvery bright and shining, which he tried in the lock of the purple\r\ndespatch-case. It fitted, and he opened the box, but after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhesitation, closed and relocked it, and slipped the bunch of keys, as\r\nwell as the key that had originally stood in the lock, into his own\r\npocket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no authority to go through these papers. But it should be\r\ndone\xe2\x80\x94at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe then made a very careful examination of the drawers of the\r\nwash-stand. Crossing the room to the left-hand window, a round stain,\r\nhardly visible on the dark brown carpet, seemed to interest him\r\nparticularly. He went down on his knees, examining it minutely\xe2\x80\x94even\r\ngoing so far as to smell it.\r\n\r\nFinally, he poured a few drops of the cocoa into a test tube, sealing\r\nit up carefully. His next proceeding was to take out a little notebook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have found in this room,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, writing busily, \xe2\x80\x9csix points of\r\ninterest. Shall I enumerate them, or will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you,\xe2\x80\x9d I replied hastily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well, then. One, a coffee-cup that has been ground into powder;\r\ntwo, a despatch-case with a key in the lock; three, a stain on the\r\nfloor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat may have been done some time ago,\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, for it is still perceptibly damp and smells of coffee. Five, _this_!\xe2\x80\x9d With a\r\ndramatic gesture, he pointed to a large splash of candle grease on the\r\nfloor by the writing-table. \xe2\x80\x9cIt must have been done since yesterday,\r\notherwise a good housemaid would have at once removed it with\r\nblotting-paper and a hot iron. One of my best hats once\xe2\x80\x94but that is not\r\nto the point.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was very likely done last night. We were very agitated.
