This is an exact reproduction of it:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n       03\r\n\r\n\r\nI was puzzled. It was unusually thick, quite unlike ordinary notepaper.\r\nSuddenly an idea struck me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is a fragment of a will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked up at him sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not surprised?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cI expected it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI relinquished the piece of paper, and watched him put it away in his\r\ncase, with the same methodical care that he bestowed on everything. My\r\nbrain was in a whirl. What was this complication of a will? Who had\r\ndestroyed it? The person who had left the candle grease on the floor?\r\nObviously. But how had anyone gained admission? All the doors had been\r\nbolted on the inside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot briskly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe will go. As to the sleeping powders, I knew by this.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\nsuddenly produced a small cardboard box, such as chemists use for\r\npowders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you find it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the wash-stand drawer in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s bedroom. Mrs. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x99 No, I see nothing unusual.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the fact that there is no chemist\xe2\x80\x99s name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cTo be sure, that is odd!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever known a chemist to send out a box like that, without his\r\nprinted name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say that I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was becoming quite excited, but Poirot damped my ardour by remarking:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet the explanation is quite simple. So do not intrigue yourself, my\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn audible creaking proclaimed the approach of Annie, so I had no time\r\nto reply.\r\n\r\nAnnie was a fine, strapping girl, and was evidently labouring under\r\nintense excitement, mingled with a certain ghoulish enjoyment of the\r\ntragedy.\r\n\r\nPoirot came to the point at once, with a business-like briskness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sent for you, Annie, because I thought you might be able to tell me\r\nsomething about the letters Mrs. Inglethorp wrote last night. How many\r\nwere there? And can you tell me any of the names and addresses?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnnie considered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were four letters, sir. One was to Miss Howard, and one was to\r\nMr. Wells, the lawyer, and the other two I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I remember,\r\nsir\xe2\x80\x94oh, yes, one was to Ross\xe2\x80\x99s, the caterers in Tadminster. The other\r\none, I don\xe2\x80\x99t remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThink,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Poirot.\r\n\r\nAnnie racked her brains in vain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry, sir, but it\xe2\x80\x99s clean gone. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I can have noticed\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, not betraying any sign of\r\ndisappointment. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I want to ask you about something else. There is a\r\nsaucepan in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room with some cocoa in it. Did she have\r\nthat every night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, it was put in her room every evening, and she warmed it up\r\nin the night\xe2\x80\x94whenever she fancied it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it? Plain cocoa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, made with milk, with a teaspoonful of sugar, and two\r\nteaspoonfuls of rum in it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho took it to her room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt what time?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I went to draw the curtains, as a rule, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you bring it straight up from the kitchen then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, you see there\xe2\x80\x99s not much room on the gas stove, so cook used\r\nto make it early, before putting the vegetables on for supper. Then I\r\nused to bring it up, and put it on the table by the swing door, and\r\ntake it into her room later.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe swing door is in the left wing, is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the table, is it on this side of the door, or on the\r\nfarther\xe2\x80\x94servants\xe2\x80\x99 side?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s this side, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time did you bring it up last night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout quarter-past seven, I should say, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd when did you take it into Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I went to shut up, sir. About eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Mrs. Inglethorp came\r\nup to bed before I\xe2\x80\x99d finished.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, between seven-fifteen and eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, the cocoa was standing\r\non the table in the left wing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d Annie had been growing redder and redder in the face, and\r\nnow she blurted out unexpectedly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if there _was_ salt in it, sir, it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t me. I never took the\r\nsalt near it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat makes you think there was salt in it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing it on the tray, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw some salt on the tray?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Coarse kitchen salt, it looked. I never noticed it when I took\r\nthe tray up, but when I came to take it into the mistress\xe2\x80\x99s room I saw\r\nit at once, and I suppose I ought to have taken it down again, and\r\nasked cook to make some fresh. But I was in a hurry, because Dorcas was\r\nout, and I thought maybe the cocoa itself was all right, and the salt\r\nhad only gone on the tray. So I dusted it off with my apron, and took\r\nit in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had the utmost difficulty in controlling my excitement. Unknown to\r\nherself, Annie had provided us with an important piece of evidence. How\r\nshe would have gaped if she had realized that her \xe2\x80\x9ccoarse kitchen salt\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwas strychnine, one of the most deadly poisons known to mankind. I\r\nmarvelled at Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s calm. His self-control was astonishing. I awaited\r\nhis next question with impatience, but it disappointed me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you went into Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room, was the door leading into\r\nMiss Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room bolted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh! Yes, sir; it always was. It had never been opened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the door into Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room? Did you notice if that was\r\nbolted too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnnie hesitated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t rightly say, sir; it was shut but I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t say whether it\r\nwas bolted or not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you finally left the room, did Mrs. Inglethorp bolt the door\r\nafter you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, not then, but I expect she did later. She usually did lock it\r\nat night. The door into the passage, that is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you notice any candle grease on the floor when you did the room\r\nyesterday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCandle grease? Oh, no, sir. Mrs. Inglethorp didn\xe2\x80\x99t have a candle, only\r\na reading-lamp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, if there had been a large patch of candle grease on the floor,\r\nyou think you would have been sure to have seen it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, and I would have taken it out with a piece of blotting-paper\r\nand a hot iron.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen Poirot repeated the question he had put to Dorcas:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid your mistress ever have a green dress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor a mantle, nor a cape, nor a\xe2\x80\x94how do you call it?\xe2\x80\x94a sports coat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot green, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor anyone else in the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnnie reflected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sure of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Bien!_ That is all I want to know. Thank you very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a nervous giggle, Annie took herself creakingly out of the room.\r\nMy pent-up excitement burst forth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I cried, \xe2\x80\x9cI congratulate you! This is a great discovery.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is a great discovery?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, that it was the cocoa and not the coffee that was poisoned. That\r\nexplains everything! Of course it did not take effect until the early\r\nmorning, since the cocoa was only drunk in the middle of the night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you think that the cocoa\xe2\x80\x94mark well what I say, Hastings, the\r\n_cocoa_\xe2\x80\x94contained strychnine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course! That salt on the tray, what else could it have been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might have been salt,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot placidly.\r\n\r\nI shrugged my shoulders. If he was going to take the matter that way,\r\nit was no good arguing with him. The idea crossed my mind, not for the\r\nfirst time, that poor old Poirot was growing old. Privately I thought\r\nit lucky that he had associated with him someone of a more receptive\r\ntype of mind.\r\n\r\nPoirot was surveying me with quietly twinkling eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not pleased with me, _mon ami?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I said coldly, \xe2\x80\x9cit is not for me to dictate to you.\r\nYou have a right to your own opinion, just as I have to mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most admirable sentiment,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Poirot, rising briskly to his\r\nfeet. \xe2\x80\x9cNow I have finished with this room. By the way, whose is the\r\nsmaller desk in the corner?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d He tried the roll top tentatively. \xe2\x80\x9cLocked. But perhaps one of\r\nMrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s keys would open it.\xe2\x80\x9d He tried several, twisting and\r\nturning them with a practiced hand, and finally uttering an ejaculation\r\nof satisfaction. \xe2\x80\x9c_Voil\xc3\xa0!_ It is not the key, but it will open it at a\r\npinch.\xe2\x80\x9d He slid back the roll top, and ran a rapid eye over the neatly\r\nfiled papers. To my surprise, he did not examine them, merely remarking\r\napprovingly as he relocked the desk: \xe2\x80\x9cDecidedly, he is a man of method,\r\nthis Mr. Inglethorp!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA \xe2\x80\x9cman of method\xe2\x80\x9d was, in Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s estimation, the highest praise that\r\ncould be bestowed on any individual.\r\n\r\nI felt that my friend was not what he had been as he rambled on\r\ndisconnectedly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere were no stamps in his desk, but there might have been, eh, _mon\r\nami?_ There might have been? Yes\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94his eyes wandered round the\r\nroom\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthis boudoir has nothing more to tell us. It did not yield much.\r\nOnly this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe pulled a crumpled envelope out of his pocket, and tossed it over to\r\nme. It was rather a curious document. A plain, dirty looking old\r\nenvelope with a few words scrawled across it, apparently at random. The\r\nfollowing is a facsimile of it.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n       04\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V. \xe2\x80\x9cIT ISN\xe2\x80\x99T STRYCHNINE, IS IT?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you find this?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked Poirot, in lively curiosity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the waste-paper basket. You recognise the handwriting?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s. But what does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot say\xe2\x80\x94but it is suggestive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA wild idea flashed across me. Was it possible that Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmind was deranged? Had she some fantastic idea of demoniacal\r\npossession? And, if that were so, was it not also possible that she\r\nmight have taken her own life?\r\n\r\nI was about to expound these theories to Poirot, when his own words\r\ndistracted me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cnow to examine the coffee-cups!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Poirot! What on earth is the good of that, now that we know\r\nabout the cocoa?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, _l\xc3\xa0 l\xc3\xa0!_ That miserable cocoa!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot flippantly.\r\n\r\nHe laughed with apparent enjoyment, raising his arms to heaven in mock\r\ndespair, in what I could not but consider the worst possible taste.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, anyway,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, with increasing coldness, \xe2\x80\x9cas Mrs. Inglethorp\r\ntook her coffee upstairs with her, I do not see what you expect to\r\nfind, unless you consider it likely that we shall discover a packet of\r\nstrychnine on the coffee tray!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot was sobered at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, come, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, slipping his arms through mine. \xe2\x80\x9c_Ne\r\nvous f\xc3\xa2chez pas!_ Allow me to interest myself in my coffee-cups, and I\r\nwill respect your cocoa. There! Is it a bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was so quaintly humorous that I was forced to laugh; and we went\r\ntogether to the drawing-room, where the coffee-cups and tray remained\r\nundisturbed as we had left them.\r\n\r\nPoirot made me recapitulate the scene of the night before, listening\r\nvery carefully, and verifying the position of the various cups.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Mrs. Cavendish stood by the tray\xe2\x80\x94and poured out. Yes. Then she came\r\nacross to the window where you sat with Mademoiselle Cynthia. Yes. Here\r\nare the three cups. And the cup on the mantelpiece, half drunk, that\r\nwould be Mr. Lawrence Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s. And the one on the tray?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s. I saw him put it down there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. One, two, three, four, five\xe2\x80\x94but where, then, is the cup of Mr.\r\nInglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does not take coffee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen all are accounted for. One moment, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith infinite care, he took a drop or two from the grounds in each cup,\r\nsealing them up in separate test tubes, tasting each in turn as he did\r\nso. His physiognomy underwent a curious change. An expression gathered\r\nthere that I can only describe as half puzzled, and half relieved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Bien!_\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is evident! I had an idea\xe2\x80\x94but clearly I\r\nwas mistaken. Yes, altogether I was mistaken. Yet it is strange. But no\r\nmatter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, with a characteristic shrug, he dismissed whatever it was that was\r\nworrying him from his mind. I could have told him from the beginning\r\nthat this obsession of his over the coffee was bound to end in a blind\r\nalley, but I restrained my tongue. After all, though he was old, Poirot\r\nhad been a great man in his day.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBreakfast is ready,\xe2\x80\x9d said John Cavendish, coming in from the hall.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will breakfast with us, Monsieur Poirot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot acquiesced. I observed John. Already he was almost restored to\r\nhis normal self. The shock of the events of the last night had upset\r\nhim temporarily, but his equable poise soon swung back to the normal.\r\nHe was a man of very little imagination, in sharp contrast with his\r\nbrother, who had, perhaps, too much.\r\n\r\nEver since the early hours of the morning, John had been hard at work,\r\nsending telegrams\xe2\x80\x94one of the first had gone to Evelyn Howard\xe2\x80\x94writing\r\nnotices for the papers, and generally occupying himself with the\r\nmelancholy duties that a death entails.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask how things are proceeding?\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cDo your investigations\r\npoint to my mother having died a natural death\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94or must we prepare\r\nourselves for the worst?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think, Mr. Cavendish,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would do well\r\nnot to buoy yourself up with any false hopes. Can you tell me the views\r\nof the other members of the family?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother Lawrence is convinced that we are making a fuss over\r\nnothing. He says that everything points to its being a simple case of\r\nheart failure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe does, does he? That is very interesting\xe2\x80\x94very interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured\r\nPoirot softly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mrs. Cavendish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA faint cloud passed over John\xe2\x80\x99s face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not the least idea what my wife\xe2\x80\x99s views on the subject are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe answer brought a momentary stiffness in its train. John broke the\r\nrather awkward silence by saying with a slight effort:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI told you, didn\xe2\x80\x99t I, that Mr. Inglethorp has returned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot bent his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s an awkward position for all of us. Of course one has to treat him\r\nas usual\xe2\x80\x94but, hang it all, one\xe2\x80\x99s gorge does rise at sitting down to eat\r\nwith a possible murderer!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded sympathetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI quite understand. It is a very difficult situation for you, Mr.\r\nCavendish. I would like to ask you one question. Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreason for not returning last night was, I believe, that he had\r\nforgotten the latch-key. Is not that so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you are quite sure that the latch-key _was_ forgotten\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nhe did not take it after all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no idea. I never thought of looking. We always keep it in the\r\nhall drawer. I\xe2\x80\x99ll go and see if it\xe2\x80\x99s there now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot held up his hand with a faint smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, Mr. Cavendish, it is too late now. I am certain that you would\r\nfind it. If Mr. Inglethorp did take it, he has had ample time to\r\nreplace it by now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut do you think\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think nothing. If anyone had chanced to look this morning before his\r\nreturn, and seen it there, it would have been a valuable point in his\r\nfavour. That is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn looked perplexed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo not worry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot smoothly. \xe2\x80\x9cI assure you that you need not\r\nlet it trouble you. Since you are so kind, let us go and have some\r\nbreakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEveryone was assembled in the dining-room. Under the circumstances, we\r\nwere naturally not a cheerful party. The reaction after a shock is\r\nalways trying, and I think we were all suffering from it. Decorum and\r\ngood breeding naturally enjoined that our demeanour should be much as\r\nusual, yet I could not help wondering if this self-control were really\r\na matter of great difficulty. There were no red eyes, no signs of\r\nsecretly indulged grief. I felt that I was right in my opinion that\r\nDorcas was the person most affected by the personal side of the\r\ntragedy.\r\n\r\nI pass over Alfred Inglethorp, who acted the bereaved widower in a\r\nmanner that I felt to be disgusting in its hypocrisy. Did he know that\r\nwe suspected him, I wondered. Surely he could not be unaware of the\r\nfact, conceal it as we would. Did he feel some secret stirring of fear,\r\nor was he confident that his crime would go unpunished? Surely the\r\nsuspicion in the atmosphere must warn him that he was already a marked\r\nman.\r\n\r\nBut did everyone suspect him? What about Mrs. Cavendish? I watched her\r\nas she sat at the head of the table, graceful, composed, enigmatic. In\r\nher soft grey frock, with white ruffles at the wrists falling over her\r\nslender hands, she looked very beautiful. When she chose, however, her\r\nface could be sphinx-like in its inscrutability. She was very silent,\r\nhardly opening her lips, and yet in some queer way I felt that the\r\ngreat strength of her personality was dominating us all.\r\n\r\nAnd little Cynthia? Did she suspect? She looked very tired and ill, I\r\nthought. The heaviness and languor of her manner were very marked. I\r\nasked her if she were feeling ill, and she answered frankly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got the most beastly headache.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave another cup of coffee, mademoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot solicitously.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will revive you. It is unparalleled for the _mal de t\xc3\xaate_.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\njumped up and took her cup.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo sugar,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cynthia, watching him, as he picked up the\r\nsugar-tongs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo sugar? You abandon it in the war-time, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I never take it in coffee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Sacr\xc3\xa9!_\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Poirot to himself, as he brought back the\r\nreplenished cup.\r\n\r\nOnly I heard him, and glancing up curiously at the little man I saw\r\nthat his face was working with suppressed excitement, and his eyes were\r\nas green as a cat\xe2\x80\x99s. He had heard or seen something that had affected\r\nhim strongly\xe2\x80\x94but what was it? I do not usually label myself as dense,\r\nbut I must confess that nothing out of the ordinary had attracted _my_\r\nattention.\r\n\r\nIn another moment, the door opened and Dorcas appeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Wells to see you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to John.\r\n\r\nI remembered the name as being that of the lawyer to whom Mrs.\r\nInglethorp had written the night before.\r\n\r\nJohn rose immediately.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShow him into my study.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he turned to us. \xe2\x80\x9cMy mother\xe2\x80\x99s lawyer,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe explained. And in a lower voice: \xe2\x80\x9cHe is also Coroner\xe2\x80\x94you understand.\r\nPerhaps you would like to come with me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe acquiesced and followed him out of the room. John strode on ahead\r\nand I took the opportunity of whispering to Poirot:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere will be an inquest then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded absently. He seemed absorbed in thought; so much so that\r\nmy curiosity was aroused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it? You are not attending to what I say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true, my friend. I am much worried.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause Mademoiselle Cynthia does not take sugar in her coffee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat? You cannot be serious?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I am most serious. Ah, there is something there that I do not\r\nunderstand. My instinct was right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat instinct?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe instinct that led me to insist on examining those coffee-cups.\r\n_Chut!_ no more now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe followed John into his study, and he closed the door behind us.\r\n\r\nMr. Wells was a pleasant man of middle-age, with keen eyes, and the\r\ntypical lawyer\xe2\x80\x99s mouth. John introduced us both, and explained the\r\nreason of our presence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will understand, Wells,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cthat this is all strictly\r\nprivate. We are still hoping that there will turn out to be no need for\r\ninvestigation of any kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so, quite so,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wells soothingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish we could have\r\nspared you the pain and publicity of an inquest, but of course it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nquite unavoidable in the absence of a doctor\xe2\x80\x99s certificate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I suppose so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClever man, Bauerstein. Great authority on toxicology, I believe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said John with a certain stiffness in his manner. Then he\r\nadded rather hesitatingly: \xe2\x80\x9cShall we have to appear as witnesses\xe2\x80\x94all of\r\nus, I mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou, of course\xe2\x80\x94and ah\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94Mr.\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA slight pause ensued before the lawyer went on in his soothing manner:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny other evidence will be simply confirmatory, a mere matter of\r\nform.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA faint expression of relief swept over John\xe2\x80\x99s face. It puzzled me, for\r\nI saw no occasion for it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you know of nothing to the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Mr. Wells, \xe2\x80\x9cI had\r\nthought of Friday. That will give us plenty of time for the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreport. The post-mortem is to take place to-night, I believe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen that arrangement will suit you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI need not tell you, my dear Cavendish, how distressed I am at this\r\nmost tragic affair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you give us no help in solving it, monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Poirot,\r\nspeaking for the first time since we had entered the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we heard that Mrs. Inglethorp wrote to you last night. You should\r\nhave received the letter this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did, but it contains no information. It is merely a note asking me\r\nto call upon her this morning, as she wanted my advice on a matter of\r\ngreat importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe gave you no hint as to what that matter might be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately, no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a pity,\xe2\x80\x9d said John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA great pity,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Poirot gravely.\r\n\r\nThere was silence. Poirot remained lost in thought for a few minutes.\r\nFinally he turned to the lawyer again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Wells, there is one thing I should like to ask you\xe2\x80\x94that is, if it\r\nis not against professional etiquette. In the event of Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s death, who would inherit her money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe lawyer hesitated a moment, and then replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe knowledge will be public property very soon, so if Mr. Cavendish\r\ndoes not object\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d interpolated John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not see any reason why I should not answer your question. By her\r\nlast will, dated August of last year, after various unimportant\r\nlegacies to servants, etc., she gave her entire fortune to her stepson,\r\nMr. John Cavendish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas not that\xe2\x80\x94pardon the question, Mr. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x94rather unfair to her\r\nother stepson, Mr. Lawrence Cavendish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I do not think so. You see, under the terms of their father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwill, while John inherited the property, Lawrence, at his stepmother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath, would come into a considerable sum of money. Mrs. Inglethorp\r\nleft her money to her elder stepson, knowing that he would have to keep\r\nup Styles. It was, to my mind, a very fair and equitable distribution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see. But I am right in saying, am I not, that by your English law\r\nthat will was automatically revoked when Mrs. Inglethorp remarried?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wells bowed his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I was about to proceed, Monsieur Poirot, that document is now null\r\nand void.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Hein!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot. He reflected for a moment, and then asked: \xe2\x80\x9cWas\r\nMrs. Inglethorp herself aware of that fact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not know. She may have been.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was,\xe2\x80\x9d said John unexpectedly. \xe2\x80\x9cWe were discussing the matter of\r\nwills being revoked by marriage only yesterday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! One more question, Mr. Wells. You say \xe2\x80\x98her last will.\xe2\x80\x99 Had Mrs.\r\nInglethorp, then, made several former wills?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn an average, she made a new will at least once a year,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nWells imperturbably. \xe2\x80\x9cShe was given to changing her mind as to her\r\ntestamentary dispositions, now benefiting one, now another member of\r\nher family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuppose,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cthat, unknown to you, she had made a new\r\nwill in favour of someone who was not, in any sense of the word, a\r\nmember of the family\xe2\x80\x94we will say Miss Howard, for instance\xe2\x80\x94would you be\r\nsurprised?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in the least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot seemed to have exhausted his questions.\r\n\r\nI drew close to him, while John and the lawyer were debating the\r\nquestion of going through Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s papers.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think Mrs. Inglethorp made a will leaving all her money to Miss\r\nHoward?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked in a low voice, with some curiosity.\r\n\r\nPoirot smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why did you ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHush!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn Cavendish had turned to Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you come with us, Monsieur Poirot? We are going through my\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s papers. Mr. Inglethorp is quite willing to leave it entirely\r\nto Mr. Wells and myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich simplifies matters very much,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the lawyer. \xe2\x80\x9cAs\r\ntechnically, of course, he was entitled\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He did not finish the\r\nsentence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will look through the desk in the boudoir first,\xe2\x80\x9d explained John,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cand go up to her bedroom afterwards. She kept her most important\r\npapers in a purple despatch-case, which we must look through\r\ncarefully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lawyer, \xe2\x80\x9cit is quite possible that there may be a later\r\nwill than the one in my possession.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere _is_ a later will.\xe2\x80\x9d It was Poirot who spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d John and the lawyer looked at him startled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOr, rather,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued my friend imperturbably, \xe2\x80\x9cthere _was_ one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean\xe2\x80\x94there was one? Where is it now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBurnt!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBurnt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. See here.\xe2\x80\x9d He took out the charred fragment we had found in the\r\ngrate in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room, and handed it to the lawyer with a\r\nbrief explanation of when and where he had found it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut possibly this is an old will?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think so. In fact I am almost certain that it was made no\r\nearlier than yesterday afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cImpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d broke simultaneously from both men.\r\n\r\nPoirot turned to John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you will allow me to send for your gardener, I will prove it to\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, of course\xe2\x80\x94but I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot raised his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo as I ask you. Afterwards you shall question as much as you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\xe2\x80\x9d He rang the bell.\r\n\r\nDorcas answered it in due course.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDorcas, will you tell Manning to come round and speak to me here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDorcas withdrew.\r\n\r\nWe waited in a tense silence. Poirot alone seemed perfectly at his\r\nease, and dusted a forgotten corner of the bookcase.\r\n\r\nThe clumping of hobnailed boots on the gravel outside proclaimed the\r\napproach of Manning. John looked questioningly at Poirot. The latter\r\nnodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome inside, Manning,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to speak to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nManning came slowly and hesitatingly through the French window, and\r\nstood as near it as he could. He held his cap in his hands, twisting it\r\nvery carefully round and round. His back was much bent, though he was\r\nprobably not as old as he looked, but his eyes were sharp and\r\nintelligent, and belied his slow and rather cautious speech.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cManning,\xe2\x80\x9d said John, \xe2\x80\x9cthis gentleman will put some questions to you\r\nwhich I want you to answer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYessir,\xe2\x80\x9d mumbled Manning.\r\n\r\nPoirot stepped forward briskly. Manning\xe2\x80\x99s eye swept over him with a\r\nfaint contempt.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were planting a bed of begonias round by the south side of the\r\nhouse yesterday afternoon, were you not, Manning?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, me and Willum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mrs. Inglethorp came to the window and called you, did she not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, she did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me in your own words exactly what happened after that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, nothing much. She just told Willum to go on his bicycle\r\ndown to the village, and bring back a form of will, or such-like\xe2\x80\x94I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what exactly\xe2\x80\x94she wrote it down for him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he did, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what happened next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe went on with the begonias, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid not Mrs. Inglethorp call you again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, both me and Willum, she called.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe made us come right in, and sign our names at the bottom of a long\r\npaper\xe2\x80\x94under where she\xe2\x80\x99d signed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you see anything of what was written above her signature?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nPoirot sharply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, there was a bit of blotting paper over that part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you signed where she told you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, first me and then Willum.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did she do with it afterwards?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, she slipped it into a long envelope, and put it inside a\r\nsort of purple box that was standing on the desk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat time was it when she first called you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout four, I should say, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot earlier? Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t it have been about half-past three?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say so, sir. It would be more likely to be a bit after\r\nfour\xe2\x80\x94not before it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Manning, that will do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot pleasantly.\r\n\r\nThe gardener glanced at his master, who nodded, whereupon Manning\r\nlifted a finger to his forehead with a low mumble, and backed\r\ncautiously out of the window.\r\n\r\nWe all looked at each other.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured John. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat an extraordinary coincidence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x94a coincidence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat my mother should have made a will on the very day of her death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wells cleared his throat and remarked drily:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you so sure it is a coincidence, Cavendish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour mother, you tell me, had a violent quarrel with\xe2\x80\x94someone yesterday\r\nafternoon\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d cried John again. There was a tremor in his voice,\r\nand he had gone very pale.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn consequence of that quarrel, your mother very suddenly and\r\nhurriedly makes a new will. The contents of that will we shall never\r\nknow. She told no one of its provisions. This morning, no doubt, she\r\nwould have consulted me on the subject\xe2\x80\x94but she had no chance. The will\r\ndisappears, and she takes its secret with her to her grave. Cavendish,\r\nI much fear there is no coincidence there. Monsieur Poirot, I am sure\r\nyou agree with me that the facts are very suggestive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuggestive, or not,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted John, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are most grateful to\r\nMonsieur Poirot for elucidating the matter. But for him, we should\r\nnever have known of this will. I suppose, I may not ask you, monsieur,\r\nwhat first led you to suspect the fact?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot smiled and answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA scribbled over old envelope, and a freshly planted bed of begonias.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn, I think, would have pressed his questions further, but at that\r\nmoment the loud purr of a motor was audible, and we all turned to the\r\nwindow as it swept past.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried John. \xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, Wells.\xe2\x80\x9d He went hurriedly out into the\r\nhall.\r\n\r\nPoirot looked inquiringly at me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard,\xe2\x80\x9d I explained.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I am glad she has come. There is a woman with a head and a heart\r\ntoo, Hastings. Though the good God gave her no beauty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI followed John\xe2\x80\x99s example, and went out into the hall, where Miss\r\nHoward was endeavouring to extricate herself from the voluminous mass\r\nof veils that enveloped her head. As her eyes fell on me, a sudden pang\r\nof guilt shot through me. This was the woman who had warned me so\r\nearnestly, and to whose warning I had, alas, paid no heed! How soon,\r\nand how contemptuously, I had dismissed it from my mind. Now that she\r\nhad been proved justified in so tragic a manner, I felt ashamed. She\r\nhad known Alfred Inglethorp only too well. I wondered whether, if she\r\nhad remained at Styles, the tragedy would have taken place, or would\r\nthe man have feared her watchful eyes?\r\n\r\nI was relieved when she shook me by the hand, with her well remembered\r\npainful grip. The eyes that met mine were sad, but not reproachful;\r\nthat she had been crying bitterly, I could tell by the redness of her\r\neyelids, but her manner was unchanged from its old gruffness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStarted the moment I got the wire. Just come off night duty. Hired\r\ncar. Quickest way to get here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you had anything to eat this morning, Evie?\xe2\x80\x9d asked John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought not. Come along, breakfast\xe2\x80\x99s not cleared away yet, and\r\nthey\xe2\x80\x99ll make you some fresh tea.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to me. \xe2\x80\x9cLook after her,\r\nHastings, will you? Wells is waiting for me. Oh, here\xe2\x80\x99s Monsieur\r\nPoirot. He\xe2\x80\x99s helping us, you know, Evie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard shook hands with Poirot, but glanced suspiciously over her\r\nshoulder at John.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean\xe2\x80\x94helping us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHelping us to investigate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing to investigate. Have they taken him to prison yet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTaken who to prison?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? Alfred Inglethorp, of course!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Evie, do be careful. Lawrence is of the opinion that my mother\r\ndied from heart seizure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore fool, Lawrence!\xe2\x80\x9d retorted Miss Howard. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course Alfred\r\nInglethorp murdered poor Emily\xe2\x80\x94as I always told you he would.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Evie, don\xe2\x80\x99t shout so. Whatever we may think or suspect, it is\r\nbetter to say as little as possible for the present. The inquest isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nuntil Friday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot until fiddlesticks!\xe2\x80\x9d The snort Miss Howard gave was truly\r\nmagnificent. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re all off your heads. The man will be out of the\r\ncountry by then. If he\xe2\x80\x99s any sense, he won\xe2\x80\x99t stay here tamely and wait\r\nto be hanged.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn Cavendish looked at her helplessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what it is,\xe2\x80\x9d she accused him, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ve been listening to the\r\ndoctors. Never should. What do they know? Nothing at all\xe2\x80\x94or just enough\r\nto make them dangerous. I ought to know\xe2\x80\x94my own father was a doctor.\r\nThat little Wilkins is about the greatest fool that even I have ever\r\nseen. Heart seizure! Sort of thing he would say. Anyone with any sense\r\ncould see at once that her husband had poisoned her. I always said he\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nmurder her in her bed, poor soul. Now he\xe2\x80\x99s done it. And all you can do\r\nis to murmur silly things about \xe2\x80\x98heart seizure\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98inquest on\r\nFriday.\xe2\x80\x99 You ought to be ashamed of yourself, John Cavendish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you want me to do?\xe2\x80\x9d asked John, unable to help a faint smile.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDash it all, Evie, I can\xe2\x80\x99t haul him down to the local police station\r\nby the scruff of his neck.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you might do something. Find out how he did it. He\xe2\x80\x99s a crafty\r\nbeggar. Dare say he soaked fly papers. Ask cook if she\xe2\x80\x99s missed any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt occurred to me very forcibly at that moment that to harbour Miss\r\nHoward and Alfred Inglethorp under the same roof, and keep the peace\r\nbetween them, was likely to prove a Herculean task, and I did not envy\r\nJohn. I could see by the expression of his face that he fully\r\nappreciated the difficulty of the position. For the moment, he sought\r\nrefuge in retreat, and left the room precipitately.\r\n\r\nDorcas brought in fresh tea. As she left the room, Poirot came over\r\nfrom the window where he had been standing, and sat down facing Miss\r\nHoward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cI want to ask you something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAsk away,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, eyeing him with some disfavour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to be able to count upon your help.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll help you to hang Alfred with pleasure,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied gruffly.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHanging\xe2\x80\x99s too good for him. Ought to be drawn and quartered, like in\r\ngood old times.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are at one then,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cfor I, too, want to hang the\r\ncriminal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlfred Inglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHim, or another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo question of another. Poor Emily was never murdered until _he_ came\r\nalong. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t surrounded by sharks\xe2\x80\x94she was. But it was\r\nonly her purse they were after. Her life was safe enough. But along\r\ncomes Mr. Alfred Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x94and within two months\xe2\x80\x94hey presto!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBelieve me, Miss Howard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot very earnestly, \xe2\x80\x9cif Mr.\r\nInglethorp is the man, he shall not escape me. On my honour, I will\r\nhang him as high as Haman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Howard more enthusiastically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I must ask you to trust me. Now your help may be very valuable to\r\nme. I will tell you why. Because, in all this house of mourning, yours\r\nare the only eyes that have wept.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard blinked, and a new note crept into the gruffness of her\r\nvoice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean that I was fond of her\xe2\x80\x94yes, I was. You know, Emily was a\r\nselfish old woman in her way. She was very generous, but she always\r\nwanted a return. She never let people forget what she had done for\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x94and, that way she missed love. Don\xe2\x80\x99t think she ever realized it,\r\nthough, or felt the lack of it. Hope not, anyway. I was on a different\r\nfooting. I took my stand from the first. \xe2\x80\x98So many pounds a year I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\nworth to you. Well and good. But not a penny piece besides\xe2\x80\x94not a pair\r\nof gloves, nor a theatre ticket.\xe2\x80\x99 She didn\xe2\x80\x99t understand\xe2\x80\x94was very\r\noffended sometimes. And so, out of the\r\nwhole bunch, I was the only one who could allow myself to be fond of\r\nher. I watched over her. I guarded her from the lot of them, and then a\r\nglib-tongued scoundrel comes along, and pooh! all my years of devotion\r\ngo for nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded sympathetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand, mademoiselle, I understand all you feel. You think that we are lukewarm\xe2\x80\x94that we lack fire and\r\nenergy\xe2\x80\x94but trust me, it is not so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn stuck his head in at this juncture, and invited us both to come up\r\nto Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room, as he and Mr. Wells had finished looking\r\nthrough the desk in the boudoir.\r\n\r\nAs we went up the stairs, John looked back to the dining-room door, and\r\nlowered his voice confidentially:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook here, what\xe2\x80\x99s going to happen when these two meet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head helplessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve told Mary to keep them apart if she can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill she be able to do so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lord only knows. There\xe2\x80\x99s one thing, Inglethorp himself won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\ntoo keen on meeting her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got the keys still, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you, Poirot?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, as we\r\nreached the door of the locked room.\r\n\r\nTaking the keys from Poirot, John unlocked it, and we all passed in.\r\nThe lawyer went straight to the desk, and John followed him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother kept most of her important papers in this despatch-case, I\r\nbelieve,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nPoirot drew out the small bunch of keys.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPermit me. I locked it, out of precaution, this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it\xe2\x80\x99s not locked now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee.\xe2\x80\x9d And John lifted the lid as he spoke.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Milles tonnerres!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot, dumbfounded. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I\xe2\x80\x94who have both\r\nthe keys in my pocket!\xe2\x80\x9d He flung himself upon the case. Suddenly he\r\nstiffened. \xe2\x80\x9c_Eh voil\xc3\xa0 une affaire!_ This lock has been forced.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot laid down the case again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut who forced it? Why should they? When? But the door was locked?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nThese exclamations burst from us disjointedly.\r\n\r\nPoirot answered them categorically\xe2\x80\x94almost mechanically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho? That is the question. Why? Ah, if I only knew. When? Since I was\r\nhere an hour ago. As to the door being locked, it is a very ordinary\r\nlock. Probably any other of the doorkeys in this passage would fit it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe stared at one another blankly. Poirot had walked over to the\r\nmantelpiece. He was outwardly calm, but I noticed his hands, which from\r\nlong force of habit were mechanically straightening the spill vases on\r\nthe mantelpiece, were shaking violently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee here, it was like this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was something in\r\nthat case\xe2\x80\x94some piece of evidence, slight in itself perhaps, but still\r\nenough of a clue to connect the murderer with the crime. It was vital\r\nto him that it should be destroyed before it was discovered and its\r\nsignificance appreciated. Therefore, he took the risk, the great risk,\r\nof coming in here. Finding the case locked, he was obliged to force it,\r\nthus betraying his presence. For him to take that risk, it must have\r\nbeen something of great importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot, with a gesture of anger. \xe2\x80\x9cThat, I do not know! A\r\ndocument of some kind, without doubt, possibly the scrap of paper\r\nDorcas saw in her hand yesterday afternoon. And I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d his anger burst\r\nforth freely\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmiserable animal that I am! I guessed nothing! I have\r\nbehaved like an imbecile! I should never have left that case here. I\r\nshould have carried it away with me. Ah, triple pig! And now it is\r\ngone. It is destroyed\xe2\x80\x94but is it destroyed? Is there not yet a chance\xe2\x80\x94we\r\nmust leave no stone unturned\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe rushed like a madman from the room, and I followed him as soon as I\r\nhad sufficiently recovered my wits. But, by the time I had reached the\r\ntop of the stairs, he was out of sight.\r\n\r\nMary Cavendish was standing where the staircase branched, staring down\r\ninto the hall in the direction in which he had disappeared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has happened to your extraordinary little friend, Mr. Hastings?\r\nHe has just rushed past me like a mad bull.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s rather upset about something,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked feebly. I really did\r\nnot know how much Poirot would wish me to disclose. As I saw a faint\r\nsmile gather on Mrs. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s expressive mouth, I endeavoured to try\r\nand turn the conversation by saying: \xe2\x80\x9cThey haven\xe2\x80\x99t met yet, have they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp and Miss Howard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at me in rather a disconcerting manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think it would be such a disaster if they did meet?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d I said, rather taken aback.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d She was smiling in her quiet way. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see a good\r\nflare up. It would clear the air. At present we are all thinking so\r\nmuch, and saying so little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJohn doesn\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s anxious to keep them apart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, John!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomething in her tone fired me, and I blurted out:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOld John\xe2\x80\x99s an awfully good sort.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe studied me curiously for a minute or two, and then said, to my\r\ngreat surprise:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are loyal to your friend. I am charming to my friends one day, and forget\r\nall about them the next.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what impelled me, but I was nettled, and I said foolishly\r\nand not in the best of taste:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet you seem to be invariably charming to Dr. Bauerstein!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInstantly I regretted my words. Without a word, she turned and went swiftly up the stairs,\r\nwhilst I stood like an idiot gaping after her.\r\n\r\nI was recalled to other matters by a frightful row going on below. I\r\ncould hear Poirot shouting and expounding. I was vexed to think that my\r\ndiplomacy had been in vain. The little man appeared to be taking the\r\nwhole house into his confidence, a proceeding of which I, for one,\r\ndoubted the wisdom. Once again I could not help regretting that my\r\nfriend was so prone to lose his head in moments of excitement. I\r\nstepped briskly down the stairs. You are actually playing into\r\nthe criminal\xe2\x80\x99s hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think so, Hastings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, well, my friend, I will be guided by you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. Cynthia Murdoch was just coming in, and\r\nPoirot stood aside to let her pass.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me, mademoiselle, one minute.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d she turned inquiringly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever make up Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s medicines?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA slight flush rose in her face, as she answered rather constrainedly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly her powders?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe flush deepened as Cynthia replied:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I did make up some sleeping powders for her once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot produced the empty box which had contained powders.\r\n\r\nShe nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you tell me what they were?
