Thank you, mademoiselle; good morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs we walked briskly away from the house, I glanced at him more than\r\nonce. I had often before noticed that, if anything excited him, his\r\neyes turned green like a cat\xe2\x80\x99s. They were shining like emeralds now.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he broke out at last, \xe2\x80\x9cI have a little idea, a very\r\nstrange, and probably utterly impossible idea. And yet\xe2\x80\x94it fits in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shrugged my shoulders. I privately thought that Poirot was rather too\r\nmuch given to these fantastic ideas. In this case, surely, the truth\r\nwas only too plain and apparent.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo that is the explanation of the blank label on the box,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery simple, as you said. I really wonder that I did not think of it\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot did not appear to be listening to me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have made one more discovery, _l\xc3\xa0-bas_,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed, jerking his\r\nthumb over his shoulder in the direction of Styles. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Wells told me\r\nas we were going upstairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLocked up in the desk in the boudoir, they found a will of Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s, dated before her marriage, leaving her fortune to Alfred\r\nInglethorp. It must have been made just at the time they were engaged.\r\nIt came quite as a surprise to Wells\xe2\x80\x94and to John Cavendish also. It was\r\nwritten on one of those printed will forms, and witnessed by two of the\r\nservants\xe2\x80\x94not Dorcas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid Mr. Inglethorp know of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne might take that with a grain of salt,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked sceptically.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll these wills are very confusing. Tell me, how did those scribbled\r\nwords on the envelope help you to discover that a will was made\r\nyesterday afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon ami_, have you ever, when writing a letter, been arrested by the\r\nfact that you did not know how to spell a certain word?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, often. I suppose everyone has.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. And have you not, in such a case, tried the word once or\r\ntwice on the edge of the blotting-paper, or a spare scrap of paper, to\r\nsee if it looked right? Well, that is what Mrs. Inglethorp did. You\r\nwill notice that the word \xe2\x80\x98possessed\xe2\x80\x99 is spelt first with one \xe2\x80\x98s\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nsubsequently with two\xe2\x80\x94correctly. To make sure, she had further tried it\r\nin a sentence, thus: \xe2\x80\x98I am possessed.\xe2\x80\x99 Now, what did that tell me? It\r\ntold me that Mrs. Inglethorp had been writing the word \xe2\x80\x98possessed\xe2\x80\x99 that\r\nafternoon, and, having the fragment of paper found in the grate fresh\r\nin my mind, the possibility of a will\xe2\x80\x94(a document almost certain to\r\ncontain that word)\xe2\x80\x94occurred to me at once. This possibility was\r\nconfirmed by a further circumstance. In the general confusion, the\r\nboudoir had not been swept that morning, and near the desk were several\r\ntraces of brown mould and earth. The weather had been perfectly fine\r\nfor some days, and no ordinary boots would have left such a heavy\r\ndeposit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI strolled to the window, and saw at once that the begonia beds had\r\nbeen newly planted. The mould in the beds was exactly similar to that\r\non the floor of the boudoir, and also I learnt from you that they _had_\r\nbeen planted yesterday afternoon. I was now sure that one, or possibly\r\nboth of the gardeners\xe2\x80\x94for there were two sets of footprints in the\r\nbed\xe2\x80\x94had entered the boudoir, for if Mrs. Inglethorp had merely wished\r\nto speak to them she would in all probability have stood at the window,\r\nand they would not have come into the room at all. I was now quite\r\nconvinced that she had made a fresh will, and had called the two\r\ngardeners in to witness her signature. Events proved that I was right\r\nin my supposition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was very ingenious,\xe2\x80\x9d I could not help admitting. \xe2\x80\x9cI must confess\r\nthat the conclusions I drew from those few scribbled words were quite\r\nerroneous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou gave too much rein to your imagination. Imagination is a good\r\nservant, and a bad master. The simplest explanation is always the most\r\nlikely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother point\xe2\x80\x94how did you know that the key of the despatch-case had\r\nbeen lost?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not know it. It was a guess that turned out to be correct. You\r\nobserved that it had a piece of twisted wire through the handle. That\r\nsuggested to me at once that it had possibly been wrenched off a flimsy\r\nkey-ring. Now, if it had been lost and recovered, Mrs. Inglethorp would\r\nat once have replaced it on her bunch; but on her bunch I found what\r\nwas obviously the duplicate key, very new and bright, which led me to\r\nthe hypothesis that somebody else had inserted the original key in the\r\nlock of the despatch-case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cAlfred Inglethorp, without doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot looked at me curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very sure of his guilt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, naturally. Every fresh circumstance seems to establish it more\r\nclearly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cthere are several points in\r\nhis favour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see only one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat he was not in the house last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Bad shot!\xe2\x80\x99 as you English say! You have chosen the one point that to\r\nmy mind tells against him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause if Mr. Inglethorp knew that his wife would be poisoned last\r\nnight, he would certainly have arranged to be away from the house. His\r\nexcuse was an obviously trumped up one. That leaves us two\r\npossibilities: either he knew what was going to happen or he had a\r\nreason of his own for his absence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that reason?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked sceptically.\r\n\r\nPoirot shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should I know? Discreditable, without doubt. This Mr. Inglethorp,\r\nI should say, is somewhat of a scoundrel\xe2\x80\x94but that does not of necessity\r\nmake him a murderer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head, unconvinced.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe do not agree, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, let us leave it. Time will\r\nshow which of us is right. Now let us turn to other aspects of the\r\ncase. What do you make of the fact that all the doors of the bedroom\r\nwere bolted on the inside?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I considered. \xe2\x80\x9cOne must look at it logically.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should put it this way. The doors _were_ bolted\xe2\x80\x94our own eyes have\r\ntold us that\xe2\x80\x94yet the presence of the candle grease on the floor, and\r\nthe destruction of the will, prove that during the night someone\r\nentered the room. You agree so far?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerfectly. Put with admirable clearness. Proceed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, encouraged, \xe2\x80\x9cas the person who entered did not do so by\r\nthe window, nor by miraculous means, it follows that the door must have\r\nbeen opened from inside by Mrs. Inglethorp herself. That strengthens\r\nthe conviction that the person in question was her husband. She would\r\nnaturally open the door to her own husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should she? She had bolted the door leading into his room\xe2\x80\x94a most\r\nunusual proceeding on her part\xe2\x80\x94she had had a most violent quarrel with\r\nhim that very afternoon. No, he was the last person she would admit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you agree with me that the door must have been opened by Mrs.\r\nInglethorp herself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is another possibility. She may have forgotten to bolt the door\r\ninto the passage when she went to bed, and have got up later, towards\r\nmorning, and bolted it then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot, is that seriously your opinion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I do not say it is so, but it might be. Now, to turn to another\r\nfeature, what do you make of the scrap of conversation you overheard\r\nbetween Mrs. Cavendish and her mother-in-law?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten that,\xe2\x80\x9d I said thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is as enigmatical as\r\never. It seems incredible that a woman like Mrs. Cavendish, proud and\r\nreticent to the last degree, should interfere so violently in what was\r\ncertainly not her affair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely. It was an astonishing thing for a woman of her breeding to\r\ndo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is certainly curious,\xe2\x80\x9d I agreed. \xe2\x80\x9cStill, it is unimportant, and\r\nneed not be taken into account.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA groan burst from Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat have I always told you? Everything must be taken into account. If\r\nthe fact will not fit the theory\xe2\x80\x94let the theory go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we shall see,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, nettled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we shall see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe had reached Leastways Cottage, and Poirot ushered me upstairs to his\r\nown room. He offered me one of the tiny Russian cigarettes he himself\r\noccasionally smoked. I was amused to notice that he stowed away the\r\nused matches most carefully in a little china pot. My momentary\r\nannoyance vanished.\r\n\r\nPoirot had placed our two chairs in front of the open window which\r\ncommanded a view of the village street. The fresh air blew in warm and\r\npleasant. It was going to be a hot day.\r\n\r\nSuddenly my attention was arrested by a weedy looking young man rushing\r\ndown the street at a great pace. It was the expression on his face that\r\nwas extraordinary\xe2\x80\x94a curious mingling of terror and agitation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook, Poirot!\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\nHe leant forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Tiens!_\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is Mr. Mace, from the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop. He is\r\ncoming here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man came to a halt before Leastways Cottage, and, after\r\nhesitating a moment, pounded vigorously at the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little minute,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot from the window. \xe2\x80\x9cI come.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMotioning to me to follow him, he ran swiftly down the stairs and\r\nopened the door. Mr. Mace began at once.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Poirot, I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry for the inconvenience, but I heard that\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d just come back from the Hall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man moistened his dry lips. His face was working curiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s all over the village about old Mrs. Inglethorp dying so suddenly.\r\nThey do say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he lowered his voice cautiously\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat it\xe2\x80\x99s poison?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot\xe2\x80\x99s face remained quite impassive.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly the doctors can tell us that, Mr. Mace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, exactly\xe2\x80\x94of course\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The young man hesitated, and then his\r\nagitation was too much for him. He clutched Poirot by the arm, and sank\r\nhis voice to a whisper: \xe2\x80\x9cJust tell me this, Mr. Poirot, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94it\r\nisn\xe2\x80\x99t strychnine, is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI hardly heard what Poirot replied. Something evidently of a\r\nnon-committal nature. The young man departed, and as he closed the door\r\nPoirot\xe2\x80\x99s eyes met mine.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, nodding gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will have evidence to give at the\r\ninquest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe went slowly upstairs again. I was opening my lips, when Poirot\r\nstopped me with a gesture of his hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot now, not now, _mon ami_. I have need of reflection. My mind is in\r\nsome disorder\xe2\x80\x94which is not well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor about ten minutes he sat in dead silence, perfectly still, except\r\nfor several expressive motions of his eyebrows, and all the time his\r\neyes grew steadily greener. At last he heaved a deep sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is well. The bad moment has passed. Now all is arranged and\r\nclassified. One must never permit confusion. The case is not clear\r\nyet\xe2\x80\x94no. For it is of the most complicated! It puzzles _me_. _Me_,\r\nHercule Poirot! There are two facts of significance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what are they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe first is the state of the weather yesterday. That is very\r\nimportant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it was a glorious day!\xe2\x80\x9d I interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cPoirot, you\xe2\x80\x99re pulling my\r\nleg!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. The thermometer registered 80 degrees in the shade. Do not\r\nforget that, my friend. It is the key to the whole riddle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the second point?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe important fact that Monsieur Inglethorp wears very peculiar\r\nclothes, has a black beard, and uses glasses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot, I cannot believe you are serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am absolutely serious, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this is childish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is very momentous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd supposing the Coroner\xe2\x80\x99s jury returns a verdict of Wilful Murder\r\nagainst Alfred Inglethorp. What becomes of your theories, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey would not be shaken because twelve stupid men had happened to\r\nmake a mistake! But that will not occur. For one thing, a country jury\r\nis not anxious to take responsibility upon itself, and Mr. Inglethorp\r\nstands practically in the position of local squire. Also,\xe2\x80\x9d he added\r\nplacidly, \xe2\x80\x9c_I_ should not allow it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_You_ would not allow it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at the extraordinary little man, divided between annoyance and\r\namusement. He was so tremendously sure of himself. As though he read my\r\nthoughts, he nodded gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, _mon ami_, I would do what I say.\xe2\x80\x9d He got up and laid his\r\nhand on my shoulder. His physiognomy underwent a complete change. Tears\r\ncame into his eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cIn all this, you see, I think of that poor Mrs.\r\nInglethorp who is dead. She was not extravagantly loved\xe2\x80\x94no. But she was\r\nvery good to us Belgians\xe2\x80\x94I owe her a debt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI endeavoured to interrupt, but Poirot swept on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me tell you this, Hastings. She would never forgive me if I let\r\nAlfred Inglethorp, her husband, be arrested _now_\xe2\x80\x94when a word from me\r\ncould save him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI. THE INQUEST\r\n\r\nIn the interval before the inquest, Poirot was unfailing in his\r\nactivity. Twice he was closeted with Mr. Wells. He also took long walks\r\ninto the country. I rather resented his not taking me into his\r\nconfidence, the more so as I could not in the least guess what he was\r\ndriving at.\r\n\r\nIt occurred to me that he might have been making inquiries at Raikes\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfarm; so, finding him out when I called at Leastways Cottage on\r\nWednesday evening, I walked over there by the fields, hoping to meet\r\nhim. But there was no sign of him, and I hesitated to go right up to\r\nthe farm itself. As I walked away, I met an aged rustic, who leered at\r\nme cunningly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99m from the Hall, bain\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I\xe2\x80\x99m looking for a friend of mine whom I thought might have walked\r\nthis way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little chap? As waves his hands when he talks? One of them Belgies\r\nfrom the village?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said eagerly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has been here, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, ay, he\xe2\x80\x99s been here, right enough. More\xe2\x80\x99n once too. Friend of\r\nyours, is he? Ah, you gentlemen from the Hall\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99m a pretty lot!\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nhe leered more jocosely than ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, do the gentlemen from the Hall come here often?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, as\r\ncarelessly as I could.\r\n\r\nHe winked at me knowingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_One_ does, mister. Naming no names, mind. And a very liberal\r\ngentleman too! Oh, thank you, sir, I\xe2\x80\x99m sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI walked on sharply. Evelyn Howard had been right then, and I\r\nexperienced a sharp twinge of disgust, as I thought of Alfred\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s liberality with another woman\xe2\x80\x99s money. Had that piquant\r\ngipsy face been at the bottom of the crime, or was it the baser\r\nmainspring of money? Probably a judicious mixture of both.\r\n\r\nOn one point, Poirot seemed to have a curious obsession. He once or\r\ntwice observed to me that he thought Dorcas must have made an error in\r\nfixing the time of the quarrel. He suggested to her repeatedly that it\r\nwas four-thirty, and not four o\xe2\x80\x99clock when she had heard the voices.\r\n\r\nBut Dorcas was unshaken. Quite an hour, or even more, had elapsed\r\nbetween the time when she had heard the voices and five o\xe2\x80\x99clock, when\r\nshe had taken tea to her mistress.\r\n\r\nThe inquest was held on Friday at the Stylites Arms in the village.\r\nPoirot and I sat together, not being required to give evidence.\r\n\r\nThe preliminaries were gone through. The jury viewed the body, and John\r\nCavendish gave evidence of identification.\r\n\r\nFurther questioned, he described his awakening in the early hours of\r\nthe morning, and the circumstances of his mother\xe2\x80\x99s death.\r\n\r\nThe medical evidence was next taken. There was a breathless hush, and\r\nevery eye was fixed on the famous London specialist, who was known to\r\nbe one of the greatest authorities of the day on the subject of\r\ntoxicology.\r\n\r\nIn a few brief words, he summed up the result of the post-mortem. Shorn\r\nof its medical phraseology and technicalities, it amounted to the fact\r\nthat Mrs. Inglethorp had met her death as the result of strychnine\r\npoisoning. Judging from the quantity recovered, she must have taken not\r\nless than three-quarters of a grain of strychnine, but probably one\r\ngrain or slightly over.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible that she could have swallowed the poison by accident?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nasked the Coroner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should consider it very unlikely. Strychnine is not used for\r\ndomestic purposes, as some poisons are, and there are restrictions\r\nplaced on its sale.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes anything in your examination lead you to determine how the poison\r\nwas administered?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou arrived at Styles before Dr. Wilkins, I believe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is so. The motor met me just outside the lodge gates, and I\r\nhurried there as fast as I could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you relate to us exactly what happened next?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI entered Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room. She was at that moment in a typical\r\ntetanic convulsion. She turned towards me, and gasped out:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Alfred\xe2\x80\x94Alfred\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCould the strychnine have been administered in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nafter-dinner coffee which was taken to her by her husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly, but strychnine is a fairly rapid drug in its action. The\r\nsymptoms appear from one to two hours after it has been swallowed. It\r\nis retarded under certain conditions, none of which, however, appear to\r\nhave been present in this case. I presume Mrs. Inglethorp took the\r\ncoffee after dinner about eight o\xe2\x80\x99clock, whereas the symptoms did not\r\nmanifest themselves until the early hours of the morning, which, on the\r\nface of it, points to the drug having been taken much later in the\r\nevening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Inglethorp was in the habit of drinking a cup of cocoa in the\r\nmiddle of the night. Could the strychnine have been administered in\r\nthat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I myself took a sample of the cocoa remaining in the saucepan and\r\nhad it analysed. There was no strychnine present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI heard Poirot chuckle softly beside me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you know?\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cListen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the doctor was continuing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat I would have been\r\nconsiderably surprised at any other result.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSimply because strychnine has an unusually bitter taste. It can be\r\ndetected in a solution of one in seventy thousand, and can only be\r\ndisguised by some strongly flavoured substance. Cocoa would be quite\r\npowerless to mask it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne of the jury wanted to know if the same objection applied to coffee.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Coffee has a bitter taste of its own which would probably cover\r\nthe taste of strychnine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you consider it more likely that the drug was administered in the\r\ncoffee, but that for some unknown reason its action was delayed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but, the cup being completely smashed, there is no possibility of\r\nanalyzing its contents.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis concluded Dr. Bauerstein\xe2\x80\x99s evidence. Dr. Wilkins corroborated it\r\non all points. Sounded as to the possibility of suicide, he repudiated\r\nit utterly. The deceased, he said, suffered from a weak heart, but\r\notherwise enjoyed perfect health, and was of a cheerful and\r\nwell-balanced disposition. She would be one of the last people to take\r\nher own life.\r\n\r\nLawrence Cavendish was next called. His evidence was quite unimportant,\r\nbeing a mere repetition of that of his brother. Just as he was about to\r\nstep down, he paused, and said rather hesitatingly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to make a suggestion if I may?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe glanced deprecatingly at the Coroner, who replied briskly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly, Mr. Cavendish, we are here to arrive at the truth of this\r\nmatter, and welcome anything that may lead to further elucidation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is just an idea of mine,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Lawrence. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I may be\r\nquite wrong, but it still seems to me that my mother\xe2\x80\x99s death might be\r\naccounted for by natural means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you make that out, Mr. Cavendish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother, at the time of her death, and for some time before it, was\r\ntaking a tonic containing strychnine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Coroner.\r\n\r\nThe jury looked up, interested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Lawrence, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there have been cases where the\r\ncumulative effect of a drug, administered for some time, has ended by\r\ncausing death. Also, is it not possible that she may have taken an\r\noverdose of her medicine by accident?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the first we have heard of the deceased taking strychnine at\r\nthe time of her death. We are much obliged to you, Mr. Cavendish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDr. Wilkins was recalled and ridiculed the idea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat Mr. Cavendish suggests is quite impossible. Any doctor would tell\r\nyou the same. Strychnine is, in a certain sense, a cumulative poison,\r\nbut it would be quite impossible for it to result in sudden death in\r\nthis way. There would have to be a long period of chronic symptoms\r\nwhich would at once have attracted my attention. The whole thing is\r\nabsurd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the second suggestion? That Mrs. Inglethorp may have inadvertently\r\ntaken an overdose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree, or even four doses, would not have resulted in death. Mrs.\r\nInglethorp always had an extra large amount of medicine made up at a\r\ntime, as she dealt with Coot\xe2\x80\x99s, the Cash Chemists in Tadminster. She\r\nwould have had to take very nearly the whole bottle to account for the\r\namount of strychnine found at the post-mortem.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you consider that we may dismiss the tonic as not being in any\r\nway instrumental in causing her death?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. The supposition is ridiculous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe same juryman who had interrupted before here suggested that the\r\nchemist who made up the medicine might have committed an error.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat, of course, is always possible,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the doctor.\r\n\r\nBut Dorcas, who was the next witness called, dispelled even that\r\npossibility. The medicine had not been newly made up. On the contrary,\r\nMrs. Inglethorp had taken the last dose on the day of her death.\r\n\r\nSo the question of the tonic was finally abandoned, and the Coroner\r\nproceeded with his task. Having elicited from Dorcas how she had been\r\nawakened by the violent ringing of her mistress\xe2\x80\x99s bell, and had\r\nsubsequently roused the household, he passed to the subject of the\r\nquarrel on the preceding afternoon.\r\n\r\nDorcas\xe2\x80\x99s evidence on this point was substantially what Poirot and I had\r\nalready heard, so I will not repeat it here.\r\n\r\nThe next witness was Mary Cavendish. She stood very upright, and spoke\r\nin a low, clear, and perfectly composed voice. In answer to the\r\nCoroner\xe2\x80\x99s question, she told how, her alarm clock having aroused her at\r\nfour-thirty as usual, she was dressing, when she was startled by the\r\nsound of something heavy falling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would have been the table by the bed?\xe2\x80\x9d commented the Coroner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI opened my door,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cand listened. In a few minutes a\r\nbell rang violently. Dorcas came running down and woke my husband, and\r\nwe all went to my mother-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s room, but it was locked\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner interrupted her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really do not think we need trouble you further on that point. We\r\nknow all that can be known of the subsequent happenings. But I should\r\nbe obliged if you would tell us all you overheard of the quarrel the\r\nday before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a faint insolence in her voice. She raised her hand and\r\nadjusted the ruffle of lace at her neck, turning her head a little as\r\nshe did so. And quite spontaneously the thought flashed across my mind:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is gaining time!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I understand,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the Coroner deliberately, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you were\r\nsitting reading on the bench just outside the long window of the\r\nboudoir. That is so, is it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was news to me and glancing sideways at Poirot, I fancied that it\r\nwas news to him as well.\r\n\r\nThere was the faintest pause, the mere hesitation of a moment, before\r\nshe answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the boudoir window was open, was it not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSurely her face grew a little paler as she answered:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you cannot have failed to hear the voices inside, especially as\r\nthey were raised in anger. In fact, they would be more audible where\r\nyou were than in the hall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you repeat to us what you overheard of the quarrel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really do not remember hearing anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say you did not hear voices?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I heard the voices, but I did not hear what they said.\xe2\x80\x9d A\r\nfaint spot of colour came into her cheek. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not in the habit of\r\nlistening to private conversations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner persisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you remember nothing at all? _Nothing_, Mrs. Cavendish? Not one\r\nstray word or phrase to make you realize that it _was_ a private\r\nconversation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe paused, and seemed to reflect, still outwardly as calm as ever.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I remember. Mrs. Inglethorp said something\xe2\x80\x94I do not remember\r\nexactly what\xe2\x80\x94about causing scandal between husband and wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d the Coroner leant back satisfied. \xe2\x80\x9cThat corresponds with what\r\nDorcas heard. But excuse me, Mrs. Cavendish, although you realized it\r\nwas a private conversation, you did not move away? You remained where\r\nyou were?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI caught the momentary gleam of her tawny eyes as she raised them. I\r\nfelt certain that at that moment she would willingly have torn the\r\nlittle lawyer, with his insinuations, into pieces, but she replied\r\nquietly enough:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I was very comfortable where I was. I fixed my mind on my book.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is all you can tell us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe examination was over, though I doubted if the Coroner was entirely\r\nsatisfied with it. I think he suspected that Mary Cavendish could tell\r\nmore if she chose.\r\n\r\nAmy Hill, shop assistant, was next called, and deposed to having sold a\r\nwill form on the afternoon of the 17th to William Earl, under-gardener\r\nat Styles.\r\n\r\nWilliam Earl and Manning succeeded her, and testified to witnessing a\r\ndocument. Manning fixed the time at about four-thirty, William was of\r\nthe opinion that it was rather earlier.\r\n\r\nCynthia Murdoch came next. She had, however, little to tell. She had\r\nknown nothing of the tragedy, until awakened by Mrs. Cavendish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did not hear the table fall?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I was fast asleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA good conscience makes a sound sleeper,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you,\r\nMiss Murdoch, that is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard produced the letter written to her by Mrs. Inglethorp on\r\nthe evening of the 17th. Poirot and I had, of course already seen it.\r\nIt added nothing to our knowledge of the tragedy. The following is a\r\nfacsimile:\r\n\r\nSTYLES COURT\r\nESSEX\r\nhand written note:\r\n\r\n     July 17th\r\n     My dear Evelyn\r\n     Can we not bury\r\n     the hachet? I have\r\n     found it hard to forgive\r\n     the things you said\r\n     against my dear husband\r\n     but I am an old woman\r\n     & very fond of you\r\n     Yours affectionately,\r\n    Emily Inglethorpe\r\n\r\n\r\nIt was handed to the jury who scrutinized it attentively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear it does not help us much,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Coroner, with a sigh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no mention of any of the events of that afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlain as a pikestaff to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Howard shortly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt shows\r\nclearly enough that my poor old friend had just found out she\xe2\x80\x99d been\r\nmade a fool of!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt says nothing of the kind in the letter,\xe2\x80\x9d the Coroner pointed out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, because Emily never could bear to put herself in the wrong. But\r\n_I_ know her. She wanted me back. But she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t going to own that I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\nbeen right. She went round about. Most people do. Don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in it\r\nmyself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wells smiled faintly. So, I noticed, did several of the jury. Miss\r\nHoward was obviously quite a public character.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnyway, all this tomfoolery is a great waste of time,\xe2\x80\x9d continued the\r\nlady, glancing up and down the jury disparagingly. \xe2\x80\x9cTalk\xe2\x80\x94talk\xe2\x80\x94talk!\r\nWhen all the time we know perfectly well\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner interrupted her in an agony of apprehension:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you, Miss Howard, that is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI fancy he breathed a sigh of relief when she complied.\r\n\r\nThen came the sensation of the day. The Coroner called Albert Mace,\r\nchemist\xe2\x80\x99s assistant.\r\n\r\nIt was our agitated young man of the pale face. In answer to the\r\nCoroner\xe2\x80\x99s questions, he explained that he was a qualified pharmacist,\r\nbut had only recently come to this particular shop, as the assistant\r\nformerly there had just been called up for the army.\r\n\r\nThese preliminaries completed, the Coroner proceeded to business.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Mace, have you lately sold strychnine to any unauthorized person?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen was this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLast Monday night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMonday? Not Tuesday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, Monday, the 16th.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell us to whom you sold it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYou could have heard a pin drop.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir. It was to Mr. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvery eye turned simultaneously to where Alfred Inglethorp was sitting,\r\nimpassive and wooden. He started slightly, as the damning words fell\r\nfrom the young man\xe2\x80\x99s lips. I half thought he was going to rise from his\r\nchair, but he remained seated, although a remarkably well acted\r\nexpression of astonishment rose on his face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are sure of what you say?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Coroner sternly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite sure, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you in the habit of selling strychnine indiscriminately over the\r\ncounter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe wretched young man wilted visibly under the Coroner\xe2\x80\x99s frown.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, sir\xe2\x80\x94of course not. But, seeing it was Mr. Inglethorp of the\r\nHall, I thought there was no harm in it. He said it was to poison a\r\ndog.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInwardly I sympathized. It was only human nature to endeavour to please\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Hall\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94especially when it might result in custom being transferred\r\nfrom Coot\xe2\x80\x99s to the local establishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it not customary for anyone purchasing poison to sign a book?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, Mr. Inglethorp did so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you got the book here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was produced; and, with a few words of stern censure, the Coroner\r\ndismissed the wretched Mr. Mace.\r\n\r\nThen, amidst a breathless silence, Alfred Inglethorp was called. Did he\r\nrealize, I wondered, how closely the halter was being drawn around his\r\nneck?\r\n\r\nThe Coroner went straight to the point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn Monday evening last, did you purchase strychnine for the purpose of\r\npoisoning a dog?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInglethorp replied with perfect calmness:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I did not. There is no dog at Styles, except an outdoor sheepdog,\r\nwhich is in perfect health.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou deny absolutely having purchased strychnine from Albert Mace on\r\nMonday last?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you also deny _this_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner handed him the register in which his signature was\r\ninscribed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I do. The hand-writing is quite different from mine. I will\r\nshow you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took an old envelope out of his pocket, and wrote his name on it,\r\nhanding it to the jury. It was certainly utterly dissimilar.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen what is your explanation of Mr. Mace\xe2\x80\x99s statement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlfred Inglethorp replied imperturbably:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Mace must have been mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner hesitated for a moment, and then said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp, as a mere matter of form, would you mind telling us\r\nwhere you were on the evening of Monday, July 16th?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally\xe2\x80\x94I cannot remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is absurd, Mr. Inglethorp,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Coroner sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cThink\r\nagain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInglethorp shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI cannot tell you. I have an idea that I was out walking.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn what direction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really can\xe2\x80\x99t remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner\xe2\x80\x99s face grew graver.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWere you in company with anyone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you meet anyone on your walk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a pity,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Coroner dryly. \xe2\x80\x9cI am to take it then that\r\nyou decline to say where you were at the time that Mr. Mace positively\r\nrecognized you as entering the shop to purchase strychnine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you like to take it that way, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe careful, Mr. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot was fidgeting nervously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Sacr\xc3\xa9!_\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cDoes this imbecile of a man _want_ to be\r\narrested?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInglethorp was indeed creating a bad impression. His futile denials\r\nwould not have convinced a child. The Coroner, however, passed briskly\r\nto the next point, and Poirot drew a deep breath of relief.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had a discussion with your wife on Tuesday afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Alfred Inglethorp, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have been misinformed.\r\nI had no quarrel with my dear wife. The whole story is absolutely\r\nuntrue. I was absent from the house the entire afternoon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you anyone who can testify to that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have my word,\xe2\x80\x9d said Inglethorp haughtily.\r\n\r\nThe Coroner did not trouble to reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two witnesses who will swear to having heard your\r\ndisagreement with Mrs. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThose witnesses were mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was puzzled. The man spoke with such quiet assurance that I was\r\nstaggered. I looked at Poirot. There was an expression of exultation on\r\nhis face which I could not understand. Was he at last convinced of\r\nAlfred Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s guilt?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Coroner, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have heard your wife\xe2\x80\x99s dying\r\nwords repeated here. Can you explain them in any way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me very simple. The room was dimly lighted. Dr. Bauerstein\r\nis much of my height and build, and, like me, wears a beard. In the dim\r\nlight, and suffering as she was, my poor wife mistook him for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Poirot to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it is an idea, that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think it is true?\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not say that. But it is truly an ingenious supposition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou read my wife\xe2\x80\x99s last words as an accusation\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Inglethorp was\r\ncontinuing\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthey were, on the contrary, an appeal to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Coroner reflected a moment, then he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe, Mr. Inglethorp, that you yourself poured out the coffee,\r\nand took it to your wife that evening?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI poured it out, yes. But I did not take it to her. I meant to do so,\r\nbut I was told that a friend was at the hall door, so I laid down the\r\ncoffee on the hall table. When I came through the hall again a few\r\nminutes later, it was gone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis statement might, or might not, be true, but it did not seem to me\r\nto improve matters much for Inglethorp. In any case, he had had ample\r\ntime to introduce the poison.\r\n\r\nAt that point, Poirot nudged me gently, indicating two men who were\r\nsitting together near the door. One was a little, sharp, dark,\r\nferret-faced man, the other was tall and fair.\r\n\r\nI questioned Poirot mutely. He put his lips to my ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you know who that little man is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is Detective Inspector James Japp of Scotland Yard\xe2\x80\x94Jimmy Japp.\r\nThe other man is from Scotland Yard too. Things are moving quickly, my\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI stared at the two men intently. There was certainly nothing of the\r\npoliceman about them. I should never have suspected them of being\r\nofficial personages.\r\n\r\nI was still staring, when I was startled and recalled by the verdict\r\nbeing given:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilful Murder against some person or persons unknown.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII. POIROT PAYS HIS DEBTS\r\n\r\nAs we came out of the Stylites Arms, Poirot drew me aside by a gentle\r\npressure of the arm. I understood his object. He was waiting for the\r\nScotland Yard men.\r\n\r\nIn a few moments, they emerged, and Poirot at once stepped forward, and\r\naccosted the shorter of the two.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fear you do not remember me, Inspector Japp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t Mr. Poirot!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Inspector. He turned to the\r\nother man. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve heard me speak of Mr. Poirot? It was in 1904 he and\r\nI worked together\xe2\x80\x94the Abercrombie forgery case\xe2\x80\x94you remember, he was run\r\ndown in Brussels. Ah, those were great days, moosier. Then, do you\r\nremember \xe2\x80\x98Baron\xe2\x80\x99 Altara? There was a pretty rogue for you! He eluded\r\nthe clutches of half the police in Europe. But we nailed him in\r\nAntwerp\xe2\x80\x94thanks to Mr. Poirot here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs these friendly reminiscences were being indulged in, I drew nearer,\r\nand was introduced to Detective-Inspector Japp, who, in his turn,\r\nintroduced us both to his companion, Superintendent Summerhaye.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI need hardly ask what you are doing here, gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked\r\nPoirot.\r\n\r\nJapp closed one eye knowingly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed. Pretty clear case I should say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Poirot answered gravely:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere I differ from you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come!\xe2\x80\x9d said Summerhaye, opening his lips for the first time.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely the whole thing is clear as daylight. The man\xe2\x80\x99s caught\r\nred-handed. How he could be such a fool beats me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Japp was looking attentively at Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold your fire, Summerhaye,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked jocularly. \xe2\x80\x9cMe and Moosier\r\nhere have met before\xe2\x80\x94and there\xe2\x80\x99s no man\xe2\x80\x99s judgment I\xe2\x80\x99d sooner take than\r\nhis. If I\xe2\x80\x99m not greatly mistaken, he\xe2\x80\x99s got something up his sleeve.\r\nIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that so, moosier?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot smiled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have drawn certain conclusions\xe2\x80\x94yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSummerhaye was still looking rather sceptical, but Japp continued his\r\nscrutiny of Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s this way,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cso far, we\xe2\x80\x99ve only seen the case from the\r\noutside. That\xe2\x80\x99s where the Yard\xe2\x80\x99s at a disadvantage in a case of this\r\nkind, where the murder\xe2\x80\x99s only out, so to speak, after the inquest. A\r\nlot depends on being on the spot first thing, and that\xe2\x80\x99s where Mr.\r\nPoirot\xe2\x80\x99s had the start of us. We shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have been here as soon as\r\nthis even, if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for the fact that there was a smart doctor\r\non the spot, who gave us the tip through the Coroner. But you\xe2\x80\x99ve been\r\non the spot from the first, and you may have picked up some little\r\nhints. From the evidence at the inquest, Mr. Inglethorp murdered his\r\nwife as sure as I stand here, and if anyone but you hinted the contrary\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99d laugh in his face. I must say I was surprised the jury didn\xe2\x80\x99t bring\r\nit in Wilful Murder against him right off. I think they would have, if\r\nit hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for the Coroner\xe2\x80\x94he seemed to be holding them back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, though, you have a warrant for his arrest in your pocket\r\nnow,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Poirot.\r\n\r\nA kind of wooden shutter of officialdom came down from Japp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexpressive countenance.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps I have, and perhaps I haven\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked dryly.\r\n\r\nPoirot looked at him thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very anxious, Messieurs, that he should not be arrested.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI dare say,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Summerhaye sarcastically.\r\n\r\nJapp was regarding Poirot with comical perplexity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you go a little further, Mr. Poirot? A wink\xe2\x80\x99s as good as a\r\nnod\xe2\x80\x94from you. You\xe2\x80\x99ve been on the spot\xe2\x80\x94and the Yard doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want to make\r\nany mistakes, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is exactly what I thought. Well, I will tell you this. Use your\r\nwarrant: Arrest Mr. Inglethorp. But it will bring you no kudos\xe2\x80\x94the case\r\nagainst him will be dismissed at once! _Comme \xc3\xa7a!_\xe2\x80\x9d And he snapped his\r\nfingers expressively.\r\n\r\nJapp\xe2\x80\x99s face grew grave, though Summerhaye gave an incredulous snort.\r\n\r\nAs for me, I was literally dumb with astonishment. I could only\r\nconclude that Poirot was mad.\r\n\r\nJapp had taken out a handkerchief, and was gently dabbing his brow.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI daren\xe2\x80\x99t do it, Mr. Poirot. _I_\xe2\x80\x99d take your word, but there\xe2\x80\x99s others\r\nover me who\xe2\x80\x99ll be asking what the devil I mean by it. Can\xe2\x80\x99t you give me\r\na little more to go on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot reflected a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt can be done,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last. \xe2\x80\x9cI admit I do not wish it. It forces\r\nmy hand. I would have preferred to work in the dark just for the\r\npresent, but what you say is very just\xe2\x80\x94the word of a Belgian policeman,\r\nwhose day is past, is not enough! And Alfred Inglethorp must not be\r\narrested. That I have sworn, as my friend Hastings here knows. See,\r\nthen, my good Japp, you go at once to Styles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, in about half an hour. We\xe2\x80\x99re seeing the Coroner and the doctor\r\nfirst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood. Call for me in passing\xe2\x80\x94the last house in the village. I will go\r\nwith you. At Styles, Mr. Inglethorp will give you, or if he refuses\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nis probable\xe2\x80\x94I will give you such proofs that shall satisfy you that the\r\ncase against him could not possibly be sustained. Is that a bargain?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a bargain,\xe2\x80\x9d said Japp heartily. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd, on behalf of the Yard,\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m much obliged to you, though I\xe2\x80\x99m bound to confess I can\xe2\x80\x99t at present\r\nsee the faintest possible loop-hole in the evidence, but you always\r\nwere a marvel! So long, then, moosier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two detectives strode away, Summerhaye with an incredulous grin on\r\nhis face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot, before I could get in a word, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat do\r\nyou think? _Mon Dieu!_ I had some warm moments in that court; I did not\r\nfigure to myself that the man would be so pig-headed as to refuse to\r\nsay anything at all. Decidedly, it was the policy of an imbecile.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cH\xe2\x80\x99m! There are other explanations besides that of imbecility,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nremarked. \xe2\x80\x9cFor, if the case against him is true, how could he defend\r\nhimself except by silence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, in a thousand ingenious ways,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cSee; say that it is\r\nI who have committed this murder, I can think of seven most plausible\r\nstories! Far more convincing than Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s stony denials!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could not help laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Poirot, I am sure you are capable of thinking of seventy! But,\r\nseriously, in spite of what I heard you say to the detectives, you\r\nsurely cannot still believe in the possibility of Alfred Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninnocence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not now as much as before? Nothing has changed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the evidence is so conclusive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, too conclusive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe turned in at the gate of Leastways Cottage, and proceeded up the now\r\nfamiliar stairs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes, too conclusive,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Poirot, almost to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cReal\r\nevidence is usually vague and unsatisfactory. It has to be\r\nexamined\xe2\x80\x94sifted. But here the whole thing is cut and dried. No, my\r\nfriend, this evidence has been very cleverly manufactured\xe2\x80\x94so cleverly\r\nthat it has defeated its own ends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you make that out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, so long as the evidence against him was vague and intangible,\r\nit was very hard to disprove. But, in his anxiety, the criminal has\r\ndrawn the net so closely that one cut will set Inglethorp free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was silent. And in a minute or two, Poirot continued:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us look at the matter like this. Here is a man, let us say, who\r\nsets out to poison his wife. He has lived by his wits as the saying\r\ngoes. Presumably, therefore, he has some wits. He is not altogether a\r\nfool. Well, how does he set about it? He goes boldly to the village\r\nchemist\xe2\x80\x99s and purchases strychnine under his own name, with a trumped\r\nup story about a dog which is bound to be proved absurd. He does not\r\nemploy the poison that night. No, he waits until he has had a violent\r\nquarrel with her, of which the whole household is cognisant, and which\r\nnaturally directs their suspicions upon him. He prepares no defence\xe2\x80\x94no\r\nshadow of an alibi, yet he knows the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s assistant must\r\nnecessarily come forward with the facts. Bah! Do not ask me to believe\r\nthat any man could be so idiotic! Only a lunatic, who wished to commit\r\nsuicide by causing himself to be hanged, would act so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill\xe2\x80\x94I do not see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither do I see. I tell you, _mon ami_, it puzzles me. _Me_\xe2\x80\x94Hercule\r\nPoirot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut if you believe him innocent, how do you explain his buying the\r\nstrychnine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery simply. He did _not_ buy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Mace recognized him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg your pardon, he saw a man with a black beard like Mr.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s, and wearing glasses like Mr. Inglethorp, and dressed in\r\nMr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s rather noticeable clothes. He could not recognize a\r\nman whom he had probably only seen in the distance, since, you\r\nremember, he himself had only been in the village a fortnight, and Mrs.\r\nInglethorp dealt principally with Coot\xe2\x80\x99s in Tadminster.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you think\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon ami_, do you remember the two points I laid stress upon? Leave\r\nthe first one for the moment, what was the second?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe important fact that Alfred Inglethorp wears peculiar clothes, has\r\na black beard, and uses glasses,\xe2\x80\x9d I quoted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. Now suppose anyone wished to pass himself off as John or\r\nLawrence Cavendish. Would it be easy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I said thoughtfully. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course an actor\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Poirot cut me short ruthlessly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why would it not be easy? I will tell you, my friend: Because they\r\nare both clean-shaven men. To make up successfully as one of these two\r\nin broad daylight, it would need an actor of genius, and a certain\r\ninitial facial resemblance. But in the case of Alfred Inglethorp, all\r\nthat is changed. His clothes, his beard, the glasses which hide his\r\neyes\xe2\x80\x94those are the salient points about his personal appearance. Now,\r\nwhat is the first instinct of the criminal? To divert suspicion from\r\nhimself, is it not so? And how can he best do that? By throwing it on\r\nsomeone else. In this instance, there was a man ready to his hand.\r\nEverybody was predisposed to believe in Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s guilt. It was\r\na foregone conclusion that he would be suspected; but, to make it a\r\nsure thing there must be tangible proof\xe2\x80\x94such as the actual buying of\r\nthe poison, and that, with a man of the peculiar appearance of Mr.\r\nInglethorp, was not difficult. Remember, this young Mace had never\r\nactually spoken to Mr. Inglethorp. How should he doubt that the man in\r\nhis clothes, with his beard and his glasses, was not Alfred\r\nInglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt may be so,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, fascinated by Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s eloquence. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, if that\r\nwas the case, why does he not say where he was at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock on Monday\r\nevening?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, why indeed?\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, calming down. \xe2\x80\x9cIf he were arrested, he\r\nprobably would speak, but I do not want it to come to that. I must make\r\nhim see the gravity of his position. There is, of course, something\r\ndiscreditable behind his silence. If he did not murder his wife, he is,\r\nnevertheless, a scoundrel, and has something of his own to conceal,\r\nquite apart from the murder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can it be?\xe2\x80\x9d I mused, won over to Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s views for the moment,\r\nalthough still retaining a faint conviction that the obvious deduction\r\nwas the correct one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCan you not guess?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Poirot, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, can you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I had a little idea sometime ago\xe2\x80\x94and it has turned out to be\r\ncorrect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou never told me,\xe2\x80\x9d I said reproachfully.\r\n\r\nPoirot spread out his hands apologetically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, _mon ami_, you were not precisely _sympathique_.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned\r\nto me earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cTell me\xe2\x80\x94you see now that he must not be arrested?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d I said doubtfully, for I was really quite indifferent to the\r\nfate of Alfred Inglethorp, and thought that a good fright would do him\r\nno harm.\r\n\r\nPoirot, who was watching me intently, gave a sigh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, changing the subject, \xe2\x80\x9capart from Mr.\r\nInglethorp, how did the evidence at the inquest strike you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, pretty much what I expected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid nothing strike you as peculiar about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy thoughts flew to Mary Cavendish, and I hedged:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn what way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Lawrence Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s evidence for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was relieved.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Lawrence! No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so. He\xe2\x80\x99s always a nervous chap.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis suggestion that his mother might have been poisoned accidentally\r\nby means of the tonic she was taking, that did not strike you as\r\nstrange\xe2\x80\x94 _hein?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say it did. The doctors ridiculed it of course. But it was\r\nquite a natural suggestion for a layman to make.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Monsieur Lawrence is not a layman. You told me yourself that he\r\nhad started by studying medicine, and that he had taken his degree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s true. I never thought of that.\xe2\x80\x9d I was rather startled. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\n_is_ odd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the first, his behaviour has been peculiar. Of all the household,\r\nhe alone would be likely to recognize the symptoms of strychnine\r\npoisoning, and yet we find him the only member of the family to uphold\r\nstrenuously the theory of death from natural causes. If it had been\r\nMonsieur John, I could have understood it. He has no technical\r\nknowledge, and is by nature unimaginative. But Monsieur Lawrence\xe2\x80\x94no!\r\nAnd now, to-day, he puts forward a suggestion that he himself must have\r\nknown was ridiculous. There is food for thought in this, _mon ami!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very confusing,\xe2\x80\x9d I agreed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen there is Mrs. Cavendish,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s another who\r\nis not telling all she knows! What do you make of her attitude?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to make of it. It seems inconceivable that she\r\nshould be shielding Alfred Inglethorp. Yet that is what it looks like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded reflectively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is queer. One thing is certain, she overheard a good deal more\r\nof that \xe2\x80\x98private conversation\xe2\x80\x99 than she was willing to admit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet she is the last person one would accuse of stooping to\r\neavesdrop!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. One thing her evidence _has_ shown me. I made a mistake.\r\nDorcas was quite right. The quarrel did take place earlier in the\r\nafternoon, about four o\xe2\x80\x99clock, as she said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at him curiously. I had never understood his insistence on\r\nthat point.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, a good deal that was peculiar came out to-day,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Poirot.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDr. Bauerstein, now, what was _he_ doing up and dressed at that hour\r\nin the morning? It is astonishing to me that no one commented on the\r\nfact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has insomnia, I believe,\xe2\x80\x9d I said doubtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is a very good, or a very bad explanation,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\ncovers everything, and explains nothing. I shall keep my eye on our\r\nclever Dr. Bauerstein.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAny more faults to find with the evidence?\xe2\x80\x9d I inquired satirically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mon ami_,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you find that people are not\r\ntelling you the truth\xe2\x80\x94look out! Now, unless I am much mistaken, at the\r\ninquest to-day only one\xe2\x80\x94at most, two persons were speaking the truth\r\nwithout reservation or subterfuge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come now, Poirot! I won\xe2\x80\x99t cite Lawrence, or Mrs. Cavendish. But\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x99s John\xe2\x80\x94and Miss Howard, surely they were speaking the truth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoth of them, my friend? One, I grant you, but both\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis words gave me an unpleasant shock. Miss Howard\xe2\x80\x99s evidence,\r\nunimportant as it was, had been given in such a downright\r\nstraightforward manner that it had never occurred to me to doubt her\r\nsincerity. Still, I had a great respect for Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s sagacity\xe2\x80\x94except on\r\nthe occasions when he was what I described to myself as \xe2\x80\x9cfoolishly\r\npig-headed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think so?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard had always seemed to me\r\nso essentially honest\xe2\x80\x94almost uncomfortably so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot gave me a curious look, which I could not quite fathom. He\r\nseemed to speak, and then checked himself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Murdoch too,\xe2\x80\x9d I continued, \xe2\x80\x9cthere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing untruthful about\r\n_her_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. But it was strange that she never heard a sound, sleeping next\r\ndoor; whereas Mrs. Cavendish, in the other wing of the building,\r\ndistinctly heard the table fall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, she\xe2\x80\x99s young. And she sleeps soundly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes, indeed! She must be a famous sleeper, that one!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did not quite like the tone of his voice, but at that moment a smart\r\nknock reached our ears, and looking out of the window we perceived the\r\ntwo detectives waiting for us below.\r\n\r\nPoirot seized his hat, gave a ferocious twist to his moustache, and,\r\ncarefully brushing an imaginary speck of dust from his sleeve, motioned\r\nme to precede him down the stairs; there we joined the detectives and\r\nset out for Styles.\r\n\r\nI think the appearance of the two Scotland Yard men was rather a\r\nshock\xe2\x80\x94especially to John, though of course after the verdict, he had\r\nrealized that it was only a matter of time. Still, the presence of the\r\ndetectives brought the truth home to him more than anything else could\r\nhave done.\r\n\r\nPoirot had conferred with Japp in a low tone on the way up, and it was\r\nthe latter functionary who requested that the household, with the\r\nexception of the servants, should be assembled together in the\r\ndrawing-room. I realized the significance of this. It was up to Poirot\r\nto make his boast good.\r\n\r\nPersonally, I was not sanguine. Poirot might have excellent reasons for\r\nhis belief in Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s innocence, but a man of the type of\r\nSummerhaye would require tangible proofs, and these I doubted if Poirot\r\ncould supply.\r\n\r\nBefore very long we had all trooped into the drawing-room, the door of\r\nwhich Japp closed. Poirot politely set chairs for everyone. The\r\nScotland Yard men were the cynosure of all eyes. I think that for the\r\nfirst time we realized that the thing was not a bad dream, but a\r\ntangible reality. We had read of such things\xe2\x80\x94now we ourselves were\r\nactors in the drama. To-morrow the daily papers, all over England,\r\nwould blazon out the news in staring headlines:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMYSTERIOUS TRAGEDY IN ESSEX\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9cWEALTHY LADY POISONED\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\nThere would be pictures of Styles, snap-shots of \xe2\x80\x9cThe family leaving\r\nthe Inquest\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94the village photographer had not been idle! All the things\r\nthat one had read a hundred times\xe2\x80\x94things that happen to other people,\r\nnot to oneself. And now, in this house, a murder had been committed. In\r\nfront of us were \xe2\x80\x9cthe detectives in charge of the case.\xe2\x80\x9d The well-known\r\nglib phraseology passed rapidly through my mind in the interval before\r\nPoirot opened the proceedings.\r\n\r\nI think everyone was a little surprised that it should be he and not\r\none of the official detectives who took the initiative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mesdames_ and _messieurs_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, bowing as though he were a\r\ncelebrity about to deliver a lecture, \xe2\x80\x9cI have asked you to come here\r\nall together, for a certain object. That object, it concerns Mr. Alfred\r\nInglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInglethorp was sitting a little by himself\xe2\x80\x94I think, unconsciously,\r\neveryone had drawn his chair slightly away from him\xe2\x80\x94and he gave a faint\r\nstart as Poirot pronounced his name.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, addressing him directly, \xe2\x80\x9ca very dark\r\nshadow is resting on this house\xe2\x80\x94the shadow of murder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInglethorp shook his head sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor wife,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Emily! It is terrible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think, monsieur,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot pointedly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you quite\r\nrealize how terrible it may be\xe2\x80\x94for you.\xe2\x80\x9d And as Inglethorp did not\r\nappear to understand, he added: \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp, you are standing in\r\nvery grave danger.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe two detectives fidgeted. I saw the official caution \xe2\x80\x9cAnything you\r\nsay will be used in evidence against you,\xe2\x80\x9d actually hovering on\r\nSummerhaye\xe2\x80\x99s lips. Poirot went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you understand now, monsieur?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. What do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot deliberately, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are suspected of\r\npoisoning your wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA little gasp ran round the circle at this plain speaking.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Inglethorp, starting up. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a monstrous idea!\r\n_I_\xe2\x80\x94poison my dearest Emily!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Poirot watched him narrowly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat you quite realize\r\nthe unfavourable nature of your evidence at the inquest. Mr.\r\nInglethorp, knowing what I have now told you, do you still refuse to\r\nsay where you were at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock on Monday afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a groan, Alfred Inglethorp sank down again and buried his face in\r\nhis hands. Poirot approached and stood over him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried menacingly.\r\n\r\nWith an effort, Inglethorp raised his face from his hands. Then, slowly\r\nand deliberately, he shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not speak?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I do not believe that anyone could be so monstrous as to accuse me\r\nof what you say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded thoughtfully, like a man whose mind is made up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Soit!_\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cThen I must speak for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAlfred Inglethorp sprang up again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou? How can you speak? You do not know\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he broke off abruptly.\r\n\r\nPoirot turned to face us. \xe2\x80\x9c_Mesdames_ and _messieurs_! I speak! Listen!\r\nI, Hercule Poirot, affirm that the man who entered the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop,\r\nand purchased strychnine at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock on Monday last was not Mr.\r\nInglethorp, for at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock on that day Mr. Inglethorp was escorting\r\nMrs. Raikes back to her home from a neighbouring farm. I can produce no\r\nless than five witnesses to swear to having seen them together, either\r\nat six or just after and, as you may know, the Abbey Farm, Mrs.\r\nRaikes\xe2\x80\x99s home, is at least two and a half miles distant from the\r\nvillage. There is absolutely no question as to the alibi!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII. FRESH SUSPICIONS\r\n\r\nThere was a moment\xe2\x80\x99s stupefied silence. Japp, who was the least\r\nsurprised of any of us, was the first to speak.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy word,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re the goods! And no mistake, Mr. Poirot!\r\nThese witnesses of yours are all right, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Voil\xc3\xa0!_ I have prepared a list of them\xe2\x80\x94names and addresses. You must\r\nsee them, of course. But you will find it all right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure of that.\xe2\x80\x9d Japp lowered his voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m much obliged to you. A\r\npretty mare\xe2\x80\x99s nest arresting him would have been.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to\r\nInglethorp. \xe2\x80\x9cBut, if you\xe2\x80\x99ll excuse me, sir, why couldn\xe2\x80\x99t you say all\r\nthis at the inquest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you why,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a certain\r\nrumour\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most malicious and utterly untrue one,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Alfred\r\nInglethorp in an agitated voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd Mr. Inglethorp was anxious to have no scandal revived just at\r\npresent. Am I right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right.\xe2\x80\x9d Inglethorp nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cWith my poor Emily not yet buried,\r\ncan you wonder I was anxious that no more lying rumours should be\r\nstarted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetween you and me, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Japp, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d sooner have any amount\r\nof rumours than be arrested for murder. And I venture to think your\r\npoor lady would have felt the same. And, if it hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been for Mr.\r\nPoirot here, arrested you would have been, as sure as eggs is eggs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was foolish, no doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Inglethorp. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you do not know,\r\ninspector, how I have been persecuted and maligned.\xe2\x80\x9d And he shot a\r\nbaleful glance at Evelyn Howard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Japp, turning briskly to John, \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see\r\nthe lady\xe2\x80\x99s bedroom, please, and after that I\xe2\x80\x99ll have a little chat with\r\nthe servants. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you bother about anything. Mr. Poirot, here, will\r\nshow me the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAs they all went out of the room, Poirot turned and made me a sign to\r\nfollow him upstairs. There he caught me by the arm, and drew me aside.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuick, go to the other wing. Stand there\xe2\x80\x94just this side of the baize\r\ndoor. Do not move till I come.\xe2\x80\x9d Then, turning rapidly, he rejoined the\r\ntwo detectives.\r\n\r\nI followed his instructions, taking up my position by the baize door,\r\nand wondering what on earth lay behind the request. Why was I to stand\r\nin this particular spot on guard? I looked thoughtfully down the\r\ncorridor in front of me. An idea struck me. With the exception of\r\nCynthia Murdoch\xe2\x80\x99s, everyone\xe2\x80\x99s room was in this left wing. Had that\r\nanything to do with it? Was I to report who came or went? I stood\r\nfaithfully at my post. The minutes passed. Nobody came. Nothing\r\nhappened.\r\n\r\nIt must have been quite twenty minutes before Poirot rejoined me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have not stirred?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ve stuck here like a rock. Nothing\xe2\x80\x99s happened.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Was he pleased, or disappointed? \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve seen nothing at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have probably heard something? A big bump\xe2\x80\x94eh, _mon ami?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible? Ah, but I am vexed with myself! I am not usually\r\nclumsy. I made but a slight gesture\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94I know Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s gestures\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cwith the\r\nleft hand, and over went the table by the bed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked so childishly vexed and crest-fallen that I hastened to\r\nconsole him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind, old chap. What does it matter? Your triumph downstairs\r\nexcited you. I can tell you, that was a surprise to us all. There must\r\nbe more in this affair of Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s with Mrs. Raikes than we\r\nthought, to make him hold his tongue so persistently. What are you\r\ngoing to do now? Where are the Scotland Yard fellows?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone down to interview the servants. I showed them all our exhibits. I\r\nam disappointed in Japp. He has no method!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo!\xe2\x80\x9d I said, looking out of the window. \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s Dr. Bauerstein. I\r\nbelieve you\xe2\x80\x99re right about that man, Poirot. I don\xe2\x80\x99t like him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is clever,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Poirot meditatively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, clever as the devil! I must say I was overjoyed to see him in the\r\nplight he was in on Tuesday. You never saw such a spectacle!\xe2\x80\x9d And I\r\ndescribed the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s adventure. \xe2\x80\x9cHe looked a regular scarecrow!\r\nPlastered with mud from head to foot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw him, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Of course, he didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to come in\xe2\x80\x94it was just after dinner\xe2\x80\x94but\r\nMr. Inglethorp insisted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot caught me violently by the shoulders. \xe2\x80\x9cWas Dr.\r\nBauerstein here on Tuesday evening? Here? And you never told me? Why\r\ndid you not tell me? Why? Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe appeared to be in an absolute frenzy.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I expostulated, \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought it would interest\r\nyou. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it was of any importance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImportance? It is of the first importance! So Dr. Bauerstein was here\r\non Tuesday night\xe2\x80\x94the night of the murder. Hastings, do you not see?\r\nThat alters everything\xe2\x80\x94everything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had never seen him so upset. Loosening his hold of me, he\r\nmechanically straightened a pair of candlesticks, still murmuring to\r\nhimself: \xe2\x80\x9cYes, that alters everything\xe2\x80\x94everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly he seemed to come to a decision.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Allons!_\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must act at once. Where is Mr. Cavendish?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn was in the smoking-room. Poirot went straight to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Cavendish, I have some important business in Tadminster. A new\r\nclue. May I take your motor?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, of course. Do you mean at once?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn rang the bell, and ordered round the car. In another ten minutes,\r\nwe were racing down the park and along the high road to Tadminster.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked resignedly, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps you will tell me what\r\nall this is about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, _mon ami_, a good deal you can guess for yourself. Of course you\r\nrealize that, now Mr. Inglethorp is out of it, the whole position is\r\ngreatly changed. We are face to face with an entirely new problem. We\r\nknow now that there is one person who did not buy the poison. We have\r\ncleared away the manufactured clues. Now for the real ones. I have\r\nascertained that anyone in the household, with the exception of Mrs.\r\nCavendish, who was playing tennis with you, could have personated Mr.\r\nInglethorp on Monday evening. In the same way, we have his statement\r\nthat he put the coffee down in the hall. No one took much notice of\r\nthat at the inquest\xe2\x80\x94but now it has a very different significance. We\r\nmust find out who did take that coffee to Mrs. Inglethorp eventually,\r\nor who passed through the hall whilst it was standing there. From your\r\naccount, there are only two people whom we can positively say did not\r\ngo near the coffee\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Cavendish, and Mademoiselle Cynthia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is so.\xe2\x80\x9d I felt an inexpressible lightening of the heart.\r\nMary Cavendish could certainly not rest under suspicion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn clearing Alfred Inglethorp,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cI have been obliged\r\nto show my hand sooner than I intended. As long as I might be thought\r\nto be pursuing him, the criminal would be off his guard. Now, he will\r\nbe doubly careful. Yes\xe2\x80\x94doubly careful.\xe2\x80\x9d He turned to me abruptly. \xe2\x80\x9cTell\r\nme, Hastings, you yourself\xe2\x80\x94have you no suspicions of anybody?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI hesitated. To tell the truth, an idea, wild and extravagant in\r\nitself, had once or twice that morning flashed through my brain. I had\r\nrejected it as absurd, nevertheless it persisted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou couldn\xe2\x80\x99t call it a suspicion,\xe2\x80\x9d I murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s so utterly\r\nfoolish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome now,\xe2\x80\x9d urged Poirot encouragingly. \xe2\x80\x9cDo not fear. Speak your mind.\r\nYou should always pay attention to your instincts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell then,\xe2\x80\x9d I blurted out, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s absurd\xe2\x80\x94but I suspect Miss Howard of\r\nnot telling all she knows!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ll laugh at me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. But, after all, she was only fifteen\r\nmiles away. A car would do it in half an hour. Can we say positively\r\nthat she was away from Styles on the night of the murder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my friend,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot unexpectedly, \xe2\x80\x9cwe can. One of my first\r\nactions was to ring up the hospital where she was working.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I learnt that Miss Howard had been on afternoon duty on Tuesday,\r\nand that\xe2\x80\x94a convoy coming in unexpectedly\xe2\x80\x94she had kindly offered to\r\nremain on night duty, which offer was gratefully accepted. She might have burnt the new one, mistaking it for the earlier\r\none in his favour. She is sanity itself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet her hatred of Inglethorp seems almost a mania. It is always wise to suspect\r\neverybody until you can prove logically, and to your own satisfaction,\r\nthat they are innocent.
