No, we must look elsewhere. You\r\nare perfectly correct in your assumption that her vehemence against\r\nAlfred Inglethorp is too violent to be natural; but you are quite wrong\r\nin the deduction you draw from it. I have drawn my own deductions,\r\nwhich I believe to be correct, but I will not speak of them at\r\npresent.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused a minute, then went on. \xe2\x80\x9cNow, to my way of\r\nthinking, there is one insuperable objection to Miss Howard\xe2\x80\x99s being the\r\nmurderess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat in no possible way could Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s death benefit Miss\r\nHoward. It was what you said\r\nabout her evidence at the inquest that set me off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot looked puzzled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did I say about her evidence at the inquest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you remember? When I cited her and John Cavendish as being above\r\nsuspicion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94yes.\xe2\x80\x9d He seemed a little confused, but recovered himself. \xe2\x80\x9cBy\r\nthe way, Hastings, there is something I want you to do for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. What is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNext time you happen to be alone with Lawrence Cavendish, I want you\r\nto say this to him. \xe2\x80\x98I have a message for you, from Poirot. He says:\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFind the extra coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace!\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x99 Nothing more.\r\nNothing less.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Find the extra coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace.\xe2\x80\x99 Is that\r\nright?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, much mystified.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcellent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what does it mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that I will leave you to find out. I could see neither rhyme nor reason in it. However, my\r\nconfidence in him, which at one time had rather waned, was fully\r\nrestored since his belief in Alfred Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s innocence had been so\r\ntriumphantly vindicated.\r\n\r\nThe funeral of Mrs. Inglethorp took place the following day, and on\r\nMonday, as I came down to a late breakfast, John drew me aside, and\r\ninformed me that Mr. Inglethorp was leaving that morning, to take up\r\nhis quarters at the Stylites Arms until he should have completed his\r\nplans.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd really it\xe2\x80\x99s a great relief to think he\xe2\x80\x99s going, Hastings,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\ncontinued my honest friend. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was bad enough before, when we thought\r\nhe\xe2\x80\x99d done it, but I\xe2\x80\x99m hanged if it isn\xe2\x80\x99t worse now, when we all feel\r\nguilty for having been so down on the fellow. The fact is, we\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\ntreated him abominably. Of course, things did look black against him. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t see how anyone could blame us for jumping to the conclusions we\r\ndid. Still, there it is, we were in the wrong, and now there\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbeastly feeling that one ought to make amends; which is difficult, when\r\none doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like the fellow a bit better than one did before. The whole\r\nthing\xe2\x80\x99s damned awkward! And I\xe2\x80\x99m thankful he\xe2\x80\x99s had the tact to take\r\nhimself off. It\xe2\x80\x99s a good thing Styles wasn\xe2\x80\x99t the mater\xe2\x80\x99s to leave to\r\nhim. Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t bear to think of the fellow lording it here. He\xe2\x80\x99s welcome\r\nto her money.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll be able to keep up the place all right?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. We shall be pinched at first,\r\nof course, because, as I once told you, I am in a bit of a hole\r\nfinancially myself. Still, the Johnnies will wait now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the general relief at Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s approaching departure, we had the\r\nmost genial breakfast we had experienced since the tragedy. Cynthia,\r\nwhose young spirits were naturally buoyant, was looking quite her\r\npretty self again, and we all, with the exception of Lawrence, who\r\nseemed unalterably gloomy and nervous, were quietly cheerful, at the\r\nopening of a new and hopeful future.\r\n\r\nThe papers, of course, had been full of the tragedy. Glaring headlines,\r\nsandwiched biographies of every member of the household, subtle\r\ninnuendoes, the usual familiar tag about the police having a clue.\r\nNothing was spared us. It was a slack time. The war was momentarily\r\ninactive, and the newspapers seized with avidity on this crime in\r\nfashionable life: \xe2\x80\x9cThe Mysterious Affair at Styles\xe2\x80\x9d was the topic of\r\nthe moment.\r\n\r\nNaturally it was very annoying for the Cavendishes. The house was\r\nconstantly besieged by reporters, who were consistently denied\r\nadmission, but who continued to haunt the village and the grounds,\r\nwhere they lay in wait with cameras, for any unwary members of the\r\nhousehold. We all lived in a blast of publicity. The Scotland Yard men\r\ncame and went, examining, questioning, lynx-eyed and reserved of\r\ntongue. Towards what end they were working, we did not know. Had they\r\nany clue, or would the whole thing remain in the category of\r\nundiscovered crimes?\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast, Dorcas came up to me rather mysteriously, and asked if\r\nshe might have a few words with me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. What is it, Dorcas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s just this, sir. You\xe2\x80\x99ll be seeing the Belgian gentleman\r\nto-day perhaps?\xe2\x80\x9d I nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, you know how he asked me so\r\nparticular if the mistress, or anyone else, had a green dress?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes. But since then I\xe2\x80\x99ve remembered what the young\r\ngentlemen\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94John and Lawrence were still the \xe2\x80\x9cyoung gentlemen\xe2\x80\x9d to\r\nDorcas\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9ccall the \xe2\x80\x98dressing-up box.\xe2\x80\x99 It\xe2\x80\x99s up in the front attic, sir. There\r\nwere one or two green fabrics of varying shades; but Poirot shook his\r\nhead over them all. He seemed somewhat apathetic in the search, as\r\nthough he expected no great results from it. Suddenly he gave an\r\nexclamation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe chest was nearly empty, and there, reposing right at the bottom,\r\nwas a magnificent black beard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Oh\xc3\xb3!_\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9c_Oh\xc3\xb3!_\xe2\x80\x9d He turned it over in his hands,\r\nexamining it closely. \xe2\x80\x9cNew,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, quite new.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a moment\xe2\x80\x99s hesitation, he replaced it in the chest, heaped all\r\nthe other things on top of it as before, and made his way briskly\r\ndownstairs. Are\r\nthey often used, may I ask?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir, not very often nowadays, though from time to time we do\r\nhave what the young gentlemen call \xe2\x80\x98a dress-up night.\xe2\x80\x99 And very funny\r\nit is sometimes, sir. Nobody would have known her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese evenings must have been great fun,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot genially. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsuppose Mr. Lawrence wore that fine black beard in the chest upstairs,\r\nwhen he was Shah of Persia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe did have a beard, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Dorcas, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd well I know\r\nit, for he borrowed two skeins of my black wool to make it with! There was a red wig, I know, but nothing else in the way of\r\nhair. Burnt corks they use mostly\xe2\x80\x94though \xe2\x80\x98tis messy getting it off\r\nagain. Miss Cynthia was a nigger once, and, oh, the trouble she had.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo Dorcas knows nothing about that black beard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot\r\nthoughtfully, as we walked out into the hall again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think it is _the_ one?\xe2\x80\x9d I whispered eagerly.\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do. Hastings, this affair is very deep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho put it in the chest, I wonder?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomeone with a good deal of intelligence,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Poirot dryly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nrealize that he chose the one place in the house to hide it where its\r\npresence would not be remarked? You are known to be working with me.\r\nI want somebody who is not associated with us in any way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see. How about John?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I think not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe dear fellow isn\xe2\x80\x99t perhaps very bright,\xe2\x80\x9d I said thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere comes Miss Howard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is the very\r\nperson. But I am in her black books, since I cleared Mr. Inglethorp.\r\nStill, we can but try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a nod that was barely civil, Miss Howard assented to Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nrequest for a few minutes\xe2\x80\x99 conversation.\r\n\r\nWe went into the little morning-room, and Poirot closed the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, Monsieur Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Howard impatiently, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat is it? Out\r\nwith it. I\xe2\x80\x99m busy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember, mademoiselle, that I once asked you to help me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do.\xe2\x80\x9d The lady nodded. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I told you I\xe2\x80\x99d help you with\r\npleasure\xe2\x80\x94to hang Alfred Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot studied her seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard, I will ask you one\r\nquestion. I beg of you to reply to it truthfully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever tell lies,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Miss Howard.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is this. Do you still believe that Mrs. Inglethorp was poisoned by\r\nher husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked sharply. \xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t think your pretty\r\nexplanations influence me in the slightest. I\xe2\x80\x99ll admit that it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nhe who bought strychnine at the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop. What of that? I dare\r\nsay he soaked fly paper, as I told you at the beginning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is arsenic\xe2\x80\x94not strychnine,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot mildly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat does that matter? Arsenic would put poor Emily out of the way\r\njust as well as strychnine. If I\xe2\x80\x99m convinced he did it, it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmatter a jot to me _how_ he did it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. _If_ you are convinced he did it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwill put my question in another form. Did you ever in your heart of\r\nhearts believe that Mrs. Inglethorp was poisoned by her husband?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Miss Howard. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t I always told you the man\r\nis a villain? Haven\xe2\x80\x99t I always told you he would murder her in her bed?\r\nHaven\xe2\x80\x99t I always hated him like poison?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cThat bears out my little idea entirely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat little idea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard, do you remember a conversation that took place on the day\r\nof my friend\xe2\x80\x99s arrival here? He repeated it to me, and there is a\r\nsentence of yours that has impressed me very much. Do you remember\r\naffirming that if a crime had been committed, and anyone you loved had\r\nbeen murdered, you felt certain that you would know by instinct who the\r\ncriminal was, even if you were quite unable to prove it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I remember saying that. I believe it too. I suppose you think it\r\nnonsense?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet you will pay no attention to my instinct against Alfred\r\nInglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot curtly. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause your instinct is not against Mr.\r\nInglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. You wish to believe he committed the crime. You believe him\r\ncapable of committing it. But your instinct tells you he did not commit\r\nit. It tells you more\xe2\x80\x94shall I go on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was staring at him, fascinated, and made a slight affirmative\r\nmovement of the hand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShall I tell you why you have been so vehement against Mr. Inglethorp?\r\nIt is because you have been trying to believe what you wish to believe.\r\nIt is because you are trying to drown and stifle your instinct, which\r\ntells you another name\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, no!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Miss Howard wildly, flinging up her hands. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay it! Oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t say it! It isn\xe2\x80\x99t true! It can\xe2\x80\x99t be true. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhat put such a wild\xe2\x80\x94such a dreadful\xe2\x80\x94idea into my head!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am right, am I not?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Poirot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes; you must be a wizard to have guessed. But it can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nso\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s too monstrous, too impossible. It _must_ be Alfred Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot shook his head gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask me about it,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Miss Howard, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause I shan\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\nyou. I won\xe2\x80\x99t admit it, even to myself. I must be mad to think of such a\r\nthing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded, as if satisfied.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will ask you nothing. It is enough for me that it is as I thought.\r\nAnd I\xe2\x80\x94I, too, have an instinct. We are working together towards a\r\ncommon end.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask me to help you, because I won\xe2\x80\x99t. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t lift a finger\r\nto\xe2\x80\x94to\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She faltered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will help me in spite of yourself. I ask you nothing\xe2\x80\x94but you will\r\nbe my ally. You will not be able to help yourself. You will do the only\r\nthing that I want of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will watch!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvelyn Howard bowed her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I can\xe2\x80\x99t help doing that. I am always watching\xe2\x80\x94always hoping I\r\nshall be proved wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we are wrong, well and good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cNo one will be more\r\npleased than I shall. But, if we are right? If we are right, Miss\r\nHoward, on whose side are you then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt could be hushed up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere must be no hushing up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut Emily herself\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She broke off.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot gravely, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is unworthy of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly she took her face from her hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat was not Evelyn Howard who spoke!\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nflung her head up proudly. \xe2\x80\x9c_This_ is Evelyn Howard! And she is on the\r\nside of Justice! Let the cost be what it may.\xe2\x80\x9d And with these words,\r\nshe walked firmly out of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, looking after her, \xe2\x80\x9cgoes a very valuable ally.\r\nThat woman, Hastings, has got brains as well as a heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI did not reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInstinct is a marvellous thing,\xe2\x80\x9d mused Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cIt can neither be\r\nexplained nor ignored.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou and Miss Howard seem to know what you are talking about,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nobserved coldly. \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you don\xe2\x80\x99t realize that _I_ am still in the\r\ndark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally? Is that so, _mon ami?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Enlighten me, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot studied me attentively for a moment or two. Then, to my intense\r\nsurprise, he shook his head decidedly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, my friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, look here, why not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo is enough for a secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I think it is very unfair to keep back facts from me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not keeping back facts. Every fact that I know is in your\r\npossession. You can draw your own deductions from them. This time it is\r\na question of ideas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, it would be interesting to know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot looked at me very earnestly, and again shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sadly, \xe2\x80\x9c_you_ have no instincts.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was intelligence you were requiring just now,\xe2\x80\x9d I pointed out.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe two often go together,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot enigmatically.\r\n\r\nThe remark seemed so utterly irrelevant that I did not even take the\r\ntrouble to answer it. But I decided that if I made any interesting and\r\nimportant discoveries\xe2\x80\x94as no doubt I should\xe2\x80\x94I would keep them to myself,\r\nand surprise Poirot with the ultimate result.\r\n\r\nThere are times when it is one\xe2\x80\x99s duty to assert oneself.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX. DR. BAUERSTEIN\r\n\r\nI had had no opportunity as yet of passing on Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s message to\r\nLawrence. But now, as I strolled out on the lawn, still nursing a\r\ngrudge against my friend\xe2\x80\x99s high-handedness, I saw Lawrence on the\r\ncroquet lawn, aimlessly knocking a couple of very ancient balls about,\r\nwith a still more ancient mallet.\r\n\r\nIt struck me that it would be a good opportunity to deliver my message.\r\nOtherwise, Poirot himself might relieve me of it. It was true that I\r\ndid not quite gather its purport, but I flattered myself that by\r\nLawrence\xe2\x80\x99s reply, and perhaps a little skillful cross-examination on my\r\npart, I should soon perceive its significance. Accordingly I accosted\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been looking for you,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked untruthfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. The truth is, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got a message for you\xe2\x80\x94from Poirot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe told me to wait until I was alone with you,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, dropping my\r\nvoice significantly, and watching him intently out of the corner of my\r\neye. I have always been rather good at what is called, I believe,\r\ncreating an atmosphere.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no change of expression in the dark melancholic face. Had he\r\nany idea of what I was about to say?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the message.\xe2\x80\x9d I dropped my voice still lower. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Find the extra\r\ncoffee-cup, and you can rest in peace.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth does he mean?\xe2\x80\x9d Lawrence stared at me in quite unaffected\r\nastonishment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot in the least. Do you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was compelled to shake my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat extra coffee-cup?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99d better ask Dorcas, or one of the maids, if he wants to know about\r\ncoffee-cups. It\xe2\x80\x99s their business, not mine. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about\r\nthe coffee-cups, except that we\xe2\x80\x99ve got some that are never used, which\r\nare a perfect dream! Old Worcester. You\xe2\x80\x99re not a connoisseur, are you,\r\nHastings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou miss a lot. A really perfect bit of old china\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s pure delight to\r\nhandle it, or even to look at it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, what am I to tell Poirot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell him I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what he\xe2\x80\x99s talking about. It\xe2\x80\x99s double Dutch to\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was moving off towards the house again when he suddenly called me\r\nback.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, what was the end of that message? Say it over again, will you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Find the extra coffee-cup, and you can rest in peace.\xe2\x80\x99 Are you sure\r\nyou don\xe2\x80\x99t know what it means?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked him earnestly.\r\n\r\nHe shook his head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said musingly, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t. I\xe2\x80\x94I wish I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boom of the gong sounded from the house, and we went in together.\r\nPoirot had been asked by John to remain to lunch, and was already\r\nseated at the table.\r\n\r\nBy tacit consent, all mention of the tragedy was barred. We conversed\r\non the war, and other outside topics. But after the cheese and biscuits\r\nhad been handed round, and Dorcas had left the room, Poirot suddenly\r\nleant forward to Mrs. Cavendish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, madame, for recalling unpleasant memories, but I have a\r\nlittle idea\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9clittle ideas\xe2\x80\x9d were becoming a perfect\r\nbyword\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand would like to ask one or two questions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf me? Certainly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too amiable, madame. What I want to ask is this: the door\r\nleading into Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room from that of Mademoiselle Cynthia,\r\nit was bolted, you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly it was bolted,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mary Cavendish, rather surprised. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nsaid so at the inquest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBolted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d She looked perplexed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are sure it was bolted, and not merely\r\nlocked?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I see what you mean. No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I said bolted, meaning that\r\nit was fastened, and I could not open it, but I believe all the doors\r\nwere found bolted on the inside.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, as far as you are concerned, the door might equally well have\r\nbeen locked?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou yourself did not happen to notice, madame, when you entered Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room, whether that door was bolted or not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I believe it was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you did not see it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. I\xe2\x80\x94never looked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut _I_ did,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Lawrence suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cI happened to notice\r\nthat it _was_ bolted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that settles it.\xe2\x80\x9d And Poirot looked crestfallen.\r\n\r\nI could not help rejoicing that, for once, one of his \xe2\x80\x9clittle ideas\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhad come to naught.\r\n\r\nAfter lunch Poirot begged me to accompany him home. I consented rather\r\nstiffly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are annoyed, is it not so?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked anxiously, as we walked\r\nthrough the park.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d I said coldly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well. That lifts a great load from my mind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was not quite what I had intended. I had hoped that he would have\r\nobserved the stiffness of my manner. Still, the fervour of his words\r\nwent towards the appeasing of my just displeasure. I thawed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI gave Lawrence your message,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what did he say? He was entirely puzzled?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I am quite sure he had no idea of what you meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI had expected Poirot to be disappointed; but, to my surprise, he\r\nreplied that that was as he had thought, and that he was very glad. My\r\npride forbade me to ask any questions.\r\n\r\nPoirot switched off on another tack.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle Cynthia was not at lunch to-day? How was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is at the hospital again. She resumed work to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, she is an industrious little demoiselle. And pretty too. She is\r\nlike pictures I have seen in Italy. I would rather like to see that\r\ndispensary of hers. Do you think she would show it to me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure she would be delighted. It\xe2\x80\x99s an interesting little place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes she go there every day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has all Wednesdays off, and comes back to lunch on Saturdays.\r\nThose are her only times off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will remember. Women are doing great work nowadays, and Mademoiselle\r\nCynthia is clever\xe2\x80\x94oh, yes, she has brains, that little one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I believe she has passed quite a stiff exam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout doubt. After all, it is very responsible work. I suppose they\r\nhave very strong poisons there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she showed them to us. They are kept locked up in a little\r\ncupboard. I believe they have to be very careful. They always take out\r\nthe key before leaving the room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed. I shall go round the long way\r\nthrough the woods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woods round Styles were very beautiful. After the walk across the\r\nopen park, it was pleasant to saunter lazily through the cool glades.\r\nThere was hardly a breath of wind, the very chirp of the birds was\r\nfaint and subdued. Of course, it was all a\r\nbad dream. The truth of the matter was that it was Lawrence who had\r\nmurdered Alfred Inglethorp with a croquet mallet. But it was absurd of\r\nJohn to make such a fuss about it, and to go shouting out: \xe2\x80\x9cI tell you\r\nI won\xe2\x80\x99t have it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI woke up with a start.\r\n\r\nAt once I realized that I was in a very awkward predicament. Look, there is John\xe2\x80\x94and Miss Howard\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCynthia nodded rather gloomily. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, John likes me, I think, and of\r\ncourse Evie, for all her gruff ways, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be unkind to a fly. But\r\nLawrence never speaks to me if he can help it, and Mary can hardly\r\nbring herself to be civil to me. She wants Evie to stay on, is begging\r\nher to, but she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want me, and\xe2\x80\x94and\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nSuddenly the poor child burst out crying.\r\n\r\nI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what possessed me. Her beauty, perhaps, as she sat there,\r\nwith the sunlight glinting down on her head; perhaps the sense of\r\nrelief at encountering someone who so obviously could have no\r\nconnection with the tragedy; perhaps honest pity for her youth and\r\nloneliness. Anyway, I leant forward, and taking her little hand, I said\r\nawkwardly:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry me, Cynthia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nUnwittingly, I had hit upon a sovereign remedy for her tears. She sat\r\nup at once, drew her hand away, and said, with some asperity:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be silly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was a little annoyed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not being silly. I am asking you to do me the honour of becoming\r\nmy wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo my intense surprise, Cynthia burst out laughing, and called me a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cfunny dear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s perfectly sweet of you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you know you don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\nto!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind what you\xe2\x80\x99ve got. You don\xe2\x80\x99t really want to\xe2\x80\x94and I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\neither.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, of course, that settles it,\xe2\x80\x9d I said stiffly. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\r\nanything to laugh at. There\xe2\x80\x99s nothing funny about a proposal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d said Cynthia. \xe2\x80\x9cSomebody might accept you next time.\r\nGood-bye, you\xe2\x80\x99ve cheered me up _very_ much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, with a final uncontrollable burst of merriment, she vanished\r\nthrough the trees.\r\n\r\nThinking over the interview, it struck me as being profoundly\r\nunsatisfactory.\r\n\r\nIt occurred to me suddenly that I would go down to the village, and\r\nlook up Bauerstein. Somebody ought to be keeping an eye on the fellow.\r\nAt the same time, it would be wise to allay any suspicions he might\r\nhave as to his being suspected. I remembered how Poirot had relied on\r\nmy diplomacy. Accordingly, I went to the little house with the\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cApartments\xe2\x80\x9d card inserted in the window, where I knew he lodged, and\r\ntapped on the door.\r\n\r\nAn old woman came and opened it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood afternoon,\xe2\x80\x9d I said pleasantly. \xe2\x80\x9cIs Dr. Bauerstein in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe stared at me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you heard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeard what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAbout him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat about him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s took.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTook? Dead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, took by the perlice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the police!\xe2\x80\x9d I gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean they\xe2\x80\x99ve arrested him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it, and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI waited to hear no more, but tore up the village to find Poirot.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X. THE ARREST\r\n\r\nTo my extreme annoyance, Poirot was not in, and the old Belgian who\r\nanswered my knock informed me that he believed he had gone to London.\r\n\r\nI was dumbfounded. What on earth could Poirot be doing in London! Was\r\nit a sudden decision on his part, or had he already made up his mind\r\nwhen he parted from me a few hours earlier?\r\n\r\nI retraced my steps to Styles in some annoyance. With Poirot away, I\r\nwas uncertain how to act. Had he foreseen this arrest? Had he not, in\r\nall probability, been the cause of it? Those questions I could not\r\nresolve. But in the meantime what was I to do? Should I announce the\r\narrest openly at Styles, or not? Though I did not acknowledge it to\r\nmyself, the thought of Mary Cavendish was weighing on me. Would it not\r\nbe a terrible shock to her? For the moment, I set aside utterly any\r\nsuspicions of her. She could not be implicated\xe2\x80\x94otherwise I should have\r\nheard some hint of it.\r\n\r\nOf course, there was no possibility of being able permanently to\r\nconceal Dr. Bauerstein\xe2\x80\x99s arrest from her. It would be announced in\r\nevery newspaper on the morrow. Still, I shrank from blurting it out. If\r\nonly Poirot had been accessible, I could have asked his advice. What\r\npossessed him to go posting off to London in this unaccountable way?\r\n\r\nIn spite of myself, my opinion of his sagacity was immeasurably\r\nheightened. I would never have dreamt of suspecting the doctor, had not\r\nPoirot put it into my head. Yes, decidedly, the little man was clever.\r\n\r\nAfter some reflecting, I decided to take John into my confidence, and\r\nleave him to make the matter public or not, as he thought fit.\r\n\r\nHe gave vent to a prodigious whistle, as I imparted the news.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGreat Scott! You _were_ right, then. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t believe it at the\r\ntime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is astonishing until you get used to the idea, and see how it\r\nmakes everything fit in. Now, what are we to do? Of course, it will be\r\ngenerally known to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn reflected.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever mind,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cwe won\xe2\x80\x99t say anything at present. There\r\nis no need. As you say, it will be known soon enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut to my intense surprise, on getting down early the next morning, and\r\neagerly opening the newspapers, there was not a word about the arrest!\r\nThere was a column of mere padding about \xe2\x80\x9cThe Styles Poisoning Case,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nbut nothing further. It was rather inexplicable, but I supposed that,\r\nfor some reason or other, Japp wished to keep it out of the papers. It\r\nworried me just a little, for it suggested the possibility that there\r\nmight be further arrests to come.\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast, I decided to go down to the village, and see if Poirot\r\nhad returned yet; but, before I could start, a well-known face blocked\r\none of the windows, and the well-known voice said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Bonjour, mon ami!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed, with relief, and seizing him by both hands, I\r\ndragged him into the room. \xe2\x80\x9cI was never so glad to see anyone. Listen,\r\nI have said nothing to anybody but John. Is that right?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cI do not know what you are talking\r\nabout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDr. Bauerstein\xe2\x80\x99s arrest, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d I answered impatiently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs Bauerstein arrested, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you not know it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot the least in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d But, pausing a moment, he added: \xe2\x80\x9cStill,\r\nit does not surprise me. After all, we are only four miles from the\r\ncoast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe coast?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, puzzled. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat has that got to do with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, it is obvious!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to me. No doubt I am very dense, but I cannot see what the\r\nproximity of the coast has got to do with the murder of Mrs.\r\nInglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing at all, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we were\r\nspeaking of the arrest of Dr. Bauerstein.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he is arrested for the murder of Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Poirot, in apparently lively astonishment. \xe2\x80\x9cDr.\r\nBauerstein arrested for the murder of Mrs. Inglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible! That would be too good a farce! Who told you that, my\r\nfriend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, no one exactly told me,\xe2\x80\x9d I confessed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut he is arrested.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, very likely. But for espionage, _mon ami_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEspionage?\xe2\x80\x9d I gasped.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot for poisoning Mrs. Inglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot unless our friend Japp has taken leave of his senses,\xe2\x80\x9d replied\r\nPoirot placidly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94but I thought you thought so too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot gave me one look, which conveyed a wondering pity, and his full\r\nsense of the utter absurdity of such an idea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say,\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, slowly adapting myself to the new idea,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat Dr. Bauerstein is a spy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you never suspected it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt never entered my head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt did not strike you as peculiar that a famous London doctor should\r\nbury himself in a little village like this, and should be in the habit\r\nof walking about at all hours of the night, fully dressed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I confessed, \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought of such a thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is, of course, a German by birth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot thoughtfully,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthough he has practised so long in this country that nobody thinks of\r\nhim as anything but an Englishman. He was naturalized about fifteen\r\nyears ago. A very clever man\xe2\x80\x94a Jew, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe blackguard!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried indignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. He is, on the contrary, a patriot. Think what he stands to\r\nlose. I admire the man myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I could not look at it in Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s philosophical way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this is the man with whom Mrs. Cavendish has been wandering about\r\nall over the country!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried indignantly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I should fancy he had found her very useful,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Poirot.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo long as gossip busied itself in coupling their names together, any\r\nother vagaries of the doctor\xe2\x80\x99s passed unobserved.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you think he never really cared for her?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked eagerly\xe2\x80\x94rather\r\ntoo eagerly, perhaps, under the circumstances.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat, of course, I cannot say, but\xe2\x80\x94shall I tell you my own private\r\nopinion, Hastings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is this: that Mrs. Cavendish does not care, and never has\r\ncared one little jot about Dr. Bauerstein!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you really think so?\xe2\x80\x9d I could not disguise my pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am quite sure of it. And I will tell you why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause she cares for someone else, _mon ami_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d What did he mean? In spite of myself, an agreeable warmth spread\r\nover me. I am not a vain man where women are concerned, but I\r\nremembered certain evidences, too lightly thought of at the time,\r\nperhaps, but which certainly seemed to indicate\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\nMy pleasing thoughts were interrupted by the sudden entrance of Miss\r\nHoward. She glanced round hastily to make sure there was no one else in\r\nthe room, and quickly produced an old sheet of brown paper. This she\r\nhanded to Poirot, murmuring as she did so the cryptic words:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn top of the wardrobe.\xe2\x80\x9d Then she hurriedly left the room.\r\n\r\nPoirot unfolded the sheet of paper eagerly, and uttered an exclamation\r\nof satisfaction. He spread it out on the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome here, Hastings. Now tell me, what is that initial\xe2\x80\x94J. or L.?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a medium sized sheet of paper, rather dusty, as though it had\r\nlain by for some time. But it was the label that was attracting\r\nPoirot\xe2\x80\x99s attention. At the top, it bore the printed stamp of Messrs.\r\nParkson\xe2\x80\x99s, the well-known theatrical costumiers, and it was addressed\r\nto \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94(the debatable initial) Cavendish, Esq., Styles Court, Styles St.\r\nMary, Essex.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might be T., or it might be L.,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, after studying the thing\r\nfor a minute or two. \xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly isn\xe2\x80\x99t a J.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot, folding up the paper again. \xe2\x80\x9cI, also, am of\r\nyour way of thinking. It is an L., depend upon it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did it come from?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked curiously. \xe2\x80\x9cIs it important?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cModerately so. It confirms a surmise of mine. Having deduced its\r\nexistence, I set Miss Howard to search for it, and, as you see, she has\r\nbeen successful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did she mean by \xe2\x80\x98On the top of the wardrobe\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe meant,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot promptly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat she found it on top of a\r\nwardrobe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA funny place for a piece of brown paper,\xe2\x80\x9d I mused.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. The top of a wardrobe is an excellent place for brown\r\npaper and cardboard boxes. I have kept them there myself. Neatly\r\narranged, there is nothing to offend the eye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I asked earnestly, \xe2\x80\x9chave you made up your mind about this\r\ncrime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94that is to say, I believe I know how it was committed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnfortunately, I have no proof beyond my surmise, unless\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d With\r\nsudden energy, he caught me by the arm, and whirled me down the hall,\r\ncalling out in French in his excitement: \xe2\x80\x9cMademoiselle Dorcas,\r\nMademoiselle Dorcas, _un moment, s\xe2\x80\x99il vous pla\xc3\xaet!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDorcas, quite flurried by the noise, came hurrying out of the pantry.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy good Dorcas, I have an idea\xe2\x80\x94a little idea\xe2\x80\x94if it should prove\r\njustified, what magnificent chance! Tell me, on Monday, not Tuesday,\r\nDorcas, but Monday, the day before the tragedy, did anything go wrong\r\nwith Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s bell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDorcas looked very surprised.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, now you mention it, it did; though I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how you came\r\nto hear of it. A mouse, or some such, must have nibbled the wire\r\nthrough. The man came and put it right on Tuesday morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a long drawn exclamation of ecstasy, Poirot led the way back to\r\nthe morning-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee you, one should not ask for outside proof\xe2\x80\x94no, reason should be\r\nenough. But the flesh is weak, it is consolation to find that one is on\r\nthe right track. Ah, my friend, I am like a giant refreshed. I run! I\r\nleap!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd, in very truth, run and leap he did, gambolling wildly down the\r\nstretch of lawn outside the long window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your remarkable little friend doing?\xe2\x80\x9d asked a voice behind me,\r\nand I turned to find Mary Cavendish at my elbow. She smiled, and so did\r\nI. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it all about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you. He asked Dorcas some question about a bell,\r\nand appeared so delighted with her answer that he is capering about as\r\nyou see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow ridiculous! He\xe2\x80\x99s going out of the gate. Isn\xe2\x80\x99t he coming back\r\nto-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I\xe2\x80\x99ve given up trying to guess what he\xe2\x80\x99ll do next.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs he quite mad, Mr. Hastings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI honestly don\xe2\x80\x99t know. Sometimes, I feel sure he is as mad as a\r\nhatter; and then, just as he is at his maddest, I find there is method\r\nin his madness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of her laugh, Mary was looking thoughtful this morning. She\r\nseemed grave, almost sad.\r\n\r\nIt occurred to me that it would be a good opportunity to tackle her on\r\nthe subject of Cynthia. I began rather tactfully, _I_ thought, but I\r\nhad not gone far before she stopped me authoritatively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are an excellent advocate, I have no doubt, Mr. Hastings, but in\r\nthis case your talents are quite thrown away. Cynthia will run no risk\r\nof encountering any unkindness from me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI began to stammer feebly that I hoped she hadn\xe2\x80\x99t thought\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94 But again\r\nshe stopped me, and her words were so unexpected that they quite drove\r\nCynthia, and her troubles, out of my mind.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Hastings,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you think I and my husband are happy\r\ntogether?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was considerably taken aback, and murmured something about it\xe2\x80\x99s not\r\nbeing my business to think anything of the sort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she said quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cwhether it is your business or not, I will\r\ntell you that we are _not_ happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI said nothing, for I saw that she had not finished.\r\n\r\nShe began slowly, walking up and down the room, her head a little bent,\r\nand that slim, supple figure of hers swaying gently as she walked. She\r\nstopped suddenly, and looked up at me.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know anything about me, do you?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere I come\r\nfrom, who I was before I married John\xe2\x80\x94anything, in fact? Well, I will\r\ntell you. I will make a father confessor of you. You are kind, I\r\nthink\xe2\x80\x94yes, I am sure you are kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSomehow, I was not quite as elated as I might have been. I remembered\r\nthat Cynthia had begun her confidences in much the same way. Besides, a\r\nfather confessor should be elderly, it is not at all the role for a\r\nyoung man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father was English,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Cavendish, \xe2\x80\x9cbut my mother was a\r\nRussian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cnow I understand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnderstand what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA hint of something foreign\xe2\x80\x94different\xe2\x80\x94that there has always been about\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy mother was very beautiful, I believe. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know, because I never\r\nsaw her. She died when I was quite a little child. I believe there was\r\nsome tragedy connected with her death\xe2\x80\x94she took an overdose of some\r\nsleeping draught by mistake. However that may be, my father was\r\nbroken-hearted. Shortly afterwards, he went into the Consular Service.\r\nEverywhere he went, I went with him. When I was twenty-three, I had\r\nbeen nearly all over the world. It was a splendid life\xe2\x80\x94I loved it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a smile on her face, and her head was thrown back. She seemed\r\nliving in the memory of those old glad days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen my father died. He left me very badly off. I had to go and live\r\nwith some old aunts in Yorkshire.\xe2\x80\x9d She shuddered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will understand\r\nme when I say that it was a deadly life for a girl brought up as I had\r\nbeen. The narrowness, the deadly monotony of it, almost drove me mad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe paused a minute, and added in a different tone: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then I met\r\nJohn Cavendish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can imagine that, from my aunts\xe2\x80\x99 point of view, it was a very good\r\nmatch for me. He declared that that satisfied him, and so\xe2\x80\x94we were married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe waited a long time, a little frown had gathered on her forehead.\r\nShe seemed to be looking back earnestly into those past days.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think\xe2\x80\x94I am sure\xe2\x80\x94he cared for me at first. He\xe2\x80\x94it is not a pleasing\r\nthing for my pride, but it is the truth\xe2\x80\x94tired of me very soon.\xe2\x80\x9d I must\r\nhave made some murmur of dissent, for she went on quickly: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, he\r\ndid! Would you mind\r\nmoving\xe2\x80\x94thank you, Mr. Hastings.\xe2\x80\x9d And she walked quietly past me out of\r\nthe window, with a cool little nod of dismissal.\r\n\r\nNo, surely she could not care for Bauerstein. No woman could act her\r\npart with that icy unconcern.\r\n\r\nPoirot did not make his appearance the following morning, and there was\r\nno sign of the Scotland Yard men.\r\n\r\nBut, at lunch-time, there arrived a new piece of evidence\xe2\x80\x94or rather\r\nlack of evidence. We had vainly tried to trace the fourth letter, which\r\nMrs. Inglethorp had written on the evening preceding her death. Our\r\nefforts having been in vain, we had abandoned the matter, hoping that\r\nit might turn up of itself one day. The wind through the trees made a mournful noise, like\r\nsome great giant sighing.\r\n\r\nWe walked up to the great door of Styles, and at once the knowledge\r\ncame to us that something was wrong.\r\n\r\nDorcas came running out to meet us. She was crying and wringing her\r\nhands. I was aware of other servants huddled together in the\r\nbackground, all eyes and ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, m\xe2\x80\x99am! Oh, m\xe2\x80\x99am! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know how to tell you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Dorcas?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cTell us at once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s those wicked detectives. They\xe2\x80\x99ve arrested him\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99ve arrested\r\nMr. Cavendish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArrested Lawrence?\xe2\x80\x9d I gasped.\r\n\r\nI saw a strange look come into Dorcas\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir. Not Mr. Lawrence\xe2\x80\x94Mr. John.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBehind me, with a wild cry, Mary Cavendish fell heavily against me, and\r\nas I turned to catch her I met the quiet triumph in Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s eyes.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI. THE CASE FOR THE PROSECUTION\r\n\r\nThe trial of John Cavendish for the murder of his stepmother took place\r\ntwo months later.\r\n\r\nOf the intervening weeks I will say little, but my admiration and\r\nsympathy went out unfeignedly to Mary Cavendish. She ranged herself\r\npassionately on her husband\xe2\x80\x99s side, scorning the mere idea of his\r\nguilt, and fought for him tooth and nail.\r\n\r\nI expressed my admiration to Poirot, and he nodded thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she is of those women who show at their best in adversity. It\r\nbrings out all that is sweetest and truest in them. Her pride and her\r\njealousy have\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJealousy?\xe2\x80\x9d I queried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Have you not realized that she is an unusually jealous woman? As\r\nI was saying, her pride and jealousy have been laid aside. She thinks\r\nof nothing but her husband, and the terrible fate that is hanging over\r\nhim.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe spoke very feelingly, and I looked at him earnestly, remembering\r\nthat last afternoon, when he had been deliberating whether or not to\r\nspeak. With his tenderness for \xe2\x80\x9ca woman\xe2\x80\x99s happiness,\xe2\x80\x9d I felt glad that\r\nthe decision had been taken out of his hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven now,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI can hardly believe it. You see, up to the very\r\nlast minute, I thought it was Lawrence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot grinned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know you did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut John! My old friend John!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEvery murderer is probably somebody\xe2\x80\x99s old friend,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Poirot\r\nphilosophically. \xe2\x80\x9cYou cannot mix up sentiment and reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI must say I think you might have given me a hint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, _mon ami_, I did not do so, just because he _was_ your old\r\nfriend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI was rather disconcerted by this, remembering how I had busily passed\r\non to John what I believed to be Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s views concerning Bauerstein.\r\nHe, by the way, had been acquitted of the charge brought against him.\r\nNevertheless, although he had been too clever for them this time, and\r\nthe charge of espionage could not be brought home to him, his wings\r\nwere pretty well clipped for the future.\r\n\r\nI asked Poirot whether he thought John would be condemned. To my\r\nintense surprise, he replied that, on the contrary, he was extremely\r\nlikely to be acquitted.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, Poirot\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d I protested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my friend, have I not said to you all along that I have no proofs.\r\nIt is one thing to know that a man is guilty, it is quite another\r\nmatter to prove him so. And, in this case, there is terribly little\r\nevidence. That is the whole trouble. I, Hercule Poirot, know, but I\r\nlack the last link in my chain. And unless I can find that missing\r\nlink\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He shook his head gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you first suspect John Cavendish?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, after a minute or\r\ntwo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you not suspect him at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, indeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot after that fragment of conversation you overheard between Mrs.\r\nCavendish and her mother-in-law, and her subsequent lack of frankness\r\nat the inquest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you not put two and two together, and reflect that if it was not\r\nAlfred Inglethorp who was quarrelling with his wife\xe2\x80\x94and you remember,\r\nhe strenuously denied it at the inquest\xe2\x80\x94it must be either Lawrence or\r\nJohn. Now, if it was Lawrence, Mary Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s conduct was just as\r\ninexplicable. But if, on the other hand, it was John, the whole thing\r\nwas explained quite naturally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo,\xe2\x80\x9d I cried, a light breaking in upon me, \xe2\x80\x9cit was John who quarrelled\r\nwith his mother that afternoon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have known this all along?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly. Mrs. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s behaviour could only be explained that\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet you say he may be acquitted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot shrugged his shoulders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly I do. At the police court proceedings, we shall hear the\r\ncase for the prosecution, but in all probability his solicitors will\r\nadvise him to reserve his defence. That will be sprung upon us at the\r\ntrial. And\xe2\x80\x94ah, by the way, I have a word of caution to give you, my\r\nfriend. I must not appear in the case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Officially, I have nothing to do with it. Until I have found that\r\nlast link in my chain, I must remain behind the scenes. Mrs. Cavendish\r\nmust think I am working for her husband, not against him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say, that\xe2\x80\x99s playing it a bit low down,\xe2\x80\x9d I protested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. We have to deal with a most clever and unscrupulous man,\r\nand we must use any means in our power\xe2\x80\x94otherwise he will slip through\r\nour fingers. That is why I have been careful to remain in the\r\nbackground. All the discoveries have been made by Japp, and Japp will\r\ntake all the credit. If I am called upon to give evidence at all\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he\r\nsmiled broadly\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cit will probably be as a witness for the defence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could hardly believe my ears.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is quite _en r\xc3\xa8gle_,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cStrangely enough, I can\r\ngive evidence that will demolish one contention of the prosecution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe one that relates to the destruction of the will. John Cavendish\r\ndid not destroy that will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot was a true prophet. I will not go into the details of the police\r\ncourt proceedings, as it involves many tiresome repetitions. I will\r\nmerely state baldly that John Cavendish reserved his defence, and was\r\nduly committed for trial.\r\n\r\nSeptember found us all in London. Mary took a house in Kensington,\r\nPoirot being included in the family party.\r\n\r\nI myself had been given a job at the War Office, so was able to see\r\nthem continually.\r\n\r\nAs the weeks went by, the state of Poirot\xe2\x80\x99s nerves grew worse and\r\nworse. That \xe2\x80\x9clast link\xe2\x80\x9d he talked about was still lacking. Privately, I\r\nhoped it might remain so, for what happiness could there be for Mary,\r\nif John were not acquitted?\r\n\r\nOn September 15th John Cavendish appeared in the dock at the Old\r\nBailey, charged with \xe2\x80\x9cThe Wilful Murder of Emily Agnes Inglethorp,\xe2\x80\x9d and\r\npleaded \xe2\x80\x9cNot Guilty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Ernest Heavywether, the famous K.C., had been engaged to defend\r\nhim.\r\n\r\nMr. Philips, K.C., opened the case for the Crown.\r\n\r\nThe murder, he said, was a most premeditated and cold-blooded one. It\r\nwas neither more nor less than the deliberate poisoning of a fond and\r\ntrusting woman by the stepson to whom she had been more than a mother.\r\nEver since his boyhood, she had supported him. He and his wife had\r\nlived at Styles Court in every luxury, surrounded by her care and\r\nattention. She had been their kind and generous benefactress.\r\n\r\nHe proposed to call witnesses to show how the prisoner, a profligate\r\nand spendthrift, had been at the end of his financial tether, and had\r\nalso been carrying on an intrigue with a certain Mrs. Raikes, a\r\nneighbouring farmer\xe2\x80\x99s wife. This having come to his stepmother\xe2\x80\x99s ears,\r\nshe taxed him with it on the afternoon before her death, and a quarrel\r\nensued, part of which was overheard. On the previous day, the prisoner\r\nhad purchased strychnine at the village chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop, wearing a\r\ndisguise by means of which he hoped to throw the onus of the crime upon\r\nanother man\xe2\x80\x94to wit, Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s husband, of whom he had been\r\nbitterly jealous. Luckily for Mr. Inglethorp, he had been able to\r\nproduce an unimpeachable alibi.\r\n\r\nOn the afternoon of July 17th, continued Counsel, immediately after the\r\nquarrel with her son, Mrs. Inglethorp made a new will. This will was\r\nfound destroyed in the grate of her bedroom the following morning, but\r\nevidence had come to light which showed that it had been drawn up in\r\nfavour of her husband. Deceased had already made a will in his favour\r\nbefore her marriage, but\xe2\x80\x94and Mr. Philips wagged an expressive\r\nforefinger\xe2\x80\x94the prisoner was not aware of that. What had induced the\r\ndeceased to make a fresh will, with the old one still extant, he could\r\nnot say. She was an old lady, and might possibly have forgotten the\r\nformer one; or\xe2\x80\x94this seemed to him more likely\xe2\x80\x94she may have had an idea\r\nthat it was revoked by her marriage, as there had been some\r\nconversation on the subject. Ladies were not always very well versed in\r\nlegal knowledge. She had, about a year before, executed a will in\r\nfavour of the prisoner. He would call evidence to show that it was the\r\nprisoner who ultimately handed his stepmother her coffee on the fatal\r\nnight. Later in the evening, he had sought admission to her room, on\r\nwhich occasion, no doubt, he found an opportunity of destroying the\r\nwill which, as far as he knew, would render the one in his favour\r\nvalid.\r\n\r\nThe prisoner had been arrested in consequence of the discovery, in his\r\nroom, by Detective Inspector Japp\xe2\x80\x94a most brilliant officer\xe2\x80\x94of the\r\nidentical phial of strychnine which had been sold at the village\r\nchemist\xe2\x80\x99s to the supposed Mr. Inglethorp on the day before the murder.\r\nIt would be for the jury to decide whether or not these damning facts\r\nconstituted an overwhelming proof of the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s guilt.\r\n\r\nAnd, subtly implying that a jury which did not so decide, was quite\r\nunthinkable, Mr. Philips sat down and wiped his forehead.\r\n\r\nThe first witnesses for the prosecution were mostly those who had been\r\ncalled at the inquest, the medical evidence being again taken first.\r\n\r\nSir Ernest Heavywether, who was famous all over England for the\r\nunscrupulous manner in which he bullied witnesses, only asked two\r\nquestions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take it, Dr. Bauerstein, that strychnine, as a drug, acts quickly?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that you are unable to account for the delay in this case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Mace identified the phial handed him by Counsel as that sold by him\r\nto \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d Pressed, he admitted that he only knew Mr.\r\nInglethorp by sight. He had never spoken to him. The witness was not\r\ncross-examined.\r\n\r\nAlfred Inglethorp was called, and denied having purchased the poison.\r\nHe also denied having quarrelled with his wife. Various witnesses\r\ntestified to the accuracy of these statements.\r\n\r\nThe gardeners\xe2\x80\x99 evidence, as to the witnessing of the will was taken,\r\nand then Dorcas was called.\r\n\r\nDorcas, faithful to her \xe2\x80\x9cyoung gentlemen,\xe2\x80\x9d denied strenuously that it\r\ncould have been John\xe2\x80\x99s voice she heard, and resolutely declared, in the\r\nteeth of everything, that it was Mr. Inglethorp who had been in the\r\nboudoir with her mistress. A rather wistful smile passed across the\r\nface of the prisoner in the dock. He knew only too well how useless her\r\ngallant defiance was, since it was not the object of the defence to\r\ndeny this point. Mrs. Cavendish, of course, could not be called upon to\r\ngive evidence against her husband.\r\n\r\nAfter various questions on other matters, Mr. Philips asked:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the month of June last, do you remember a parcel arriving for Mr.\r\nLawrence Cavendish from Parkson\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDorcas shook her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember, sir. It may have done, but Mr. Lawrence was away\r\nfrom home part of June.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the event of a parcel arriving for him whilst he was away, what\r\nwould be done with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would either be put in his room or sent on after him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, I should leave it on the hall table. It would be Miss Howard\r\nwho would attend to anything like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEvelyn Howard was called and, after being examined on other points, was\r\nquestioned as to the parcel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t remember. Lots of parcels come. Can\xe2\x80\x99t remember one special one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not know if it was sent after Mr. Lawrence Cavendish to Wales,\r\nor whether it was put in his room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t think it was sent after him. Should have remembered it if it\r\nwas.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSupposing a parcel arrived addressed to Mr. Lawrence Cavendish, and\r\nafterwards it disappeared, should you remark its absence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, don\xe2\x80\x99t think so. I should think someone had taken charge of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe, Miss Howard, that it was you who found this sheet of brown\r\npaper?\xe2\x80\x9d He held up the same dusty piece which Poirot and I had examined\r\nin the morning-room at Styles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you come to look for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Belgian detective who was employed on the case asked me to search\r\nfor it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere did you eventually discover it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the top of\xe2\x80\x94of\xe2\x80\x94a wardrobe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn top of the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s wardrobe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I believe so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you not find it yourself?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you must know where you found it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it was on the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s wardrobe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn assistant from Parkson\xe2\x80\x99s, Theatrical Costumiers, testified that on\r\nJune 29th, they had supplied a black beard to Mr. L. Cavendish, as\r\nrequested. It was ordered by letter, and a postal order was enclosed.\r\nNo, they had not kept the letter. All transactions were entered in\r\ntheir books. They had sent the beard, as directed, to \xe2\x80\x9cL. Cavendish,\r\nEsq., Styles Court.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Ernest Heavywether rose ponderously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere was the letter written from?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom Styles Court.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same address to which you sent the parcel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the letter came from there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLike a beast of prey, Heavywether fell upon him:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know that letter came from Styles? Did you notice the\r\npostmark?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you did _not_ notice the postmark! And yet you affirm so\r\nconfidently that it came from Styles. It might, in fact, have been any\r\npostmark?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cY\xe2\x80\x94es.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn fact, the letter, though written on stamped notepaper, might have\r\nbeen posted from anywhere? From Wales, for instance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe witness admitted that such might be the case, and Sir Ernest\r\nsignified that he was satisfied.\r\n\r\nElizabeth Wells, second housemaid at Styles, stated that after she had\r\ngone to bed she remembered that she had bolted the front door, instead\r\nof leaving it on the latch as Mr. Inglethorp had requested. She had\r\naccordingly gone downstairs again to rectify her error. Hearing a\r\nslight noise in the West wing, she had peeped along the passage, and\r\nhad seen Mr. John Cavendish knocking at Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s door.\r\n\r\nSir Ernest Heavywether made short work of her, and under his unmerciful\r\nbullying she contradicted herself hopelessly, and Sir Ernest sat down\r\nagain with a satisfied smile on his face.\r\n\r\nWith the evidence of Annie, as to the candle grease on the floor, and\r\nas to seeing the prisoner take the coffee into the boudoir, the\r\nproceedings were adjourned until the following day.\r\n\r\nAs we went home, Mary Cavendish spoke bitterly against the prosecuting\r\ncounsel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat hateful man! What a net he has drawn around my poor John! How he\r\ntwisted every little fact until he made it seem what it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I said consolingly, \xe2\x80\x9cit will be the other way about to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she said meditatively; then suddenly dropped her voice. \xe2\x80\x9cMr.\r\nHastings, you do not think\xe2\x80\x94surely it could not have been Lawrence\xe2\x80\x94Oh,\r\nno, that could not be!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I myself was puzzled, and as soon as I was alone with Poirot I\r\nasked him what he thought Sir Ernest was driving at.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh!\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot appreciatively. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is a clever man, that Sir\r\nErnest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think he believes Lawrence guilty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think he believes or cares anything! No, what he is trying\r\nfor is to create such confusion in the minds of the jury that they are\r\ndivided in their opinion as to which brother did it. He is endeavouring\r\nto make out that there is quite as much evidence against Lawrence as\r\nagainst John\xe2\x80\x94and I am not at all sure that he will not succeed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDetective-inspector Japp was the first witness called when the trial\r\nwas reopened, and gave his evidence succinctly and briefly. After\r\nrelating the earlier events, he proceeded:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cActing on information received, Superintendent Summerhaye and myself\r\nsearched the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s room, during his temporary absence from the\r\nhouse. In his chest of drawers, hidden beneath some underclothing, we\r\nfound: first, a pair of gold-rimmed pince-nez similar to those worn by\r\nMr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94these were exhibited\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9csecondly, this phial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe phial was that already recognized by the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s assistant, a\r\ntiny bottle of blue glass, containing a few grains of a white\r\ncrystalline powder, and labelled: \xe2\x80\x9cStrychnine Hydro-chloride. POISON.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA fresh piece of evidence discovered by the detectives since the police\r\ncourt proceedings was a long, almost new piece of blotting-paper. It\r\nhad been found in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s cheque book, and on being reversed\r\nat a mirror, showed clearly the words: \xe2\x80\x9c. . . erything of which I die\r\npossessed I leave to my beloved husband Alfred Ing...\xe2\x80\x9d This placed\r\nbeyond question the fact that the destroyed will had been in favour of\r\nthe deceased lady\xe2\x80\x99s husband. Japp then produced the charred fragment of\r\npaper recovered from the grate, and this, with the discovery of the\r\nbeard in the attic, completed his evidence.\r\n\r\nBut Sir Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s cross-examination was yet to come.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat day was it when you searched the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTuesday, the 24th of July.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly a week after the tragedy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou found these two objects, you say, in the chest of drawers. Was the\r\ndrawer unlocked?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it not strike you as unlikely that a man who had committed a\r\ncrime should keep the evidence of it in an unlocked drawer for anyone\r\nto find?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe might have stowed them there in a hurry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you have just said it was a whole week since the crime. He would\r\nhave had ample time to remove them and destroy them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no perhaps about it. Would he, or would he not have had\r\nplenty of time to remove and destroy them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas the pile of underclothes under which the things were hidden heavy\r\nor light?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeavyish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn other words, it was winter underclothing. Obviously, the prisoner\r\nwould not be likely to go to that drawer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKindly answer my question. Would the prisoner, in the hottest week of\r\na hot summer, be likely to go to a drawer containing winter\r\nunderclothing. Yes, or no?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case, is it not possible that the articles in question might\r\nhave been put there by a third person, and that the prisoner was quite\r\nunaware of their presence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should not think it likely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it is possible?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMore evidence followed. Evidence as to the financial difficulties in\r\nwhich the prisoner had found himself at the end of July. Evidence as to\r\nhis intrigue with Mrs. Raikes\xe2\x80\x94poor Mary, that must have been bitter\r\nhearing for a woman of her pride. Evelyn Howard had been right in her\r\nfacts, though her animosity against Alfred Inglethorp had caused her to\r\njump to the conclusion that he was the person concerned.\r\n\r\nLawrence Cavendish was then put into the box. In a low voice, in answer\r\nto Mr. Philips\xe2\x80\x99 questions, he denied having ordered anything from\r\nParkson\xe2\x80\x99s in June. In fact, on June 29th, he had been staying away, in\r\nWales.\r\n\r\nInstantly, Sir Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s chin was shooting pugnaciously forward.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou deny having ordered a black beard from Parkson\xe2\x80\x99s on June 29th?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! In the event of anything happening to your brother, who will\r\ninherit Styles Court?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe brutality of the question called a flush to Lawrence\xe2\x80\x99s pale face.\r\nThe judge gave vent to a faint murmur of disapprobation, and the\r\nprisoner in the dock leant forward angrily.\r\n\r\nHeavywether cared nothing for his client\xe2\x80\x99s anger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer my question, if you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lawrence quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I should.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by you \xe2\x80\x98suppose\xe2\x80\x99? Your brother has no children. You\r\n_would_ inherit it, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, that\xe2\x80\x99s better,\xe2\x80\x9d said Heavywether, with ferocious geniality. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd\r\nyou\xe2\x80\x99d inherit a good slice of money too, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally, Sir Ernest,\xe2\x80\x9d protested the judge, \xe2\x80\x9cthese questions are not\r\nrelevant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Ernest bowed, and having shot his arrow proceeded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn Tuesday, the 17th July, you went, I believe, with another guest, to\r\nvisit the dispensary at the Red Cross Hospital in Tadminster?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you\xe2\x80\x94while you happened to be alone for a few seconds\xe2\x80\x94unlock the\r\npoison cupboard, and examine some of the bottles?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94may have done so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI put it to you that you did do so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Ernest fairly shot the next question at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you examine one bottle in particular?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I do not think so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe careful, Mr. Cavendish. I am referring to a little bottle of\r\nHydro-chloride of Strychnine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLawrence was turning a sickly greenish colour.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cN\xe2\x80\x94o\xe2\x80\x94I am sure I didn\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen how do you account for the fact that you left the unmistakable\r\nimpress of your finger-prints on it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe bullying manner was highly efficacious with a nervous disposition.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I suppose I must have taken up the bottle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so too! Did you abstract any of the contents of the bottle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why did you take it up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI once studied to be a doctor. Such things naturally interest me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh! So poisons \xe2\x80\x98naturally interest\xe2\x80\x99 you, do they? Still, you waited to\r\nbe alone before gratifying that \xe2\x80\x98interest\xe2\x80\x99 of yours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was pure chance. If the others had been there, I should have done\r\njust the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, as it happens, the others were not there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn fact, during the whole afternoon, you were only alone for a couple\r\nof minutes, and it happened\xe2\x80\x94I say, it happened\xe2\x80\x94to be during those two\r\nminutes that you displayed your \xe2\x80\x98natural interest\xe2\x80\x99 in Hydro-chloride of\r\nStrychnine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLawrence stammered pitiably.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith a satisfied and expressive countenance, Sir Ernest observed:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have nothing more to ask you, Mr. Cavendish.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis bit of cross-examination had caused great excitement in court. The\r\nheads of the many fashionably attired women present were busily laid\r\ntogether, and their whispers became so loud that the judge angrily\r\nthreatened to have the court cleared if there was not immediate\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\nThere was little more evidence. The hand-writing experts were called\r\nupon for their opinion of the signature of \xe2\x80\x9cAlfred Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x9d in the\r\nchemist\xe2\x80\x99s poison register. They all declared unanimously that it was\r\ncertainly not his hand-writing, and gave it as their view that it might\r\nbe that of the prisoner disguised. Cross-examined, they admitted that\r\nit might be the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s hand-writing cleverly counterfeited.\r\n\r\nSir Ernest Heavywether\xe2\x80\x99s speech in opening the case for the defence was\r\nnot a long one, but it was backed by the full force of his emphatic\r\nmanner. Never, he said, in the course of his long experience, had he\r\nknown a charge of murder rest on slighter evidence. Not only was it\r\nentirely circumstantial, but the greater part of it was practically\r\nunproved. Let them take the testimony they had heard and sift it\r\nimpartially. The strychnine had been found in a drawer in the\r\nprisoner\xe2\x80\x99s room. That drawer was an unlocked one, as he had pointed\r\nout, and he submitted that there was no evidence to prove that it was\r\nthe prisoner who had concealed the poison there. It was, in fact, a\r\nwicked and malicious attempt on the part of some third person to fix\r\nthe crime on the prisoner. The prosecution had been unable to produce a\r\nshred of evidence in support of their contention that it was the\r\nprisoner who ordered the black beard from Parkson\xe2\x80\x99s. The quarrel which\r\nhad taken place between prisoner and his stepmother was freely\r\nadmitted, but both it and his financial embarrassments had been grossly\r\nexaggerated.\r\n\r\nHis learned friend\xe2\x80\x94Sir Ernest nodded carelessly at Mr. Philips\xe2\x80\x94had\r\nstated that if the prisoner were an innocent man, he would have come\r\nforward at the inquest to explain that it was he, and not Mr.\r\nInglethorp, who had been the participator in the quarrel. He thought\r\nthe facts had been misrepresented. What had actually occurred was this.\r\nThe prisoner, returning to the house on Tuesday evening, had been\r\nauthoritatively told that there had been a violent quarrel between Mr.\r\nand Mrs. Inglethorp. No suspicion had entered the prisoner\xe2\x80\x99s head that\r\nanyone could possibly have mistaken his voice for that of Mr.\r\nInglethorp.
