He naturally concluded that his stepmother had had two\r\nquarrels.\r\n\r\nThe prosecution averred that on Monday, July 16th, the prisoner had\r\nentered the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop in the village, disguised as Mr. Inglethorp.\r\nThe prisoner, on the contrary, was at that time at a lonely spot called\r\nMarston\xe2\x80\x99s Spinney, where he had been summoned by an anonymous note,\r\ncouched in blackmailing terms, and threatening to reveal certain\r\nmatters to his wife unless he complied with its demands. The prisoner\r\nhad, accordingly, gone to the appointed spot, and after waiting there\r\nvainly for half an hour had returned home. Unfortunately, he had met\r\nwith no one on the way there or back who could vouch for the truth of\r\nhis story, but luckily he had kept the note, and it would be produced\r\nas evidence.\r\n\r\nAs for the statement relating to the destruction of the will, the\r\nprisoner had formerly practised at the Bar, and was perfectly well\r\naware that the will made in his favour a year before was automatically\r\nrevoked by his stepmother\xe2\x80\x99s remarriage. He would call evidence to show\r\nwho did destroy the will, and it was possible that that might open up\r\nquite a new view of the case.\r\n\r\nFinally, he would point out to the jury that there was evidence against\r\nother people besides John Cavendish. He would direct their attention to\r\nthe fact that the evidence against Mr. Lawrence Cavendish was quite as\r\nstrong, if not stronger than that against his brother.\r\n\r\nHe would now call the prisoner.\r\n\r\nJohn acquitted himself well in the witness-box. Under Sir Ernest\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nskilful handling, he told his tale credibly and well. The anonymous\r\nnote received by him was produced, and handed to the jury to examine.\r\nThe readiness with which he admitted his financial difficulties, and\r\nthe disagreement with his stepmother, lent value to his denials.\r\n\r\nAt the close of his examination, he paused, and said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to make one thing clear. I utterly reject and disapprove\r\nof Sir Ernest Heavywether\xe2\x80\x99s insinuations against my brother. My\r\nbrother, I am convinced, had no more to do with the crime than I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSir Ernest merely smiled, and noted with a sharp eye that John\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprotest had produced a very favourable impression on the jury.\r\n\r\nThen the cross-examination began.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand you to say that it never entered your head that the\r\nwitnesses at the inquest could possibly have mistaken your voice for\r\nthat of Mr. Inglethorp. Is not that very surprising?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so. I was told there had been a quarrel between my\r\nmother and Mr. Inglethorp, and it never occurred to me that such was\r\nnot really the case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot when the servant Dorcas repeated certain fragments of the\r\nconversation\xe2\x80\x94fragments which you must have recognized?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not recognize them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour memory must be unusually short!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but we were both angry, and, I think, said more than we meant. I\r\npaid very little attention to my mother\xe2\x80\x99s actual words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Philips\xe2\x80\x99 incredulous sniff was a triumph of forensic skill. He\r\npassed on to the subject of the note.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have produced this note very opportunely. Tell me, is there\r\nnothing familiar about the hand-writing of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot that I know of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you not think that it bears a marked resemblance to your own\r\nhand-writing\xe2\x80\x94carelessly disguised?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I do not think so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI put it to you that it is your own hand-writing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI put it to you that, anxious to prove an alibi, you conceived the\r\nidea of a fictitious and rather incredible appointment, and wrote this\r\nnote yourself in order to bear out your statement!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it not a fact that, at the time you claim to have been waiting\r\nabout at a solitary and unfrequented spot, you were really in the\r\nchemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop in Styles St. Mary, where you purchased strychnine in\r\nthe name of Alfred Inglethorp?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that is a lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI put it to you that, wearing a suit of Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s clothes, with\r\na black beard trimmed to resemble his, you were there\xe2\x80\x94and signed the\r\nregister in his name!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is absolutely untrue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I will leave the remarkable similarity of hand-writing between\r\nthe note, the register, and your own, to the consideration of the\r\njury,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Philips, and sat down with the air of a man who has\r\ndone his duty, but who was nevertheless horrified by such deliberate\r\nperjury.\r\n\r\nAfter this, as it was growing late, the case was adjourned till Monday.\r\n\r\nPoirot, I noticed, was looking profoundly discouraged. He had that\r\nlittle frown between the eyes that I knew so well.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, Poirot?\xe2\x80\x9d I inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _mon ami_, things are going badly, badly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn spite of myself, my heart gave a leap of relief. Evidently there was\r\na likelihood of John Cavendish being acquitted.\r\n\r\nWhen we reached the house, my little friend waved aside Mary\xe2\x80\x99s offer of\r\ntea.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I thank you, madame. I will mount to my room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI followed him. Still frowning, he went across to the desk and took out\r\na small pack of patience cards. Then he drew up a chair to the table,\r\nand, to my utter amazement, began solemnly to build card houses!\r\n\r\nMy jaw dropped involuntarily, and he said at once:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, _mon ami_, I am not in my second childhood! I steady my nerves,\r\nthat is all. This employment requires precision of the fingers. With\r\nprecision of the fingers goes precision of the brain. And never have I\r\nneeded that more than now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked.\r\n\r\nWith a great thump on the table, Poirot demolished his carefully built\r\nup edifice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is this, _mon ami!_ That I can build card houses seven stories\r\nhigh, but I cannot\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94thump\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cfind\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94thump\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9c that last link of which I\r\nspoke to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI could not quite tell what to say, so I held my peace, and he began\r\nslowly building up the cards again, speaking in jerks as he did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is done\xe2\x80\x94so! By placing\xe2\x80\x94one card\xe2\x80\x94on another\xe2\x80\x94with\r\nmathematical\xe2\x80\x94precision!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI watched the card house rising under his hands, story by story. He\r\nnever hesitated or faltered. It was really almost like a conjuring\r\ntrick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat a steady hand you\xe2\x80\x99ve got,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe I\xe2\x80\x99ve only seen\r\nyour hand shake once.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn an occasion when I was enraged, without doubt,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Poirot,\r\nwith great placidity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes indeed! You were in a towering rage. Do you remember? It was when\r\nyou discovered that the lock of the despatch-case in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbedroom had been forced. You stood by the mantelpiece, twiddling the\r\nthings on it in your usual fashion, and your hand shook like a leaf! I\r\nmust say\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut I stopped suddenly. For Poirot, uttering a hoarse and inarticulate\r\ncry, again annihilated his masterpiece of cards, and putting his hands\r\nover his eyes swayed backwards and forwards, apparently suffering the\r\nkeenest agony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood heavens, Poirot!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter? Are you taken\r\nill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no,\xe2\x80\x9d he gasped. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is\xe2\x80\x94it is\xe2\x80\x94that I have an idea!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed, much relieved. \xe2\x80\x9cOne of your \xe2\x80\x98little ideas\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _ma foi_, no!\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot frankly. \xe2\x80\x9cThis time it is an idea\r\ngigantic! Stupendous! And you\xe2\x80\x94_you_, my friend, have given it to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly clasping me in his arms, he kissed me warmly on both cheeks,\r\nand before I had recovered from my surprise ran headlong from the room.\r\n\r\nMary Cavendish entered at that moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _is_ the matter with Monsieur Poirot? He rushed past me crying\r\nout: \xe2\x80\x98A garage! For the love of Heaven, direct me to a garage, madame!\xe2\x80\x99\r\nAnd, before I could answer, he had dashed out into the street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI hurried to the window. True enough, there he was, tearing down the\r\nstreet, hatless, and gesticulating as he went. I turned to Mary with a\r\ngesture of despair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99ll be stopped by a policeman in another minute. There he goes,\r\nround the corner!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOur eyes met, and we stared helplessly at one another.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can be the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. He was building card houses, when suddenly he said he\r\nhad an idea, and rushed off as you saw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mary, \xe2\x80\x9cI expect he will be back before dinner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut night fell, and Poirot had not returned.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII. THE LAST LINK\r\n\r\nPoirot\xe2\x80\x99s abrupt departure had intrigued us all greatly. Sunday morning\r\nwore away, and still he did not reappear. But about three o\xe2\x80\x99clock a\r\nferocious and prolonged hooting outside drove us to the window, to see\r\nPoirot alighting from a car, accompanied by Japp and Summerhaye. The\r\nlittle man was transformed. He radiated an absurd complacency. He bowed\r\nwith exaggerated respect to Mary Cavendish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMadame, I have your permission to hold a little _r\xc3\xa9union_ in the\r\n_salon_? It is necessary for everyone to attend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMary smiled sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know, Monsieur Poirot, that you have _carte blanche_ in every\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too amiable, madame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill beaming, Poirot marshalled us all into the drawing-room, bringing\r\nforward chairs as he did so.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard\xe2\x80\x94here. Mademoiselle Cynthia. Monsieur Lawrence. The good\r\nDorcas. And Annie. _Bien!_ We must delay our proceedings a few minutes\r\nuntil Mr. Inglethorp arrives. I have sent him a note.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiss Howard rose immediately from her seat.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf that man comes into the house, I leave it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no!\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot went up to her and pleaded in a low voice.\r\n\r\nFinally Miss Howard consented to return to her chair. A few minutes\r\nlater Alfred Inglethorp entered the room.\r\n\r\nThe company once assembled, Poirot rose from his seat with the air of a\r\npopular lecturer, and bowed politely to his audience.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Messieurs, mesdames_, as you all know, I was called in by Monsieur\r\nJohn Cavendish to investigate this case. I at once examined the bedroom\r\nof the deceased which, by the advice of the doctors, had been kept\r\nlocked, and was consequently exactly as it had been when the tragedy\r\noccurred. I found: first, a fragment of green material; second, a stain\r\non the carpet near the window, still damp; thirdly, an empty box of\r\nbromide powders.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo take the fragment of green material first, I found it caught in the\r\nbolt of the communicating door between that room and the adjoining one\r\noccupied by Mademoiselle Cynthia. I handed the fragment over to the\r\npolice who did not consider it of much importance. Nor did they\r\nrecognize it for what it was\xe2\x80\x94a piece torn from a green land armlet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a little stir of excitement.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow there was only one person at Styles who worked on the land\xe2\x80\x94Mrs.\r\nCavendish. Therefore it must have been Mrs. Cavendish who entered the\r\ndeceased\xe2\x80\x99s room through the door communicating with Mademoiselle\r\nCynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that door was bolted on the inside!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I examined the room, yes. But in the first place we have only her\r\nword for it, since it was she who tried that particular door and\r\nreported it fastened. In the ensuing confusion she would have had ample\r\nopportunity to shoot the bolt across. I took an early opportunity of\r\nverifying my conjectures. To begin with, the fragment corresponds\r\nexactly with a tear in Mrs. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s armlet. Also, at the inquest,\r\nMrs. Cavendish declared that she had heard, from her own room, the fall\r\nof the table by the bed. I took an early opportunity of testing that\r\nstatement by stationing my friend Monsieur Hastings in the left wing of\r\nthe building, just outside Mrs. Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s door. I myself, in company\r\nwith the police, went to the deceased\xe2\x80\x99s room, and whilst there I,\r\napparently accidentally, knocked over the table in question, but found\r\nthat, as I had expected, Monsieur Hastings had heard no sound at all.\r\nThis confirmed my belief that Mrs. Cavendish was not speaking the truth\r\nwhen she declared that she had been dressing in her room at the time of\r\nthe tragedy. In fact, I was convinced that, far from having been in her\r\nown room, Mrs. Cavendish was actually in the deceased\xe2\x80\x99s room when the\r\nalarm was given.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shot a quick glance at Mary. She was very pale, but smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI proceeded to reason on that assumption. Mrs. Cavendish is in her\r\nmother-in-law\xe2\x80\x99s room. We will say that she is seeking for something and\r\nhas not yet found it. Suddenly Mrs. Inglethorp awakens and is seized\r\nwith an alarming paroxysm. She flings out her arm, overturning the bed\r\ntable, and then pulls desperately at the bell. Mrs. Cavendish,\r\nstartled, drops her candle, scattering the grease on the carpet. She\r\npicks it up, and retreats quickly to Mademoiselle Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room,\r\nclosing the door behind her. She hurries out into the passage, for the\r\nservants must not find her where she is. But it is too late! Already\r\nfootsteps are echoing along the gallery which connects the two wings.\r\nWhat can she do? Quick as thought, she hurries back to the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nroom, and starts shaking her awake. The hastily aroused household come\r\ntrooping down the passage. They are all busily battering at Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s door. It occurs to nobody that Mrs. Cavendish has not\r\narrived with the rest, but\xe2\x80\x94and this is significant\xe2\x80\x94I can find no one\r\nwho saw her come from the other wing.\xe2\x80\x9d He looked at Mary Cavendish. \xe2\x80\x9cAm\r\nI right, madame?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe bowed her head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite right, monsieur. You understand that, if I had thought I would\r\ndo my husband any good by revealing these facts, I would have done so.\r\nBut it did not seem to me to bear upon the question of his guilt or\r\ninnocence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a sense, that is correct, madame. But it cleared my mind of many\r\nmisconceptions, and left me free to see other facts in their true\r\nsignificance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe will!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lawrence. \xe2\x80\x9cThen it was you, Mary, who destroyed the\r\nwill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe shook her head, and Poirot shook his also.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d he said quietly. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is only one person who could possibly\r\nhave destroyed that will\xe2\x80\x94Mrs. Inglethorp herself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cShe had only made it out that very\r\nafternoon!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNevertheless, _mon ami_, it was Mrs. Inglethorp. Because, in no other\r\nway can you account for the fact that, on one of the hottest days of\r\nthe year, Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire to be lighted in her room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI gave a gasp. What idiots we had been never to think of that fire as\r\nbeing incongruous! Poirot was continuing:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe temperature on that day, messieurs, was 80 degrees in the shade.\r\nYet Mrs. Inglethorp ordered a fire! Why? Because she wished to destroy\r\nsomething, and could think of no other way. You will remember that, in\r\nconsequence of the War economics practiced at Styles, no waste paper\r\nwas thrown away. There was therefore no means of destroying a thick\r\ndocument such as a will. The moment I heard of a fire being lighted in\r\nMrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room, I leaped to the conclusion that it was to\r\ndestroy some important document\xe2\x80\x94possibly a will. So the discovery of\r\nthe charred fragment in the grate was no surprise to me. I did not, of\r\ncourse, know at the time that the will in question had only been made\r\nthis afternoon, and I will admit that, when I learnt that fact, I fell\r\ninto a grievous error. I came to the conclusion that Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndetermination to destroy her will arose as a direct consequence of the\r\nquarrel she had that afternoon, and that therefore the quarrel took\r\nplace after, and not before the making of the will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, as we know, I was wrong, and I was forced to abandon that idea.\r\nI faced the problem from a new standpoint. Now, at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Dorcas\r\noverheard her mistress saying angrily: \xe2\x80\x98You need not think that any\r\nfear of publicity, or scandal between husband and wife will deter me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nI conjectured, and conjectured rightly, that these words were\r\naddressed, not to her husband, but to Mr. John Cavendish. At five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, an hour later, she uses almost the same words, but the\r\nstandpoint is different. She admits to Dorcas, \xe2\x80\x98I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to\r\ndo; scandal between husband and wife is a dreadful thing.\xe2\x80\x99 At four\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock she has been angry, but completely mistress of herself. At five\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock she is in violent distress, and speaks of having had a great\r\nshock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLooking at the matter psychologically, I drew one deduction which I\r\nwas convinced was correct. The second \xe2\x80\x98scandal\xe2\x80\x99 she spoke of was not\r\nthe same as the first\xe2\x80\x94and it concerned herself!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us reconstruct. At four o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Mrs. Inglethorp quarrels with her\r\nson, and threatens to denounce him to his wife\xe2\x80\x94who, by the way,\r\noverheard the greater part of the conversation. At four-thirty, Mrs.\r\nInglethorp, in consequence of a conversation on the validity of wills,\r\nmakes a will in favour of her husband, which the two gardeners witness.\r\nAt five o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Dorcas finds her mistress in a state of considerable\r\nagitation, with a slip of paper\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98a letter,\xe2\x80\x99 Dorcas thinks\xe2\x80\x94in her hand,\r\nand it is then that she orders the fire in her room to be lighted.\r\nPresumably, then, between four-thirty and five o\xe2\x80\x99clock, something has\r\noccurred to occasion a complete revolution of feeling, since she is now\r\nas anxious to destroy the will, as she was before to make it. What was\r\nthat something?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs far as we know, she was quite alone during that half-hour. Nobody\r\nentered or left that boudoir. What then occasioned this sudden change\r\nof sentiment?\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne can only guess, but I believe my guess to be correct. Mrs.\r\nInglethorp had no stamps in her desk. We know this, because later she\r\nasked Dorcas to bring her some. Now in the opposite corner of the room\r\nstood her husband\xe2\x80\x99s desk\xe2\x80\x94locked. She was anxious to find some stamps,\r\nand, according to my theory, she tried her own keys in the desk. That\r\none of them fitted I know. She therefore opened the desk, and in\r\nsearching for the stamps she came across something else\xe2\x80\x94that slip of\r\npaper which Dorcas saw in her hand, and which assuredly was never meant\r\nfor Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s eyes. On the other hand, Mrs. Cavendish believed\r\nthat the slip of paper to which her mother-in-law clung so tenaciously\r\nwas a written proof of her own husband\xe2\x80\x99s infidelity. She demanded it\r\nfrom Mrs. Inglethorp who assured her, quite truly, that it had nothing\r\nto do with that matter. Mrs. Cavendish did not believe her. She thought\r\nthat Mrs. Inglethorp was shielding her stepson. Now Mrs. Cavendish is a\r\nvery resolute woman, and, behind her mask of reserve, she was madly\r\njealous of her husband. She determined to get hold of that paper at all\r\ncosts, and in this resolution chance came to her aid. She happened to\r\npick up the key of Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s despatch-case, which had been lost\r\nthat morning. She knew that her mother-in-law invariably kept all\r\nimportant papers in this particular case.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Cavendish, therefore, made her plans as only a woman driven\r\ndesperate through jealousy could have done. Some time in the evening\r\nshe unbolted the door leading into Mademoiselle Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room.\r\nPossibly she applied oil to the hinges, for I found that it opened\r\nquite noiselessly when I tried it. She put off her project until the\r\nearly hours of the morning as being safer, since the servants were\r\naccustomed to hearing her move about her room at that time. She dressed\r\ncompletely in her land kit, and made her way quietly through\r\nMademoiselle Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room into that of Mrs. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe paused a moment, and Cynthia interrupted:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I should have woken up if anyone had come through my room?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot if you were drugged, mademoiselle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrugged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Mais, oui!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remember\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he addressed us collectively again\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cthat through all the\r\ntumult and noise next door Mademoiselle Cynthia slept. That admitted of\r\ntwo possibilities. Either her sleep was feigned\xe2\x80\x94which I did not\r\nbelieve\xe2\x80\x94or her unconsciousness was indeed by artificial means.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith this latter idea in my mind, I examined all the coffee-cups most\r\ncarefully, remembering that it was Mrs. Cavendish who had brought\r\nMademoiselle Cynthia her coffee the night before. I took a sample from\r\neach cup, and had them analysed\xe2\x80\x94with no result. I had counted the cups\r\ncarefully, in the event of one having been removed. Six persons had\r\ntaken coffee, and six cups were duly found. I had to confess myself\r\nmistaken.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I discovered that I had been guilty of a very grave oversight.\r\nCoffee had been brought in for seven persons, not six, for Dr.\r\nBauerstein had been there that evening. This changed the face of the\r\nwhole affair, for there was now one cup missing. The servants noticed\r\nnothing, since Annie, the housemaid, who took in the coffee, brought in\r\nseven cups, not knowing that Mr. Inglethorp never drank it, whereas\r\nDorcas, who cleared them away the following morning, found six as\r\nusual\xe2\x80\x94or strictly speaking she found five, the sixth being the one\r\nfound broken in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was confident that the missing cup was that of Mademoiselle Cynthia.\r\nI had an additional reason for that belief in the fact that all the\r\ncups found contained sugar, which Mademoiselle Cynthia never took in\r\nher coffee. My attention was attracted by the story of Annie about some\r\n\xe2\x80\x98salt\xe2\x80\x99 on the tray of cocoa which she took every night to Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room. I accordingly secured a sample of that cocoa, and\r\nsent it to be analysed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut that had already been done by Dr. Bauerstein,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lawrence\r\nquickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot exactly. The analyst was asked by him to report whether strychnine\r\nwas, or was not, present. He did not have it tested, as I did, for a\r\nnarcotic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor a narcotic?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Here is the analyst\xe2\x80\x99s report. Mrs. Cavendish administered a safe,\r\nbut effectual, narcotic to both Mrs. Inglethorp and Mademoiselle\r\nCynthia. And it is possible that she had a _mauvais quart d\xe2\x80\x99heure_ in\r\nconsequence! Imagine her feelings when her mother-in-law is suddenly\r\ntaken ill and dies, and immediately after she hears the word \xe2\x80\x98Poison\xe2\x80\x99!\r\nShe has believed that the sleeping draught she administered was\r\nperfectly harmless, but there is no doubt that for one terrible moment\r\nshe must have feared that Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s death lay at her door. She\r\nis seized with panic, and under its influence she hurries downstairs,\r\nand quickly drops the coffee-cup and saucer used by Mademoiselle\r\nCynthia into a large brass vase, where it is discovered later by\r\nMonsieur Lawrence. The remains of the cocoa she dare not touch. Too\r\nmany eyes are upon her. Guess at her relief when strychnine is\r\nmentioned, and she discovers that after all the tragedy is not her\r\ndoing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are now able to account for the symptoms of strychnine poisoning\r\nbeing so long in making their appearance. A narcotic taken with\r\nstrychnine will delay the action of the poison for some hours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot paused. Mary looked up at him, the colour slowly rising in her\r\nface.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll you have said is quite true, Monsieur Poirot. It was the most\r\nawful hour of my life. I shall never forget it. But you are wonderful.\r\nI understand now\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat I meant when I told you that you could safely confess to Papa\r\nPoirot, eh? But you would not trust me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see everything now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lawrence. \xe2\x80\x9cThe drugged cocoa, taken on top\r\nof the poisoned coffee, amply accounts for the delay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly. But was the coffee poisoned, or was it not? We come to a\r\nlittle difficulty here, since Mrs. Inglethorp never drank it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d The cry of surprise was universal.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. You will remember my speaking of a stain on the carpet in Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s room? There were some peculiar points about that stain. It\r\nwas still damp, it exhaled a strong odour of coffee, and imbedded in\r\nthe nap of the carpet I found some little splinters of china. What had\r\nhappened was plain to me, for not two minutes before I had placed my\r\nlittle case on the table near the window, and the table, tilting up,\r\nhad deposited it upon the floor on precisely the identical spot. In\r\nexactly the same way, Mrs. Inglethorp had laid down her cup of coffee\r\non reaching her room the night before, and the treacherous table had\r\nplayed her the same trick.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat happened next is mere guess work on my part, but I should say\r\nthat Mrs. Inglethorp picked up the broken cup and placed it on the\r\ntable by the bed. Feeling in need of a stimulant of some kind, she\r\nheated up her cocoa, and drank it off then and there. Now we are faced\r\nwith a new problem. We know the cocoa contained no strychnine. The\r\ncoffee was never drunk. Yet the strychnine must have been administered\r\nbetween seven and nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock that evening. What third medium was\r\nthere\xe2\x80\x94a medium so suitable for disguising the taste of strychnine that\r\nit is extraordinary no one has thought of it?\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot looked round the\r\nroom, and then answered himself impressively. \xe2\x80\x9cHer medicine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that the murderer introduced the strychnine into her\r\ntonic?\xe2\x80\x9d I cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was no need to introduce it. It was already there\xe2\x80\x94in the\r\nmixture. The strychnine that killed Mrs. Inglethorp was the identical\r\nstrychnine prescribed by Dr. Wilkins. To make that clear to you, I will\r\nread you an extract from a book on dispensing which I found in the\r\nDispensary of the Red Cross Hospital at Tadminster:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The following prescription has become famous in text books:\r\n\r\n\r\n          Strychninae Sulph. . . . . . 1 gr.\r\n\r\n          Potass Bromide . . . . . . . 3vi\r\n\r\n          Aqua ad. . . . . . . . . . . . . 3viii\r\n\r\n          Fiat Mistura\r\n\r\n\r\n_This solution deposits in a few hours the greater part of the\r\nstrychnine salt as an insoluble bromide in transparent crystals. A lady\r\nin England lost her life by taking a similar mixture: the precipitated\r\nstrychnine collected at the bottom, and in taking the last dose she\r\nswallowed nearly all of it!_\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow there was, of course, no bromide in Dr. Wilkins\xe2\x80\x99 prescription, but\r\nyou will remember that I mentioned an empty box of bromide powders. One\r\nor two of those powders introduced into the full bottle of medicine\r\nwould effectually precipitate the strychnine, as the book describes,\r\nand cause it to be taken in the last dose. You will learn later that\r\nthe person who usually poured out Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s medicine was always\r\nextremely careful not to shake the bottle, but to leave the sediment at\r\nthe bottom of it undisturbed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThroughout the case, there have been evidences that the tragedy was\r\nintended to take place on Monday evening. On that day, Mrs.\r\nInglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s bell wire was neatly cut, and on Monday evening\r\nMademoiselle Cynthia was spending the night with friends, so that Mrs.\r\nInglethorp would have been quite alone in the right wing, completely\r\nshut off from help of any kind, and would have died, in all\r\nprobability, before medical aid could have been summoned. But in her\r\nhurry to be in time for the village entertainment Mrs. Inglethorp\r\nforgot to take her medicine, and the next day she lunched away from\r\nhome, so that the last\xe2\x80\x94and fatal\xe2\x80\x94dose was actually taken twenty-four\r\nhours later than had been anticipated by the murderer; and it is owing\r\nto that delay that the final proof\xe2\x80\x94the last link of the chain\xe2\x80\x94is now in\r\nmy hands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAmid breathless excitement, he held out three thin strips of paper.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA letter in the murderer\xe2\x80\x99s own hand-writing, _mes amis!_ Had it been a\r\nlittle clearer in its terms, it is possible that Mrs. Inglethorp,\r\nwarned in time, would have escaped. As it was, she realized her danger,\r\nbut not the manner of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn the deathly silence, Poirot pieced together the slips of paper and,\r\nclearing his throat, read:\r\n\r\nDearest Evelyn:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x98You will be anxious at hearing nothing. It is all right\xe2\x80\x94only it\r\n    will be to-night instead of last night. You understand. There\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\n    good time coming once the old woman is dead and out of the way. No\r\n    one can possibly bring home the crime to me. That idea of yours\r\n    about the bromides was a stroke of genius! But we must be very\r\n    circumspect. A false step\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere, my friends, the letter breaks off. Doubtless the writer was\r\ninterrupted; but there can be no question as to his identity. We all\r\nknow this hand-writing and\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA howl that was almost a scream broke the silence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou devil! How did you get it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA chair was overturned. Poirot skipped nimbly aside. A quick movement\r\non his part, and his assailant fell with a crash.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Messieurs, mesdames_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, with a flourish, \xe2\x80\x9clet me\r\nintroduce you to the murderer, Mr. Alfred Inglethorp!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII. POIROT EXPLAINS\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoirot, you old villain,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve half a mind to strangle you!\r\nWhat do you mean by deceiving me as you have done?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWe were sitting in the library. Several hectic days lay behind us. In\r\nthe room below, John and Mary were together once more, while Alfred\r\nInglethorp and Miss Howard were in custody. Now at last, I had Poirot\r\nto myself, and could relieve my still burning curiosity.\r\n\r\nPoirot did not answer me for a moment, but at last he said:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not deceive you, _mon ami_. At most, I permitted you to deceive\r\nyourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is difficult to explain. You see, my friend, you have a\r\nnature so honest, and a countenance so transparent, that\xe2\x80\x94_enfin_, to\r\nconceal your feelings is impossible! If I had told you my ideas, the\r\nvery first time you saw Mr. Alfred Inglethorp that astute gentleman\r\nwould have\xe2\x80\x94in your so expressive idiom\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98smelt a rat\xe2\x80\x99! And then,\r\n_bonjour_ to our chances of catching him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that I have more diplomacy than you give me credit for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy friend,\xe2\x80\x9d besought Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cI implore you, do not enrage yourself!\r\nYour help has been of the most invaluable. It is but the extremely\r\nbeautiful nature that you have, which made me pause.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d I grumbled, a little mollified. \xe2\x80\x9cI still think you might have\r\ngiven me a hint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I did, my friend. Several hints. You would not take them. Think\r\nnow, did I ever say to you that I believed John Cavendish guilty? Did I\r\nnot, on the contrary, tell you that he would almost certainly be\r\nacquitted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd did I not immediately afterwards speak of the difficulty of\r\nbringing the murderer to justice? Was it not plain to you that I was\r\nspeaking of two entirely different persons?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, \xe2\x80\x9cit was not plain to me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen again,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Poirot, \xe2\x80\x9cat the beginning, did I not repeat to\r\nyou several times that I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want Mr. Inglethorp arrested _now_?\r\nThat should have conveyed something to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say you suspected him as long ago as that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. To begin with, whoever else might benefit by Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndeath, her husband would benefit the most. There was no getting away\r\nfrom that. When I went up to Styles with you that first day, I had no\r\nidea as to how the crime had been committed, but from what I knew of\r\nMr. Inglethorp I fancied that it would be very hard to find anything to\r\nconnect him with it. When I arrived at the ch\xc3\xa2teau, I realized at once\r\nthat it was Mrs. Inglethorp who had burnt the will; and there, by the\r\nway, you cannot complain, my friend, for I tried my best to force on\r\nyou the significance of that bedroom fire in midsummer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, yes,\xe2\x80\x9d I said impatiently. \xe2\x80\x9cGo on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my friend, as I say, my views as to Mr. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s guilt were\r\nvery much shaken. There was, in fact, so much evidence against him that\r\nI was inclined to believe that he had not done it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you change your mind?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I found that the more efforts I made to clear him, the more\r\nefforts he made to get himself arrested. Then, when I discovered that\r\nInglethorp had nothing to do with Mrs. Raikes and that in fact it was\r\nJohn Cavendish who was interested in that quarter, I was quite sure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSimply this. If it had been Inglethorp who was carrying on an intrigue\r\nwith Mrs. Raikes, his silence was perfectly comprehensible. But, when I\r\ndiscovered that it was known all over the village that it was John who\r\nwas attracted by the farmer\xe2\x80\x99s pretty wife, his silence bore quite a\r\ndifferent interpretation. It was nonsense to pretend that he was afraid\r\nof the scandal, as no possible scandal could attach to him. This\r\nattitude of his gave me furiously to think, and I was slowly forced to\r\nthe conclusion that Alfred Inglethorp wanted to be arrested. _Eh bien!_\r\nfrom that moment, I was equally determined that he should not be\r\narrested.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a minute. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why he wished to be arrested?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause, _mon ami_, it is the law of your country that a man once\r\nacquitted can never be tried again for the same offence. Aha! but it\r\nwas clever\xe2\x80\x94his idea! Assuredly, he is a man of method. See here, he\r\nknew that in his position he was bound to be suspected, so he conceived\r\nthe exceedingly clever idea of preparing a lot of manufactured evidence\r\nagainst himself. He wished to be arrested. He would then produce his\r\nirreproachable alibi\xe2\x80\x94and, hey presto, he was safe for life!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I still don\xe2\x80\x99t see how he managed to prove his alibi, and yet go to\r\nthe chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot stared at me in surprise.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it possible? My poor friend! You have not yet realized that it was\r\nMiss Howard who went to the chemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMiss Howard?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, certainly. Who else? It was most easy for her. She is of a good\r\nheight, her voice is deep and manly; moreover, remember, she and\r\nInglethorp are cousins, and there is a distinct resemblance between\r\nthem, especially in their gait and bearing. It was simplicity itself.\r\nThey are a clever pair!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am still a little fogged as to how exactly the bromide business was\r\ndone,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Bon!_ I will reconstruct for you as far as possible. I am inclined to\r\nthink that Miss Howard was the master mind in that affair. You remember\r\nher once mentioning that her father was a doctor? Possibly she\r\ndispensed his medicines for him, or she may have taken the idea from\r\none of the many books lying about when Mademoiselle Cynthia was\r\nstudying for her exam. Anyway, she was familiar with the fact that the\r\naddition of a bromide to a mixture containing strychnine would cause\r\nthe precipitation of the latter. Probably the idea came to her quite\r\nsuddenly. Mrs. Inglethorp had a box of bromide powders, which she\r\noccasionally took at night. What could be easier than quietly to\r\ndissolve one or more of those powders in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s large sized\r\nbottle of medicine when it came from Coot\xe2\x80\x99s? The risk is practically\r\nnil. The tragedy will not take place until nearly a fortnight later. If\r\nanyone has seen either of them touching the medicine, they will have\r\nforgotten it by that time. Miss Howard will have engineered her\r\nquarrel, and departed from the house. The lapse of time, and her\r\nabsence, will defeat all suspicion. Yes, it was a clever idea! If they\r\nhad left it alone, it is possible the crime might never have been\r\nbrought home to them. But they were not satisfied. They tried to be too\r\nclever\xe2\x80\x94and that was their undoing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoirot puffed at his tiny cigarette, his eyes fixed on the ceiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey arranged a plan to throw suspicion on John Cavendish, by buying\r\nstrychnine at the village chemist\xe2\x80\x99s, and signing the register in his\r\nhand-writing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn Monday Mrs. Inglethorp will take the last dose of her medicine. On\r\nMonday, therefore, at six o\xe2\x80\x99clock, Alfred Inglethorp arranges to be\r\nseen by a number of people at a spot far removed from the village. Miss\r\nHoward has previously made up a cock and bull story about him and Mrs.\r\nRaikes to account for his holding his tongue afterwards. At six\r\no\xe2\x80\x99clock, Miss Howard, disguised as Alfred Inglethorp, enters the\r\nchemist\xe2\x80\x99s shop, with her story about a dog, obtains the strychnine, and\r\nwrites the name of Alfred Inglethorp in John\xe2\x80\x99s handwriting, which she\r\nhad previously studied carefully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, as it will never do if John, too, can prove an alibi, she writes\r\nhim an anonymous note\xe2\x80\x94still copying his hand-writing\xe2\x80\x94which takes him to\r\na remote spot where it is exceedingly unlikely that anyone will see\r\nhim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo far, all goes well. Miss Howard goes back to Middlingham. Alfred\r\nInglethorp returns to Styles. There is nothing that can compromise him\r\nin any way, since it is Miss Howard who has the strychnine, which,\r\nafter all, is only wanted as a blind to throw suspicion on John\r\nCavendish.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut now a hitch occurs. Mrs. Inglethorp does not take her medicine\r\nthat night. The broken bell, Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s absence\xe2\x80\x94arranged by Inglethorp\r\nthrough his wife\xe2\x80\x94all these are wasted. And then\xe2\x80\x94he makes his slip.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMrs. Inglethorp is out, and he sits down to write to his accomplice,\r\nwho, he fears, may be in a panic at the non-success of their plan. It\r\nis probable that Mrs. Inglethorp returned earlier than he expected.\r\nCaught in the act, and somewhat flurried he hastily shuts and locks his\r\ndesk. He fears that if he remains in the room he may have to open it\r\nagain, and that Mrs. Inglethorp might catch sight of the letter before\r\nhe could snatch it up. So he goes out and walks in the woods, little\r\ndreaming that Mrs. Inglethorp will open his desk, and discover the\r\nincriminating document.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this, as we know, is what happened. Mrs. Inglethorp reads it, and\r\nbecomes aware of the perfidy of her husband and Evelyn Howard, though,\r\nunfortunately, the sentence about the bromides conveys no warning to\r\nher mind. She knows that she is in danger\xe2\x80\x94but is ignorant of where the\r\ndanger lies. She decides to say nothing to her husband, but sits down\r\nand writes to her solicitor, asking him to come on the morrow, and she\r\nalso determines to destroy immediately the will which she has just\r\nmade. She keeps the fatal letter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was to discover that letter, then, that her husband forced the lock\r\nof the despatch-case?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and from the enormous risk he ran we can see how fully he\r\nrealized its importance. That letter excepted, there was absolutely\r\nnothing to connect him with the crime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s only one thing I can\xe2\x80\x99t make out, why didn\xe2\x80\x99t he destroy it at\r\nonce when he got hold of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he did not dare take the biggest risk of all\xe2\x80\x94that of keeping\r\nit on his own person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook at it from his point of view. I have discovered that there were\r\nonly five short minutes in which he could have taken it\xe2\x80\x94the five\r\nminutes immediately before our own arrival on the scene, for before\r\nthat time Annie was brushing the stairs, and would have seen anyone who\r\npassed going to the right wing. Figure to yourself the scene! He enters\r\nthe room, unlocking the door by means of one of the other doorkeys\xe2\x80\x94they\r\nwere all much alike. He hurries to the despatch-case\xe2\x80\x94it is locked, and\r\nthe keys are nowhere to be seen. That is a terrible blow to him, for it\r\nmeans that his presence in the room cannot be concealed as he had\r\nhoped. But he sees clearly that everything must be risked for the sake\r\nof that damning piece of evidence. Quickly, he forces the lock with a\r\npenknife, and turns over the papers until he finds what he is looking\r\nfor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut now a fresh dilemma arises: he dare not keep that piece of paper\r\non him. He may be seen leaving the room\xe2\x80\x94he may be searched. If the\r\npaper is found on him, it is certain doom. Probably, at this minute,\r\ntoo, he hears the sounds below of Mr. Wells and John leaving the\r\nboudoir. He must act quickly. Where can he hide this terrible slip of\r\npaper? The contents of the waste-paper-basket are kept and in any case,\r\nare sure to be examined. There are no means of destroying it; and he\r\ndare not keep it. He looks round, and he sees\xe2\x80\x94what do you think, _mon\r\nami?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI shook my head.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a moment, he has torn the letter into long thin strips, and rolling\r\nthem up into spills he thrusts them hurriedly in amongst the other\r\nspills in the vase on the mantle-piece.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI uttered an exclamation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo one would think of looking there,\xe2\x80\x9d Poirot continued. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd he will\r\nbe able, at his leisure, to come back and destroy this solitary piece\r\nof evidence against him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, all the time, it was in the spill vase in Mrs. Inglethorp\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbedroom, under our very noses?\xe2\x80\x9d I cried.\r\n\r\nPoirot nodded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my friend. That is where I discovered my \xe2\x80\x98last link,\xe2\x80\x99 and I owe\r\nthat very fortunate discovery to you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Do you remember telling me that my hand shook as I was\r\nstraightening the ornaments on the mantelpiece?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t see\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, but I saw. Do you know, my friend, I remembered that earlier in\r\nthe morning, when we had been there together, I had straightened all\r\nthe objects on the mantelpiece. And, if they were already straightened,\r\nthere would be no need to straighten them again, unless, in the\r\nmeantime, someone else had touched them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me,\xe2\x80\x9d I murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cso that is the explanation of your\r\nextraordinary behaviour. You rushed down to Styles, and found it still\r\nthere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and it was a race for time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I still can\xe2\x80\x99t understand why Inglethorp was such a fool as to\r\nleave it there when he had plenty of opportunity to destroy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but he had no opportunity. _I_ saw to that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. Do you remember reproving me for taking the household into my\r\nconfidence on the subject?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, my friend, I saw there was just one chance. I was not sure then\r\nif Inglethorp was the criminal or not, but if he was I reasoned that he\r\nwould not have the paper on him, but would have hidden it somewhere,\r\nand by enlisting the sympathy of the household I could effectually\r\nprevent his destroying it. He was already under suspicion, and by\r\nmaking the matter public I secured the services of about ten amateur\r\ndetectives, who would be watching him unceasingly, and being himself\r\naware of their watchfulness he would not dare seek further to destroy\r\nthe document. He was therefore forced to depart from the house, leaving\r\nit in the spill vase.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut surely Miss Howard had ample opportunities of aiding him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but Miss Howard did not know of the paper\xe2\x80\x99s existence. In\r\naccordance with their prearranged plan, she never spoke to Alfred\r\nInglethorp. They were supposed to be deadly enemies, and until John\r\nCavendish was safely convicted they neither of them dared risk a\r\nmeeting. Of course I had a watch kept on Mr. Inglethorp, hoping that\r\nsooner or later he would lead me to the hiding-place. But he was too\r\nclever to take any chances. The paper was safe where it was; since no\r\none had thought of looking there in the first week, it was not likely\r\nthey would do so afterwards. But for your lucky remark, we might never\r\nhave been able to bring him to justice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI understand that now; but when did you first begin to suspect Miss\r\nHoward?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I discovered that she had told a lie at the inquest about the\r\nletter she had received from Mrs. Inglethorp.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, what was there to lie about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou saw that letter? Do you recall its general appearance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94more or less.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will recollect, then, that Mrs. Inglethorp wrote a very\r\ndistinctive hand, and left large clear spaces between her words. But if\r\nyou look at the date at the top of the letter you will notice that\r\n\xe2\x80\x98July 17th\xe2\x80\x99 is quite different in this respect. Do you see what I\r\nmean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I confessed, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not see that that letter was not written on the 17th, but on\r\nthe 7th\xe2\x80\x94the day after Miss Howard\xe2\x80\x99s departure? The \xe2\x80\x981\xe2\x80\x99 was written in\r\nbefore the \xe2\x80\x987\xe2\x80\x99 to turn it into the \xe2\x80\x9817th\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is exactly what I asked myself. Why does Miss Howard suppress the\r\nletter written on the 17th, and produce this faked one instead? Because\r\nshe did not wish to show the letter of the 17th. Why, again? And at\r\nonce a suspicion dawned in my mind. You will remember my saying that it\r\nwas wise to beware of people who were not telling you the truth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet,\xe2\x80\x9d I cried indignantly, \xe2\x80\x9cafter that, you gave me two reasons\r\nwhy Miss Howard could not have committed the crime!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd very good reasons too,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cFor a long time they were\r\na stumbling-block to me until I remembered a very significant fact:\r\nthat she and Alfred Inglethorp were cousins. She could not have\r\ncommitted the crime single-handed, but the reasons against that did not\r\ndebar her from being an accomplice. And, then, there was that rather\r\nover-vehement hatred of hers! It concealed a very opposite emotion.\r\nThere was, undoubtedly, a tie of passion between them long before he\r\ncame to Styles. They had already arranged their infamous plot\xe2\x80\x94that he\r\nshould marry this rich, but rather foolish old lady, induce her to make\r\na will leaving her money to him, and then gain their ends by a very\r\ncleverly conceived crime. If all had gone as they planned, they would\r\nprobably have left England, and lived together on their poor victim\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmoney.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are a very astute and unscrupulous pair. While suspicion was to\r\nbe directed against him, she would be making quiet preparations for a\r\nvery different _d\xc3\xa9nouement_. She arrives from Middlingham with all the\r\ncompromising items in her possession. No suspicion attaches to her. No\r\nnotice is paid to her coming and going in the house. She hides the\r\nstrychnine and glasses in John\xe2\x80\x99s room. She puts the beard in the attic.\r\nShe will see to it that sooner or later they are duly discovered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t quite see why they tried to fix the blame on John,\xe2\x80\x9d I\r\nremarked. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been much easier for them to bring the crime\r\nhome to Lawrence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that was mere chance. All the evidence against him arose out\r\nof pure accident. It must, in fact, have been distinctly annoying to\r\nthe pair of schemers.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis manner was unfortunate,\xe2\x80\x9d I observed thoughtfully.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. You realize, of course, what was at the back of that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did not understand that he believed Mademoiselle Cynthia guilty of\r\nthe crime?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d I exclaimed, astonished. \xe2\x80\x9cImpossible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all. I myself nearly had the same idea. It was in my mind when\r\nI asked Mr. Wells that first question about the will. Then there were\r\nthe bromide powders which she had made up, and her clever male\r\nimpersonations, as Dorcas recounted them to us. There was really more\r\nevidence against her than anyone else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are joking, Poirot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. Shall I tell you what made Monsieur Lawrence turn so pale when he\r\nfirst entered his mother\xe2\x80\x99s room on the fatal night? It was because,\r\nwhilst his mother lay there, obviously poisoned, he saw, over your\r\nshoulder, that the door into Mademoiselle Cynthia\xe2\x80\x99s room was unbolted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut he declared that he saw it bolted!\xe2\x80\x9d I cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot dryly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd that was just what confirmed my\r\nsuspicion that it was not. He was shielding Mademoiselle Cynthia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why should he shield her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause he is in love with her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, Poirot, you are quite wrong! I happen to know for a fact that,\r\nfar from being in love with her, he positively dislikes her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho told you that, _mon ami?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCynthia herself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_La pauvre petite!_ And she was concerned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe said that she did not mind at all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen she certainly did mind very much,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are\r\nlike that\xe2\x80\x94_les femmes!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you say about Lawrence is a great surprise to me,\xe2\x80\x9d I said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut why? It was most obvious. Did not Monsieur Lawrence make the sour\r\nface every time Mademoiselle Cynthia spoke and laughed with his\r\nbrother? He had taken it into his long head that Mademoiselle Cynthia\r\nwas in love with Monsieur John. When he entered his mother\xe2\x80\x99s room, and\r\nsaw her obviously poisoned, he jumped to the conclusion that\r\nMademoiselle Cynthia knew something about the matter. He was nearly\r\ndriven desperate. First he crushed the coffee-cup to powder under his\r\nfeet, remembering that _she_ had gone up with his mother the night\r\nbefore, and he determined that there should be no chance of testing its\r\ncontents. Thenceforward, he strenuously, and quite uselessly, upheld\r\nthe theory of \xe2\x80\x98Death from natural causes\xe2\x80\x99.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what about the \xe2\x80\x98extra coffee-cup\xe2\x80\x99?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was fairly certain that it was Mrs. Cavendish who had hidden it, but\r\nI had to make sure. Monsieur Lawrence did not know at all what I meant;\r\nbut, on reflection, he came to the conclusion that if he could find an\r\nextra coffee-cup anywhere his lady love would be cleared of suspicion.\r\nAnd he was perfectly right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne thing more. What did Mrs. Inglethorp mean by her dying words?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey were, of course, an accusation against her husband.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear me, Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I said with a sigh, \xe2\x80\x9cI think you have explained\r\neverything. I am glad it has all ended so happily. Even John and his\r\nwife are reconciled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean\xe2\x80\x94thanks to you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear friend, do you not realize that it was simply and solely the\r\ntrial which has brought them together again? That John Cavendish still\r\nloved his wife, I was convinced. Also, that she was equally in love\r\nwith him. But they had drifted very far apart. It all arose from a\r\nmisunderstanding. She married him without love. He knew it. He is a\r\nsensitive man in his way, he would not force himself upon her if she\r\ndid not want him. And, as he withdrew, her love awoke. But they are\r\nboth unusually proud, and their pride held them inexorably apart. He\r\ndrifted into an entanglement with Mrs. Raikes, and she deliberately\r\ncultivated the friendship of Dr. Bauerstein. Do you remember the day of\r\nJohn Cavendish\xe2\x80\x99s arrest, when you found me deliberating over a big\r\ndecision?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I quite understood your distress.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPardon me, _mon ami_, but you did not understand it in the least. I\r\nwas trying to decide whether or not I would clear John Cavendish at\r\nonce. I could have cleared him\xe2\x80\x94though it might have meant a failure to\r\nconvict the real criminals. They were entirely in the dark as to my\r\nreal attitude up to the very last moment\xe2\x80\x94which partly accounts for my\r\nsuccess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean that you could have saved John Cavendish from being\r\nbrought to trial?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my friend. But I eventually decided in favour of \xe2\x80\x98a woman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhappiness\xe2\x80\x99. Nothing but the great danger through which they have passed\r\ncould have brought these two proud souls together again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nI looked at Poirot in silent amazement. The colossal cheek of the\r\nlittle man! Who on earth but Poirot would have thought of a trial for\r\nmurder as a restorer of conjugal happiness!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI perceive your thoughts, _mon ami_,\xe2\x80\x9d said Poirot, smiling at me. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\r\none but Hercule Poirot would have attempted such a thing! And you are\r\nwrong in condemning it. The happiness of one man and one woman is the\r\ngreatest thing in all the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis words took me back to earlier events. I remembered Mary as she lay\r\nwhite and exhausted on the sofa, listening, listening. There had come\r\nthe sound of the bell below. She had started up. Poirot had opened the\r\ndoor, and meeting her agonized eyes had nodded gently. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, madame,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have brought him back to you.\xe2\x80\x9d He had stood aside, and as I\r\nwent out I had seen the look in Mary\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, as John Cavendish had\r\ncaught his wife in his arms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps you are right, Poirot,\xe2\x80\x9d I said gently. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is the\r\ngreatest thing in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSuddenly, there was a tap at the door, and Cynthia peeped in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94I only\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in,\xe2\x80\x9d I said, springing up.\r\n\r\nShe came in, but did not sit down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x94only wanted to tell you something\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCynthia fidgeted with a little tassel for some moments, then, suddenly\r\nexclaiming: \xe2\x80\x9cYou dears!\xe2\x80\x9d kissed first me and then Poirot, and rushed\r\nout of the room again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth does this mean?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked, surprised.\r\n\r\nIt was very nice to be kissed by Cynthia, but the publicity of the\r\nsalute rather impaired the pleasure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt means that she has discovered Monsieur Lawrence does not dislike\r\nher as much as she thought,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Poirot philosophically.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere he is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLawrence at that moment passed the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh! Monsieur Lawrence,\xe2\x80\x9d called Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cWe must congratulate you, is\r\nit not so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLawrence blushed, and then smiled awkwardly. A man in love is a sorry\r\nspectacle. Now Cynthia had looked charming.\r\n\r\nI sighed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, _mon ami?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d I said sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are two delightful women!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd neither of them is for you?\xe2\x80\x9d finished Poirot. \xe2\x80\x9cNever mind. Console\r\nyourself, my friend. We may hunt together again, who knows? And then\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE END\r\n\r\n\r\nEnd of Project Gutenberg\'s The Mysterious Affair at Styles, by Agatha Christie\r\n\r\n*** END OF THIS PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE MYSTERIOUS AFFAIR AT STYLES ***\r\n\r\n***** This file should be named 863-0.txt or 863-0.zip *****\r\nThis and all associated files of various formats will be found in:\r\n        http://www.gutenberg.org/8/6/863/\r\n\r\nProduced by Charles Keller\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions\r\nwill be renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from public domain print editions means that no\r\none owns a United States copyright in these works, so the Foundation\r\n(and you!) can copy and distribute it in the United States without\r\npermission and without paying copyright royalties.
